The Four Positions on Body Divisibility
Razi classifies four positions on body divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four classical positions on the divisibility of bodies and how they frame the kalam debate on the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Bodies can be divided either finitely or infinitely—four positions result from combining actual/potential and finite/infinite.

Before Razi presents his proof for the indivisible atom, he first maps the theological landscape. He identifies four possible views on how bodies can be divided, ranging from the classical mutakallimun position of finite indivisible parts to the philosophers' view of potential infinite divisibility. This classification sets the stage for the arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how Razi builds the four categories from two binary distinctions: actual vs. potential division and finite vs. infinite.
The four positions are explicitly enumerated; note each one's proponents.
2
Identify each position
For each of the four positions, identify the key claim and the group that holds it. Pay special attention to the nuance in the third position (philosophers) regarding potential infinite division.
The philosophers' view is subtle: any actual division is finite, but division can continue indefinitely.
3
Reflect on the analogy with God's power
Razi uses an analogy about God's power: God can create endlessly, but each creation is finite. This illustrates the philosophers' concept of potential infinity.
This analogy helps distinguish potential infinity from actual infinity.
4
Consider the purpose of classification
Ask yourself: why does Razi start with this classification? How does it set up the arguments for the indivisible atom that follow?
The classification defines the options; later proofs support the first position.

المسألة السابعة والعشرون
في إثبات الجوهر الفرد
اعلم أنا قبل الخوض في مسألة المعاد، نفتقر إلى إثبات أصلين : أحدهما : معرفة النفس، ومعرفة النفس محتاجة إلى معرفة الجوهر الفرد. والأصل الثاني : إثبات الخلاء. ونحن قبل الخوض في مسألة المعاد، نذكر هذه المسائل الثلاث :
أما مسألة الجوهر الفرد :
فنقول : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة قابلة للانقسامات، وهذه الانقسامات الممكنة إما أن تكون موجودة بالفعل، أو لا تكون وعلى كلا التقديرين فهي إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية . فحصل من هذا التقسيم أقسام أربعة . لا مزيد عليها :
الأول : قول من يقول : الجسم المحسوس مركب من أجزاء متناهية وكل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه وهذا قول أكثر المتكلمين.
الثاني: قول من يقول : الجسم المحسوس مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل. وهذا هو القول المنسوب إلى (النظام).
والثالث : قول من يقول : هذا الجسم المحسوس واحد في نفسه، كما أنه واحد في الحس، إلا أنه قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية لا أنه يقبل انقسامات بمعنى غير متناهية دفعة واحدة بل بمعنى أن الجسم لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد إلا ويقبل بعد ذلك الانقسام. وإن كل ما يخرج من الانقسامات إلى الفعل أبدًا فهو متناه. كما أنا نقول: إنه تعالى قادر على ما لانهاية له لا بمعنى أنه يمكن أن يخلق أشياء لا نهاية لها . فإن ذلك محال. بل بمعنى أنه تعالى لا يصل في الخلق والإيجاد إلى حد، إلا ويمكنه بعد يوجد شيئا آخر وإن كل ما يخرج إلى الوجود فإنه متناه. وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة.
الرابع : قول من يقول : الجسم البسيط واحد في نفسه، لكنه قابل لانقسامات متناهية.
فهذا هو تفصيل المذاهب في هذا الباب.
ويدل على أن الجسم مركب من أجزاء متناهية كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة بوجه من الوجوه :دلائل
الحجة الأولى : إن كانت الحركة مركبة من أمور متتالية كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة الزمانية بوجه من الوجوه كان الجسم مركبا من أمور كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة الزمانية بوجه من الوجوه والمقدم حق فالتالي مثله.
وإنما قلنا : إن الحركة مركبة من أمور متتالية كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة الزمانية، وذلك لأن الجسم يشاهد أنه تحرك بعد أن لم يكن متحركا. وهذه الحركة صفة زائدة على ذات الجسم. ثم نقول : هذه الصفة إما أن لا يحصل شيء منها في الحال، أو يحصل. فإن لم يحصل شيء منها في الحال امتنع أن يصير ماضيا أو مستقبلا. وذلك لأن الماضي هو الذي كان حاضرا في وقت من الأوقات. وقد زال الآن. والمستقبل هو الذي يتوقع أن يحضر في زمان من الأزمنة الآتية، وبعد لم يحضر. فلو امتنع أن يكون له حضور بوجه من الوجوه، لم يكن ماضيا، ولا مستقبلا ولا حاضرا. وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن موجودا البتة. فالحركة لا وجود لها البتة. وهذا خلف. فثبت أنه لا بد أن يحضر من الحركة شيء في الحال. وذلك الحاضر في الحال إما أن يقبل القسمة بحسب الزمان أو لا يقبل فإن قبلها افترض فيه جزءان أحدهما قبل الآخر، لأن القسمة الزمانية هكذا تكون وحين ما كان النصف الأول موجودًا ، لم يكن النصف الثاني حاضرا، وحينما جاء النصف الثاني، صار النصف الأول فانيا، وحينئذ لا يكون الحاضر الموجود حاضرًا موجودا ، بل الحاضر الموجود منه نصفه . ثم نعيد التقسيم الأول في ذلك النصف.
والحاصل أن كل ما كان منقسما بحسب القسمة الزمانية، لم يكن مجموعه موجودًا، وما كان مجموعا حاضرًا وجب أن لا يكون منقسما بحسب القسمة الزمانية
فثبت أن الحاضر من الحركة والحاصل منها في الحال غير قابل للقسمة الزمانية.
The Twenty-Seventh Question: On Proving the Indivisible Atom. Know that before delving into the question of resurrection, we need to establish two principles: one: knowledge of the soul, and knowledge of the soul requires knowledge of the indivisible atom. The second principle: establishing the void. And before delving into the question of resurrection, we mention these three questions. As for the question of the indivisible atom: We say: There is no doubt that these sensible bodies are susceptible to divisions, and these possible divisions are either actually existent or not, and in both cases they are either finite or infinite. So from this division there result four categories, no more: First: the statement of one who says: The sensible body is composed of finite parts, each one of which is indivisible in any respect; this is the statement of most mutakallimun. Second: the statement of one who says: The sensible body is composed of actually infinite parts; this is the statement attributed to al-Nazzam. Third: the statement of one who says: This sensible body is one in itself, just as it is one in sensation, but it is susceptible to infinite divisions—not that it admits infinite divisions all at once, but in the sense that the body does not reach a limit in smallness except that it thereafter admits division. And whatever of the divisions comes into actuality is always finite. Just as we say: He, exalted, is powerful over what has no end, not in the sense that He can create things that have no end—for that is impossible—but in the sense that He, exalted, does not reach a limit in creation and bringing into existence except that He can thereafter bring into existence another thing, and whatever comes into existence is finite. This is the doctrine of the majority of the philosophers. Fourth: the statement of one who says: The simple body is one in itself, but it is susceptible to finite divisions. This is the detailing of the doctrines in this chapter. And it is indicated that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which is indivisible in any respect, by proofs. The first proof: If motion is composed of successive things each one of which is temporally indivisible in any respect, then the body is composed of things each one of which is temporally indivisible in any respect. The premise is true, so the consequent is like it. And we say that motion is composed of successive things each one of which is temporally indivisible, because the body is observed to move after it was not moving. This motion is an attribute superadded to the essence of the body. Then we say: This attribute either nothing of it occurs in the present moment, or it does occur. If nothing of it occurs in the present moment, it is impossible for it to become past or future. That is because the past is that which was present at a time and has now ceased. The future is that which is expected to become present at a time in the future and has not yet become present. If it is impossible for it to have presence in any respect, it is neither past, nor future, nor present. Whatever is like that is not existent at all. So motion would have no existence at all. This is a contradiction. So it is established that necessarily something of motion occurs in the present moment. That which is present in the present moment either is divisible with respect to time or not. If it is divisible, then two parts are assumed in it, one before the other, because temporal division is like that. When the first half was existent, the second half was not present; when the second half came, the first half perished; then that which is present and existent is not present and existent, rather the present and existent part is half of it. Then we repeat the first division concerning that half. The upshot is that whatever is divided according to temporal division, its totality is not existent; and whatever is present in its totality must not be divided according to temporal division. So it is established that the present part of motion and what is obtained from it in the present moment is temporally indivisible.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins a new discussion: the twenty-seventh question, which is proving the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Razi states that before discussing the resurrection (ma'ad), we need to establish two principles: knowledge of the soul, which requires knowledge of the indivisible atom, and establishing the void. He then immediately starts with the question of the indivisible atom. First, he classifies the positions on the divisibility of bodies into four categories based on whether the possible divisions are actual or potential and finite or infinite. The four positions: (1) The body is composed of finite, indivisible parts – this is the view of most mutakallimun. (2) The body is composed of actually infinite parts – attributed to al-Nazzam. (3) The body is one in itself but potentially divisible without end, though any actual division is finite – this is the view of the majority of philosophers, illustrated by an analogy with God's power: He can create endlessly but each created thing is finite. (4) The body is one but divisible only finitely. After this classification, Razi begins the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) for the position that the body is composed of finite, indivisible parts. The proof is a conditional: if motion is composed of successive temporally indivisible units, then the body (which undergoes motion) is composed of such units. He then argues that motion indeed must be composed of such units. He reasons: motion is an attribute that comes to be after not being. It must exist in the present moment; otherwise it could never be past or future. That present part of motion, if divisible in time, would imply that only a part of it is present, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, the present part of motion must be temporally indivisible. This establishes the premise that motion is composed of temporally indivisible units, which then implies that the body is composed of indivisible parts. The argument is carefully structured to avoid circularity and to refute the idea of infinite divisibility by showing that temporal division leads to the non-existence of the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All four positions agree that bodies are actually divided into parts.
Correction: Only the first two positions posit actual parts; the others treat division as merely potential.

Checkpoint
Which position holds that the body is composed of actually infinite parts?
A) Position 1 (Mutakallimun)
B) Position 2 (Al-Nazzam)
C) Position 3 (Philosophers)
D) Position 4

Key Takeaway
The kalam debate on the atom is structured by a four-way classification based on whether divisions are actual or potential and finite or infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun insisted on a finite number of parts to avoid infinite regress. Reflect: Do you ever allow your reasoning about life's problems to go into an endless chain of 'what ifs' instead of deciding on a foundational truth? What foundational truth in your belief about Allah stops that regress?
?
The philosophers saw the body as one in itself but with endless potential for division. In your spiritual life, do you treat your own growth as having an 'actual' limit or as an endless potential that only becomes real step by step? Are you using the potential or letting it remain just a concept?
?
Razi begins by linking the atom to the knowledge of the soul and resurrection. Ask yourself: Do you treat the details of Islamic theology merely as intellectual puzzles, or do you see them as necessary foundations for beliefs that will transform your life—like the reality of the soul and the Hereafter? What would change if you lived as if every theological detail had that weight?

The First Proof: Motion Requires Indivisible Temporal Units
Razi presents the first proof for the indivisible atom using motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that the body is composed of indivisible parts, based on the nature of motion and time.

Core Idea
Motion cannot exist unless its present part is temporally indivisible, implying the body is composed of indivisible units.

After mapping the positions, Razi presents the first of several proofs for the mutakallimun position. This proof is a conditional argument linking the temporal indivisibility of motion to the indivisibility of the body. It proceeds by analyzing the present moment of motion and showing that it must be temporally atomic.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof as a whole
Read the Arabic passage and translation once. Notice that the proof is structured as a conditional: if motion is composed of temporally indivisible units, then body is composed of temporally indivisible units. Then Razi proves the antecedent.
The conclusion about the body depends on the analysis of motion.
2
Trace the conditional logic
Identify the two parts: (1) the conditional statement, (2) the proof that the antecedent is true. The antecedent is that motion consists of successive temporally indivisible parts.
Razi uses the fact that motion is an attribute that comes to be after not being.
3
Examine the sub-argument on presence
Razi argues that motion must have a present part; otherwise it could not be past or future. Then he shows that this present part cannot be temporally divisible, because if it were, only a fraction would be present, leading to an infinite regress.
Key terms: present, past, future, temporal division.
4
Conclude the proof
Since motion's present part is temporally indivisible, motion is composed of such units. Therefore, by the conditional, the body (which moves) is composed of temporally indivisible units, which are the indivisible atoms.
This proof supports the mutakallimun position (Position 1).

فثبت أن الحاضر من الحركة والحاصل منها في الحال غير قابل للقسمة الزمانية.
The Twenty-Seventh Question: On Proving the Indivisible Atom. Know that before delving into the question of resurrection, we need to establish two principles: one: knowledge of the soul, and knowledge of the soul requires knowledge of the indivisible atom. The second principle: establishing the void. And before delving into the question of resurrection, we mention these three questions. As for the question of the indivisible atom: We say: There is no doubt that these sensible bodies are susceptible to divisions, and these possible divisions are either actually existent or not, and in both cases they are either finite or infinite. So from this division there result four categories, no more: First: the statement of one who says: The sensible body is composed of finite parts, each one of which is indivisible in any respect; this is the statement of most mutakallimun. Second: the statement of one who says: The sensible body is composed of actually infinite parts; this is the statement attributed to al-Nazzam. Third: the statement of one who says: This sensible body is one in itself, just as it is one in sensation, but it is susceptible to infinite divisions—not that it admits infinite divisions all at once, but in the sense that the body does not reach a limit in smallness except that it thereafter admits division. And whatever of the divisions comes into actuality is always finite. Just as we say: He, exalted, is powerful over what has no end, not in the sense that He can create things that have no end—for that is impossible—but in the sense that He, exalted, does not reach a limit in creation and bringing into existence except that He can thereafter bring into existence another thing, and whatever comes into existence is finite. This is the doctrine of the majority of the philosophers. Fourth: the statement of one who says: The simple body is one in itself, but it is susceptible to finite divisions. This is the detailing of the doctrines in this chapter. And it is indicated that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which is indivisible in any respect, by proofs. The first proof: If motion is composed of successive things each one of which is temporally indivisible in any respect, then the body is composed of things each one of which is temporally indivisible in any respect. The premise is true, so the consequent is like it. And we say that motion is composed of successive things each one of which is temporally indivisible, because the body is observed to move after it was not moving. This motion is an attribute superadded to the essence of the body. Then we say: This attribute either nothing of it occurs in the present moment, or it does occur. If nothing of it occurs in the present moment, it is impossible for it to become past or future. That is because the past is that which was present at a time and has now ceased. The future is that which is expected to become present at a time in the future and has not yet become present. If it is impossible for it to have presence in any respect, it is neither past, nor future, nor present. Whatever is like that is not existent at all. So motion would have no existence at all. This is a contradiction. So it is established that necessarily something of motion occurs in the present moment. That which is present in the present moment either is divisible with respect to time or not. If it is divisible, then two parts are assumed in it, one before the other, because temporal division is like that. When the first half was existent, the second half was not present; when the second half came, the first half perished; then that which is present and existent is not present and existent, rather the present and existent part is half of it. Then we repeat the first division concerning that half. The upshot is that whatever is divided according to temporal division, its totality is not existent; and whatever is present in its totality must not be divided according to temporal division. So it is established that the present part of motion and what is obtained from it in the present moment is temporally indivisible.

Commentary
This passage begins a new discussion: the twenty-seventh question, which is proving the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Razi states that before discussing the resurrection (ma'ad), we need to establish two principles: knowledge of the soul, which requires knowledge of the indivisible atom, and establishing the void. He then immediately starts with the question of the indivisible atom. First, he classifies the positions on the divisibility of bodies into four categories based on whether the possible divisions are actual or potential and finite or infinite. The four positions: (1) The body is composed of finite, indivisible parts – this is the view of most mutakallimun. (2) The body is composed of actually infinite parts – attributed to al-Nazzam. (3) The body is one in itself but potentially divisible without end, though any actual division is finite – this is the view of the majority of philosophers, illustrated by an analogy with God's power: He can create endlessly but each created thing is finite. (4) The body is one but divisible only finitely. After this classification, Razi begins the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) for the position that the body is composed of finite, indivisible parts. The proof is a conditional: if motion is composed of successive temporally indivisible units, then the body (which undergoes motion) is composed of such units. He then argues that motion indeed must be composed of such units. He reasons: motion is an attribute that comes to be after not being. It must exist in the present moment; otherwise it could never be past or future. That present part of motion, if divisible in time, would imply that only a part of it is present, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, the present part of motion must be temporally indivisible. This establishes the premise that motion is composed of temporally indivisible units, which then implies that the body is composed of indivisible parts. The argument is carefully structured to avoid circularity and to refute the idea of infinite divisibility by showing that temporal division leads to the non-existence of the whole.

Key Terms
حركة
زمان
حاضر
ماضي
مستقبل
جزء لا يتجزأ
انقسام زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that time itself is atomistic.
Correction: Razi does not assume time is atomic; he argues that the present part of motion must be indivisible, which then implies the body's parts are indivisible.

Checkpoint
What is the key conclusion about the present part of motion in the first proof?
A) It is divisible into infinite parts.
B) It is temporally indivisible.
C) It does not exist.
D) It is the entire motion.

Key Takeaway
The indivisibility of the body is deduced from the indivisibility of motion's present part, using the principle that what is present must be whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your actions are composed of indivisible present moments. If the present part of your motion (your deeds) is not fully present, then your repentance, your prayer, your good intentions may be fragmented and incomplete. Do you truly allow each moment of obedience to be whole and undivided, or do you let yourself be half-hearted, half-present, even when you stand before Allah? How can you ensure that your present moment is wholly devoted to Him?
?
Razi argues that the present part of motion must be indivisible, or else nothing could ever be fully present. Your entire life is a series of these atomic moments. If you lose this moment, you lose it forever. Have you ever reflected that your current breath could be your last? If you knew that this present moment is all you have, what urgent act of obedience or repentance would you perform right now? Write one concrete action you will take within this hour.
?
If motion itself requires indivisible temporal units, then your ability to change—to turn from sin or to move closer to Allah—is contained in the smallest unit of time. Do you take that moment for granted? Or do you seize each present moment as a precious gift from Allah, using it for what truly matters before it slips into the past? Proof from Time to Discrete Points
Razi proves body composition from time's discrete nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that time is composed of indivisible instants, and why this leads to the conclusion that bodies are composed of discrete atoms.

Core Idea
Time's successive instants prove that body must be made of discrete points.

Razi now presents a second argument for atomism, this time based on the nature of time. If time were continuous, it would involve connecting non-existent past and future with an existent boundary—an absurdity. Thus time must be a discrete series of 'nows,' and from this, the atomic composition of bodies follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get the overall argument.
Notice the two aspects Razi uses to prove time's discrete nature.
2
Understand the first aspect: time as quantity
Razi argues that time cannot be continuous because that would require a non-existent past and future to connect via an existent now. This forces time to be a discrete quantity (كم منفصل).
The key phrase: 'يلزم أن يكون أحد متصلا بالمعدوم الآخر بطرف موجود وهو محال'.
3
Understand the second aspect: indivisibility of the now
Razi also argues that each 'now' is indivisible and that its non-existence occurs instantly, leading to a succession of discrete instants.
This reinforces the discrete nature of time.
4
Connect to body composition
Having established time as discrete, Razi concludes that body (which moves through space in time) must also be composed of discrete points, using the same reasoning as in the first proof.
The conclusion is: 'فوجب أن يكون الجسم مركبا من نقط متشافعة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية، فوجب أن يكون الجسم مركبا من نقط متشافعة. بيان المقدمة الأولى من وجهين: الأول : أن الزمان كم . وهو إما كم منفصل أو متصل لا جائز أن يكون كَما متصلا لأن الماضي معدوم والمستقبل معدوم والآن طرف، فيلزم أن يكون أحد متصلا بالمعدوم الآخر بطرف موجود وهو محال فإذن هو كم منفصل فيكون مركبا عن وحدات متعاقبة. وهو المقصود. الثاني : أن الآن الحاضر غير منقسم وإلا لم يكن حاضرًا. وإذا ثبت هذا، فعدمه يقع أيضا دفعة واحدة فيكون عدمه حاصلا عقيب وجوده. وكذا القول في الثاني. وهذا يقتضي تعاقب الآنات وإذا ثبت أن الزمان مركب من الآنات المتتالية وجب أن يكون الجسم مركبا من نقط متشافعة للتقريب المذكور في الطريقة الأولى.
The second proof: Time is composed of successive instants, so it is necessary that the body be composed of contiguous points. Explanation of the first premise from two aspects: First: that time is a quantity. It is either a discrete quantity or a continuous quantity. It cannot be continuous, because the past is non-existent and the future is non-existent and the now is a boundary. Then it would follow that one non-existent is connected to another non-existent by an existent boundary, which is impossible. Therefore it is a discrete quantity, so it is composed of successive units. That is the intended point. Second: that the present now is indivisible, otherwise it would not be present. And when this is established, its non-existence also occurs instantaneously, so its non-existence comes to be immediately after its existence. Likewise for the second. This necessitates the succession of nows. And when it is established that time is composed of successive nows, it is necessary that the body be composed of contiguous points, according to the approximation mentioned in the first method.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the atomic composition of bodies, this time from the nature of time. The proof has two parts. First, Razi establishes that time is a discrete quantity (كم منفصل) rather than continuous. He argues that if time were continuous, then the past and future (both non-existent) would be connected by an existent boundary (the present now), which is impossible. Hence time must consist of indivisible units (nows). Second, he argues that each present 'now' is indivisible, because if it were divisible it would not be present in its entirety. Its annihilation also occurs instantly, leading to a succession of discrete nows. Having established that time is composed of successive instants, Razi concludes that the body (which moves through space in time) must be composed of contiguous points (atoms), referring to the same reasoning used in the first proof.

آن
كم منفصل
نقطة
متشافعة

C) Because the present now is divisible.
D) Because time has no quantity.

Key Takeaway
Time is composed of successive indivisible instants, and body shares this discrete structure.

The Point as a Real Existent Entity
Razi starts the third proof by stating the point's existence and indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's initial argument that the point is a real, indivisible entity and how this serves as the foundation for proving the existence of the atom (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
The point is a real existent that cannot divide, proving the atom.

Razi continues his series of proofs for atomism by focusing on the nature of the point. He asserts that the point is not a mere mental abstraction but a concrete, referable entity that is indivisible. From this, he infers the necessary existence of the indivisible atom. This lesson examines the first part of his proof, establishing the reality of the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi states his conclusion before giving the supporting evidence.
The passage is cut off; focus on the initial claim and the first sub-point.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify Razi's assertions: the point is existent, referable, does not divide, and these necessitate the indivisible substance.
3
Analyze the proof for existence
Examine Razi's proof that the point exists: a line is actually finite, therefore its endpoint (the point) is actually existent.
This is a kalam argument against infinite regress in physical extension.
4
Connect to atomism
Understand why this proof supports atomism: if points are indivisible and real, then matter must be composed of indivisible units.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : النقطة شيء ،موجود، مشار إليها . وهي لا تنقسم، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان القول بالجوهر الفرد لازما أما قولنا مشار إليها وهي لا تنقسم فهذا لا يتم إلا ببيان أمور : الأول : إن النقطة شيء موجود وهذا متفق عليه. إلا أنا نقول الدليل عليه أن الخط متناه بالفعل. وإذا كان متناهيا بالفعل كانت نهايته موجودة بالفعل. ولا
The third proof: The point is something existent, referable. And it does not divide. And whenever it is thus, then the assertion of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) is necessary. As for our saying 'referable and it does not divide', this is only completed by explaining matters: First: that the point is something existent, and this is agreed upon. But we say: the proof of it is that the line is actually finite. And if it is actually finite, then its end is actually existent. And not...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof for the existence of the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard). Razi states the conclusion first: the point is an existing, referable entity that does not divide, and if this is so, then the atomic substance is necessarily admitted. He then notes that the claim 'it is referable and does not divide' requires demonstration through several points. He begins with the first point: that the point exists. This is agreed upon, but he provides a proof: because a line is actually finite (muntahan bil-fi'l), and if so, its endpoint is actually existent. The passage cuts off at 'and not...', leaving the argument incomplete. The overall direction is to show that the point (a limit of a line) is an existent indivisible entity, implying the existence of atoms.

Correction: Razi's point is a real, existing entity that can be indicated. Its indivisibility directly supports the existence of atoms as the ultimate constituents of matter.

Proof from Motion: Body Composed of Indivisible Parts
Razi's first proof that body and distance are composed of indivisible parts, using motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalām proof that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (atoms), based on the nature of motion and a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
Motion proves atoms: each indivisible moment of motion implies an indivisible unit of distance.

Imam Razi presents a decisive argument for the kalām atomistic view. Starting from the nature of motion, he demonstrates that motion consists of successive indivisible units, and from this he infers that the distance traversed and thus the body itself must be composed of contiguous indivisible parts. This lesson walks through the logical steps of this classic reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from motion to distance to body.
The passage is the Twenty-Seventh Problem of Razi's Al-Arba'een.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The argument has two main parts: first, proving that motion consists of indivisible parts; second, using that to prove that the distance and body also consist of such parts.
3
Understand the reductio (خلف)
Focus on the 'خلف' part: if the distance traversed in an indivisible moment were divisible, then motion would be divisible, contradicting the earlier conclusion.
This is a classic absurdity argument.
4
Connect to the larger kalām context
This proof supports the atomistic view of the kalām theologians. Reflect on why this was important for their theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة والعشرون
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا انقضى ذلك الجزء حصل عقيب انقضائه شيء آخر، أيضا حاضر . فوجب أن لا يكون هو أيضا منقسما . وهكذا القول في جميع الأجزاء الواقعة في تلك الحركة إلى آخرها . فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع القاهر أن الحركة مركبة من أمور متتالية كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة البتة. وإذا ثبت هذا قلنا : وجب أن يكون الجسم أيضا كذلك، لأن القدر الذي يقطع من المسافة بالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ من الحركة إن كان منقسما كانت الحركة إلى نصفها نصف الحركة إلى آخرها . وحينئذ يكون ذلك الجزء من الحركة منقسما وقد فرضنا أنه غير منقسم. هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو المطلوب.
ولما كانت الحركة مركبة من أجزاء متتالية. كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة، وثبت أن هذا القدر المقطوع بكل واحد منهما من المسافة غير منقسم، وجب أن تكون المسافة مركبة من أجزاء متلاصقة كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة المطلوب.
المقصود.
The Twenty-Seventh Problem. If this is established, we say: when that part elapses, there occurs immediately after its elapsing another thing, also present. So it must also be indivisible. And the same applies to all parts occurring in that motion until its end. Thus it is established by this decisive and overwhelming proof that motion is composed of successive things each of which is absolutely indivisible. When this is established, we say: it is necessary that the body also is likewise, because the amount of distance traversed by the indivisible part of motion, if it were divisible, then the motion to its half would be half the motion to its end. Then that part of motion would be divisible, whereas we assumed it is indivisible. This is a contradiction. And if it is not divisible, then it is the desired conclusion. And since motion is composed of successive parts each of which is indivisible, and it is established that this amount traversed by each of them of distance is indivisible, it is necessary that the distance be composed of contiguous parts each of which is indivisible, which is the desired conclusion. End of purpose.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) in body, based on the nature of motion. It begins by assuming a previous premise (likely that some part of motion is indivisible) and then argues recursively: when that part ends, the next part that appears must also be indivisible, because it is also present and not composite. This continues for all parts of motion, so motion is composed of successive indivisible units. From this, it infers that the body (that moves) must also be composed of such parts: the distance traversed in each indivisible moment of motion must itself be indivisible, otherwise the motion would be divisible. If the distance were divisible, then the motion to the midpoint would be half the total motion, making the original part of motion divisible, contradicting the assumption. Therefore, the distance is composed of indivisible parts, and thus the body (which occupies that distance) is also composed of indivisible contiguous parts. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum (خلف) and supports the Kalām atomistic view.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
حركة
مسافة
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes atomism and then proves it.
Correction: It argues from the nature of motion that each successive part must be indivisible, not assuming it. The reductio step further shows that distance parts must be indivisible, leading to the atomistic conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the parts of motion be indivisible?
A) Because time is a continuum
B) Because the next part after an indivisible part must also be indivisible, otherwise motion would be divisible
C) Because space is composed of points
D) Because the body is a continuous substance

Key Takeaway
The kalām atomistic view is supported by a logical proof: motion's indivisibility entails that the body and distance are also composed of indivisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion of your body is made up of discrete, indivisible moments, each sustained by Allah directly, how does that change your awareness of His constant creative power in your daily actions? Will you remind yourself of this during your next prayer or step?
?
The proof shows that the smallest unit of motion cannot be divided further. What does that imply about the limits of human power? In what area of your life do you need to admit your inability and rely entirely on Allah's control over every 'atom' of your existence?
?
Razi's argument forces you to accept that the world is not a smooth continuum but a series of discrete events. Does that make you more conscious of each moment as a separate gift from Allah? Commit to one specific action today that acknowledges this moment-by-moment dependence.

Time as Discrete Quantity Implies Atomic Matter
Second proof tying time's discreteness to atomic bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof uses the discrete nature of time to establish that bodies are composed of indivisible points.

Core Idea
Time's discrete nature forces matter to be atomic.

This lesson presents Imam al-Razi's second proof for atoms. It argues that time must be composed of indivisible moments, and since motion in time corresponds to spatial extension, bodies too must be made of indivisible points. Two arguments support the composed nature of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice that the proof has two distinct sub-arguments.
Pay attention to the logical flow from time to body.
2
Examine the first argument
Time is a quantity. It cannot be continuous because that would connect non-existent past and future via an existent present boundary, which is absurd. Therefore it must be discrete, composed of successive units.
This is based on the definition of quantity as either discrete or continuous.
3
Examine the second argument
The present moment is indivisible. Its non-existence also occurs at once, creating a succession of moments. This shows time is made of indivisible atoms.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: time is composed of indivisible moments. By the same reasoning as the first proof (motion and spatial extension), body must then be composed of contiguous indivisible points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية، فوجب أن يكون الجسم مركبا من نقط متشافعة. بيان المقدمة الأولى من وجهين: الأول : أن الزمان كم . وهو إما كم منفصل أو متصل لا جائز أن يكون كَما متصلا لأن الماضي معدوم والمستقبل معدوم والآن طرف، فيلزم أن يكون أحد متصلا بالمعدوم الآخر بطرف موجود وهو محال فإذن هو كم منفصل فيكون الزمان مركبا عن وحدات متعاقبة. وهو المقصود. الثاني : أن الآن الحاضر غير منقسم وإلا لم يكن حاضرًا. وإذا ثبت هذا، فعدمه يقع أيضا دفعة واحدة فيكون عدمه حاصلا عقيب وجوده. وكذا القول في الثاني. وهذا يقتضي تعاقب الآنات وإذا ثبت أن الزمان مركب من الآنات المتتالية وجب أن يكون الجسم مركبا من نقط متشافعة للتقريب المذكور في الطريقة الأولى.
The second proof: Time is composed of successive moments (ānāt), so it is necessary that the body be composed of contiguous points. The demonstration of the first premise (that time is composed of successive moments) is from two aspects: First: Time is a quantity (kamm). It is either a discrete quantity or a continuous quantity. It cannot be a continuous quantity because the past is non-existent and the future is non-existent, and the present is a boundary. Then it would follow that one non-existent is connected to another non-existent by an existent boundary, which is absurd. So it is a discrete quantity, thus time is composed of successive units (waḥdāt mutaʿāqiba). That is the purpose. Second: The present moment (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) is indivisible, otherwise it would not be present. When this is established, its non-existence also occurs all at once, so its non-existence occurs immediately after its existence. The same applies to the second. This implies the succession of moments. And when it is established that time is composed of successive moments, it is necessary that the body be composed of contiguous points, by the same reasoning mentioned in the first method.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This is the second proof for the composition of bodies from indivisible atoms, based on the nature of time. It argues that time is composed of indivisible moments (آنا). Two arguments are given: (1) time is a quantity; it must be discrete, not continuous, because past and future are non-existent and the present is a boundary. If time were continuous, then a non-existent past would be connected to a non-existent future by an existent boundary, which is impossible. Hence time is discrete, made of successive indivisible units. (2) The present moment is indivisible, otherwise it would not be a single present. Its non-existence also occurs at once, creating succession. Since time is composed of such moments, and the motion in time corresponds to spatial extension, the body must be composed of contiguous indivisible points, as argued in the first method.

Key Terms
آن
كم
كم منفصل
كم متصل
آنات متتالية
نقط متشافعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a continuous flowing continuum.
Correction: The proof shows time cannot be continuous because that would require a non-existent past to be connected to a non-existent future by an existent present boundary, which is absurd. Time must be composed of discrete, indivisible moments.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second proof?
A) Time is continuous
B) Time is composed of indivisible moments, leading to body being composed of indivisible points
C) Body is a continuous substance
D) Motion is an illusion

Key Takeaway
The composition of time from indivisible moments logically entails the composition of body from indivisible points.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that the present is a boundary between what has gone and what is yet to come. Do you treat your current actions as decisive, knowing that each moment is unique and irreversible? How can you make this present moment count for your ultimate purpose?
?
This argument challenges a common assumption about time. Are you willing to let reason reshape your worldview, even if it feels uncomfortable? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that time is composed of discrete, precious gifts from Allah?

Third Proof: The Point and the Individual Substance
al-Razi's third proof for the individual substance via the point's reality, perceptibility, and indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that the point exists, is perceptible and indivisible, and why this entails the existence of an indivisible substance (atom).

Core Idea
The endpoint of a finite line must exist and be indivisible, proving the substance atom.

Imam al-Razi now presents a detailed proof for the existence of indivisible substances (jawhar fard) based on the nature of a point. He argues that the point at the end of a finite line is real, perceptible, and indivisible, and then shows that such a point must be a substance, not an accident, leading to the conclusion that indivisible substances exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow and the three sub-claims about the point.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the objection and response
Identify the objection that the endpoint is non-existence and al-Razi's reply using the concept of contact between two magnitudes.
This is a key refutation.
3
Follow the indivisibility argument
Examine why the point cannot be divided: if divided, its endpoint would not be the true endpoint, leading to a contradiction.
Note the logical structure: if divisible, then not entirely an endpoint.
4
Trace the final deduction
See how al-Razi concludes that the point must be a substance, not an accident, by considering the substrate. If it were an accident, it would be divisible or lead to infinite regress.
This establishes the individual substance.
5
Review the overarching proof
Go back and see how the three properties of the point together force the conclusion that indivisible substances exist.
This is the third proof in al-Razi's series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : النقطة شيء موجود، مشار إليها . وهي لا تنقسم، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان القول بالجوهر الفرد لازما. أما قولنا مشار إليها وهي لا تنقسم فهذا لا يتم إلا ببيان أمور : الأول : إن النقطة شيء موجود وهذا متفق عليه. إلا أنا نقول الدليل عليه أن الخط متناه بالفعل. وإذا كان متناهيا بالفعل كانت نهايته موجودة بالفعل. ولا معنى للنقطة إلا نهاية الخط . فثبت أن النقطة موجودة بالفعل. فإن قيل : نهاية الشيء عبارة عن انقطاعه ، وانقطاع الشيء عبارة عن أنه فني وما بقي منه شيء البتة وعدم الشيء كيف يكون أمرا موجودا؟ قلنا : نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن المقدارين إذا تماسا فإنهما يتماسان بطرفيهما، فلو كان طرف الشيء نفس العدم لكان معنى المماسة، هو عدم هذا مماس لعدم ذاك وهذا غير معقول. فعلمنا أن طرف الشيء يستحيل أن يكون نفس العدم. وأما المطلوب الثاني وهو أن النقطة شيء مشار إليه – فذلك ظاهر ؛ لأنه يمكننا أن نشير بالحس إلى طرف الخط . وأما المطلوب الثالث - وهو أن النقطة غير قابلة للقسمة - فالذي يدل عليها أنها لو انقسمت لافترض فيها جزءان وحينئذ يكون طرف الخط هو القسم الثاني فقط، وحينئذ لا يكون الطرف طرفا . هذا خلف. فالحاصل أن كل ما كان منقسما، لم يكن كله طرفا وكل ما كان كله طرفا ، لم يكن منقسما والنقطة عبارة عن نفس الطرف، فوجب أن لا تكون منقسمة، ولما ثبت بالبرهان هذه المطالب الثلاثة ظهر أن النقطة موجودة مشار إليها، غير منقسمة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا الشيء إما أن يكون جوهرًا، وإما أن يكون عرضًا. لا جائز أن يكون عرضًا. وإلا لافتقر إلى محل ومحله إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامه بانقسام محله . وذلك محال . وإن لم يكن منقسما عاد التقسيم فيه. وهو أنه إما أن يكون جوهرًا أو عرضًا ويلزم إما التسلسل - وهو محال - أو الانتهاء إلى جوهر غير قابل للقسمة وهو المطلوب.
The third proof: The point (nuqṭa) is a thing that exists, is perceptible, and is indivisible. When this is the case, the assertion of the individual substance (jawhar fard) is necessary. As for our statement that it is perceptible and indivisible, this is not complete except by establishing three matters: First: The point is an existing thing, and this is agreed upon. However, we say the proof for it is that the line is actually finite. When it is actually finite, its endpoint actually exists. And there is no meaning for 'point' except the endpoint of a line. Thus it is established that the point actually exists. If it is said: 'The endpoint of a thing is its cessation, and cessation of a thing is that it has perished and nothing of it remains; how can non-existence be an existing thing?' We reply: We know by necessity that when two magnitudes are in contact, they are in contact by their extremities. If the extremity of a thing were identical to non-existence, then the meaning of contact would be the non-existence of this touching the non-existence of that, which is unintelligible. So we know that the extremity of a thing is impossible to be identical to non-existence. Second required matter: That the point is a perceptible thing – this is obvious, because we can point by sense to the endpoint of a line. Third required matter: That the point is indivisible – what indicates this is that if it were divided, two parts would be assumed, and then the endpoint of the line would be only the second part, so the endpoint would not be an endpoint. This is a contradiction. The result: everything that is divisible is not entirely an endpoint, and everything that is entirely an endpoint is not divisible. The point is identical to the endpoint itself, so it must be indivisible. When these three matters are established by proof, it becomes clear that the point exists, is perceptible, and is indivisible. When this is established, we say: This thing is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ). It cannot be an accident, because then it would depend on a substrate, and if that substrate is divisible, then it would be divisible due to the division of its substrate – which is impossible. And if the substrate is indivisible, then the same division recurs: it is either a substance or an accident, leading either to infinite regress – which is absurd – or to an indivisible substance, which is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This is the third and most detailed proof for the existence of indivisible substances (الجوهر الفرد), based on the concept of a point. The argument proceeds in three steps to establish that the point exists, is perceptible, and is indivisible. Then it argues that such a point must be a substance, not an accident, by a regress argument. The first step: the point exists because the endpoint of a finite line actually exists; an objection that the endpoint is merely non-existence is refuted by the fact that contact between two magnitudes occurs at their extremities, so extremities cannot be non-existence. The second step is straightforward sensory pointing. The third step: if a point were divisible, its dividing point would not be the endpoint, contradicting its definition. Therefore, the point is indivisible. Then, since it exists and is not divisible, it must be a substance (because an accident would depend on a substrate and either be divisible by the substrate's division or lead to infinite regress). Thus the existence of the individual substance (atom) is proven.

Key Terms
نقطة
جوهر فرد
عرض
نهاية
تماس
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The endpoint of a line is merely the absence of the line, non-existence.
Correction: al-Razi argues that contact between two magnitudes requires existent extremities, so the endpoint must exist.

Checkpoint

A) By citing scripture
B) By analyzing contact between magnitudes
C) By geometric construction
D) By consensus

Key Takeaway
A finite line's endpoint is an existent, perceptible, indivisible entity, which must be a substance, thus establishing the atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The proof argues that every finite line must have a real, existing endpoint. Consider the finite span of your own life: what is the endpoint you are moving toward, and do you treat it as a real, existing reality—or do you live as if it were mere non-existence? What would change today if you truly acknowledged its reality?
?
The indivisible point is proven to be a substance, not an accident—it cannot be reduced to something else. Identify one core belief about Allah, the unseen, or your own soul that you hold as indivisible and non-negotiable. Are you living as if that belief is a substantial reality that shapes everything, or have you let it become a mere accident, dependent on other circumstances?
?
al-Razi uses the impossibility of infinite regress to stop at an indivisible substance. In your spiritual life, are there excuses or distractions that you keep pushing back infinitely, avoiding a final, decisive commitment? What would it mean for you to stop the regress and acknowledge an indivisible, ultimate truth that demands a response now?

The Moving Line Proof for Indivisible Points
Proof that a moving line reveals composition from indivisible points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a geometric thought experiment with moving lines demonstrates that a line—and therefore a body—must be composed of indivisible points.

Core Idea
A moving line's contact point by point proves indivisible building blocks.

Imam Razi presents a clever geometric proof to establish that bodies are composed of indivisible points (atoms). He imagines a line sliding across another line, and argues that the endpoint of the moving line must touch the fixed line at successive points. This shows that the moving line itself is made of points, each of which cannot be further divided. Let's walk through this fascinating argument.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the setup
Imagine a vertical line standing on a horizontal line. The vertical line moves from one side of the horizontal line to the other.
Think of a pen moving across a ruler.
2
Focus on the endpoint
As the vertical line moves, its endpoint (a single point) touches the horizontal line at one point after another. Each contact is a distinct point.
The endpoint is the 'tip' of the moving line.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the moving line is composed of the series of endpoints that made contact, it must be made of indivisible points. Therefore, any body (representable as a line) is composed of indivisible parts.
This is a foundational proof for kalam atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إذا فرضنا خطا قائما على خط ثم إن الخط القائم انتقل من أحد جانبي الخط الثاني إلى الجانب الآخر. فهذا الخط المتحرك صار بجميع الأجزاء المفترضة فيه ممسوس طرف الخط المتحرك، وطرف الخط المتحرك نقطة النقطة نقطة، فالخط المتحرك عليه وجب أن يكون مركبا من النقط التي كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة ولا معنى لقولنا الجسم مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ إلا ذلك.
The fourth proof: If we suppose a line standing upon a line, and then the standing line moves from one side of the second line to the other side, then this moving line, with all its assumed parts, touches the endpoint of the moving line. And the endpoint of the moving line is a point. Point by point, therefore the moving line must be composed of points each of which is indivisible. And there is no meaning to our saying that the body is composed of parts that are indivisible except that.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This is a short proof. It posits a vertical line moving across a horizontal line. As the vertical line moves, its endpoint (a point) contacts the horizontal line at successive points. The vertical line, being composed of a series of such points, must itself be composed of indivisible points. This shows that any body, considered as a line, is composed of indivisible points. The conclusion is that the body is composed of inseparable indivisible parts.

Key Terms
خط قائم
ممسوس
نقط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The motion of a line across another line
B) The divisibility of space
C) The definition of a point
D) The nature of time

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof uses a moving line to argue that all bodies are composed of indivisible points, a cornerstone of kalam atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever dismissed a rational argument for a belief because it seemed too abstract, while your daily choices are governed by habit rather than conviction? What belief do you hold that is based on proof, and what belief do you hold merely because you have never questioned it?

The Sphere and Plane Proof of Indivisible Parts
Fifth proof using a sphere on a plane to demonstrate an indivisible point of contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric proof that the point of tangency between a sphere and a plane is indivisible, and how this supports the existence of indivisible parts in bodies.

Core Idea
A sphere's point of contact with a plane cannot be divisible without flattening the sphere.

Imam Razi presents a visual geometrical argument to demonstrate that bodies must be composed of indivisible parts. By considering a sphere on a plane, he shows that the point of tangency must be a single indivisible point. This proof relies on a simple property of contact between curved and flat surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: dichotomy then refutation.
2
Visualize the scenario
Imagine a perfectly smooth sphere placed on a perfectly flat plane. Think about where they touch. Is it a point or a small area? The proof argues it must be a point.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the contact point is indivisible, then it is an atom of space. This suggests that physical bodies are composed of such indivisible parts, a key claim of kalam atomism.
This connects to the broader doctrine of indivisible parts (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إذا وضعنا كرة حقيقية على سطح حقيقي، فموضع المماسة إما أن يكون منقسما أو لا يكون . والأول باطل وإلا لكان ذلك الموضع من الكرة منطبقا على السطح المستقيم، والمنطبق على المستقيم مستقيم فذلك الموضع من الكرة مستقيم .
The fifth proof: If we place a real sphere on a real plane, then the point of contact (mawḍiʿ al-mumāssa) is either divisible or not. The first is false, because otherwise that place of the sphere would conform to the straight surface, and that which conforms to a straight line is straight, so that place of the sphere would be straight.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This proof argues from the geometry of a sphere on a plane. The point of tangency must be indivisible, because if it were divisible, then the sphere at that point would be flat (straight), but a sphere is curved. Therefore, the point of tangency is an indivisible point, implying the existence of indivisible parts in the body. The argument is incomplete in the text but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
كرة حقيقية
سطح حقيقي
مماسة
منطبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sphere touches the plane over a small area (multiple points).
Correction: The proof shows that the point of tangency must be a single indivisible point, otherwise the sphere would be flat at that area.

Checkpoint

A) Because a sphere cannot touch a plane
B) Because division would require a third dimension
C) Because a divisible point would imply the sphere is flat
D) Because points are imaginary

Key Takeaway
The point of contact between a sphere and a plane is indivisible, supporting the existence of indivisible parts in bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Let this tension drive you to examine your own assumptions about reality.

The Sphere Argument for Indivisible Points
Razi uses a rolling sphere to prove that a circle is composed of indivisible points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a rolling sphere to argue that a circle is composed of indivisible points.

Core Idea
A rolling sphere's contact points must be indivisible to maintain roundness.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a clever geometric proof for the existence of indivisible points. By considering the points of contact as a sphere rolls along a line, he shows that if those points were divisible, the sphere would either become flat or polygonal—both absurd. This argument supports the Ash'ari atomist view that space is composed of discrete units.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the logical flow: rolling sphere, contact points, straight vs angled connection, absurdity, conclusion.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenarios
Focus on the two possibilities: if the connection between contact points is straight, the sphere becomes flat; if angled, it becomes polygonal. Why are both impossible?
Because a sphere is uniformly curved.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The only way to avoid absurdity is that each contact point is indivisible. Therefore, the circle (a line) is composed of contiguous indivisible points.
This is a foundational argument for atomism in kalam.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology? It establishes that matter is not infinitely divisible, supporting the idea that creation is made of discrete atoms willed by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة والعشرون: فإذا تدحرجت الكرة فالموضع الثاني من مواضع المماسة يكون أيضا مستقيما ثم إن موضع المماسة الثانية لا شك أنه منطبق متصل بموضع المماسة الأولى. فإن كان ذلك الاتصال على الاستقامة صارت الكرة سطحا مستويا وإن كان لا على الاستقامة، بل على الزاوية، صارت الكرة مضلعة . هذا خلف. فثبت أن موضع المماسة من الكرة شيء غير قابل للقسمة وإذا تدحرجت الكرة حتى تمت الدائرة، كانت تلك الدائرة مركبة من مواضع المماسات، وتلك المواضع نقط غير مستقيمة، فحينئذ حصلت تلك الدائرة خطا مركبا من النقط المتلاصقة . وذلك هو المطلوب.
The twenty-seventh issue: When the sphere rolls, the second position of the points of contact is also straight. Then the second point of contact is undoubtedly contiguous and connected to the first point of contact. If that connection is straight, the sphere becomes a flat surface; and if it is not straight but at an angle, the sphere becomes polygonal. This is absurd. So it is established that the point of contact of the sphere is something indivisible. When the sphere rolls until completing the circle, that circle is composed of the points of contact, and those points are non-straight points; then that circle becomes a line composed of contiguous points. That is what was required.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a geometric argument to prove the existence of indivisible points (atoms) in a circle. The argument proceeds by considering a sphere rolling on a line. When the sphere rolls, each successive point of contact is assumed to be straight (i.e., on the same line). If the connection between these points is straight, the sphere would be a flat surface; if at an angle, it would be polygonal. Both are false because a sphere is round. Therefore, each point of contact must be an indivisible point. Thus, when the sphere completes a full rotation, the circle is composed of these indivisible points, and the line of the circle is made of contiguous points. This refutes the idea of infinite divisibility.

Key Terms
كرة (sphere)
مماسة (contact/tangency)
نقطة (point)
غير قابل للقسمة (indivisible)
خط (line)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space and matter are infinitely divisible.
Correction: The sphere argument shows that if contact points were divisible, the sphere would become either flat or polygonal, both absurd. Therefore, points must be indivisible, supporting atomism.

Checkpoint
What happens if the connection between contact points is straight according to the sphere argument?
A) The sphere becomes a flat surface
B) The sphere becomes polygonal
C) The sphere remains a sphere
D) The points become divisible

Key Takeaway
Razi's sphere argument demonstrates that a circle must be composed of indivisible points, thereby supporting the kalam atomist view of matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every point of contact in the rolling sphere is indivisible, what does that tell you about the very structure of your own body? Are you willing to accept that your physical existence is built from tiny, indivisible particles placed by Allah? How does that affect your awe of His creation?
?
Razi's proof uses reason to defend a theological claim about the world. When you face doubts about your faith, do you actively seek rational arguments like this one? What keeps you from diving deeper into such proofs?
?
Imagine you are the sphere rolling along a line. Each point of contact is a moment of existence. If those points are indivisible, how does that change the way you think about the continuity of your life? Are there moments in your spiritual journey where you have been 'polygonal'—trying to be something you are not—instead of submitting to the straight path of Allah?

Sixth Argument: First Premise, First Face – Distinctness of Points
Razi proves that if a line were divisible infinitely, its points would be actually present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that if a line were infinitely divisible, then every possible point would actually exist, based on the unique property of each division point.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility forces all possible points into actual existence.

Imam al-Razi presents the sixth argument against infinite divisibility. He first establishes that if a body were infinitely divisible, it would be composed of actually infinite parts. To prove the first premise, he argues that every point in a line has a unique property, making it actually distinct. The following passage explains this reasoning in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the overall argument
Start by reading the passage and its translation. Notice Razi's structure: a conditional statement followed by an explanation.
The conclusion is stated at the beginning.
2
Understand the first premise
Identify the claim that if a point has a unique property, it is actually distinct. See how Razi applies this to points in a line.
The property is being the exact location of a specific division.
3
Follow the proof for uniqueness
Razi uses the example of midpoint, third-point, etc. Each has a fixed, unchangeable position. This proves each point's uniqueness.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو كان الجسم قابلا لانقسامات لا نهاية لها لكان مركبا من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وهذا محال، فذلك محال. بيان المقام الأول من وجهين : الأول: أن كل موضع اختص بخاصية لا تحصل في سائر المواضع، كان ذلك الموضع متميزا بالفعل عن سائر المواضع. وكل نقطة يمكن فرضها في خط، فإن تلك النقطة مختصة بخاصية ممتنعة الحصول في سائر النقط، فيلزم أن تكون كل النقط التي يمكن فرضها في الخط أن تكون حاصلة بالفعل. مقدمات هذه الحجة جلية إلا قولنا إن كل واحدة من النقط التي يمكن فرضها في الخط، فإنها تختص بخاصية لا توجد في سائر النقط التي يمكن فرضها. والدليل عليه : أن كل خط فإن مقطع النصف منه موضع معين، ويستحيل أن يكون غير تلك النقطة موضع النصف. وكذا القول في مقطع الثلث ومقطع الربع فإنك إن زدت على مقطع الثلث شيئا، أو نقصت منه شيئا، لم يكن ذلك مقطع الثلث بل مقطعا آخر وكذا القول في جميع المقاطع التي لانهاية لها . فإن لكل واحد منها موضعا لا يمكن أن يزاد عليه أو ينقص منه . فثبت أن كل نقطة فرضها في هذا الخط فإنها مختصة بخاصية يمتنع حصولها في سائر النقط الممكنة الفرض في هذا الخط . فثبت أنه لو كان الخط قابلا لانقسامات لا نهاية لها، لكانت تلك الانقسامات حاصلة فيه بالفعل.
The sixth argument: If a body were receptive to infinite divisions, it would be composed of actually infinite parts. This is impossible, so that is impossible. Explanation of the first station from two aspects: The first: Every position that is distinguished by a property not found in other positions is actually distinct from other positions. And every point that can be supposed in a line is distinguished by a property whose acquisition is impossible in other points, so it follows that all points that can be supposed in the line are actually present. The premises of this argument are clear except for our statement that every one of the points that can be supposed in the line is distinguished by a property not found in the other points that can be supposed. The proof for it: Every line has a midpoint that is a specific position, and it is impossible for that point to be the position of the half. Similarly for the third-point and the quarter-point; if you add something to the third-point or subtract something from it, it is not the third-point but another point. Similarly for all the infinite divisions. For each one has a position that cannot be increased or decreased from it. So it is established that every point supposed in this line is distinguished by a property whose acquisition is impossible in the other points possible to suppose in this line. So it is established that if the line were receptive to infinite divisions, those divisions would actually exist in it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is part of the sixth argument against infinite divisibility. Razi first states the overall argument: if a body were infinitely divisible, it would be composed of actually infinite parts, which is impossible. Then he explains the first premise (that infinite divisions would entail actually existing infinite parts) via two aspects. This lesson covers the first aspect. The reasoning is: any position that has a unique property is actually distinct. Each point in a line has a unique property (e.g., being the midpoint, the third-point, etc.). Therefore, if infinite divisions were possible, all those points would be actually distinct and present. A proof is given: for any line, the midpoint is a specific point; similarly for the third-point, etc. Each has a unique position that cannot be altered. Hence, all possible points would actually exist.

Key Terms
انقسام
خاصية
مقطع النصف
مقطع الثلث
مقطع الربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possible points are merely mental concepts with no actual existence.
Correction: Razi argues that each point has a unique property that would make it actually distinct if division were possible, thus they would be actual.

Checkpoint
What does Razi use to prove each point in a line is distinct?
A) The unique property of each division point
B) The physical separation of atoms
C) The impossibility of motion
D) The consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
Infinite divisibility would require all possible division points to actually exist, which is impossible.

What one sin are you treating as if it could be infinitely postponed or reinterpreted, when in fact it is a distinct point in your life that demands a definite response?

Razi shows that infinite divisions would force all possible points to become actual—nothing remains merely potential. What would it mean to treat each of them as if they must be actual right now?

The argument insists that each division point has a fixed, unchangeable position. Think of a moral boundary you have considered flexible. How does this argument press you to see that boundary as fixed and distinct, not subject to incremental erosion?

The Dilemma of Indication and Actual Parts
Razi uses a dilemma to argue that parts exist actually before division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the parts of a body must actually exist before any act of division (or indication), and why the alternative leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Before you point, the halves are already there—otherwise the universe would vanish at every thought.

Razi continues to defend the premise that bodies contain actually existing parts before any division. In this second aspect, he uses a simple thought experiment: when you point to a body, reason tells you that the two halves are distinct. He then forces a logical dilemma: either those halves existed before you pointed, or they did not. The consequences of each option will determine whether actual parts are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Focus on the logical flow: the dilemma and its two branches.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two options: (1) the halves existed before indication, (2) they did not. Notice that the first option leads directly to the conclusion that actual parts exist.
The second option is presented as a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the absurd consequence
Razi says that if halves only come into being at the moment of indication, then bodies would appear and disappear with every mental act. This would mean the entire universe oscillates in and out of existence based on our thoughts—which is irrational.
This shows why the second option must be rejected.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the second option is impossible, the first is necessary: the body already contains all possible actual parts. This also supports the mutakallimun's definition of division as separating contiguous parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في تقرير هذه المقدمة : أنا إذا أشرنا إلى جسم بسيط، فإن صريح العقل يشهد بأن هذا النصف منه مغاير للنصف الآخر منه. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول هذان النصفان قبل إشارتنا إليهما إما أن يقال : كانا موجودين أو ما كانا موجودين فإن كانا موجودين فهما كانا اثنين، وهذان الجزءان كانا موجودين بالفعل. ثم ننقل الكلام إلى نصف كل واحد من ذينك النصفين إلى آخر الانقسامات الممكنة. فيلزم أن يقال: إن بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة يحصل في الجسم أجزاء بالفعل. وهو المطلوب وأما إن قلنا بأن هذين الجزأين اللذين نشير إليهما ما كانا موجودين قبل إشارتنا إليهما، بل هذان الجسمان إنما حدثا عند إشارتنا إليهما فيلزم أن يقال بأن ذلك الجسم الذي كان قبل إشارتنا إليه واحداً صار ثانياً عند الإشارة. وحدث عند هذه الإشارة هذان النصفان. وهذا يقتضي أن يقال : الأجسام توجد وتعدم بحسب تغير أحوال الإشارات وهذا يقتضي أن هذه السموات والأرضين والجبال والبحار تعدم وتوجد في كل يوم ألف ألف مرة، بحسب إشارات المشيرين وتوهمات المتوهمين. ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالعقلاء، فثبت بما ذكرناه أن كل شيء يقبل الانقسام، فإن تلك الانقسامات كانت موجودة فيه بالفعل قبل التقسيم فظهر أن التقسيم عبارة عن تفريق المتجاورين، كما يقوله المتكلمون فثبت أن الجسم لو كان قابلا لانقسامات لا نهاية لها، لكانت تلك الأجزاء التي لانهاية لها، موجودة فيه بالفعل.
The second aspect in establishing this premise: When we point to a simple body, clear reason testifies that this half is different from the other half. Once you know this, we say: These two halves before our indication to them are either said to have existed or not existed. If they existed, then they were two, and these two parts existed actually. Then we transfer the discussion to half of each of those halves until the end of the possible divisions. So it necessarily follows that according to the possible divisions, parts exist in the body actually. That is what is required. And if we say that these two parts to which we point did not exist before our indication, but rather these two bodies only came into being at our indication, then it necessarily follows that the body which was one before our indication became two at the indication. And at that indication these two halves came into being. And this necessitates that bodies exist and cease to exist according to changes in the states of indications, and this necessitates that these heavens, earths, mountains, and seas cease to exist and come into existence a thousand thousand times every day, according to the indications of those who indicate and the suppositions of those who suppose. And it is known that this is not befitting of rational people. So it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that accepts division, its divisions existed in it actually before division. So it appears that division is the separation of contiguous things, as the mutakallimun say. So it is established that if a body were receptive to infinite divisions, then those infinite parts would actually exist in it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is the second aspect of the first premise of the sixth argument. Razi presents a thought experiment: when we point to a simple body, reason tells us the two halves are distinct. Before we point, either these halves exist actually or they do not. If they exist actually, then the body already contains actual parts, proving the premise. If they do not exist before indication, then the body would have to create them at the moment of indication, leading to the absurd consequence that bodies would come in and out of existence depending on our mental acts. This would mean the heavens and earth would appear and disappear countless times daily, which is irrational. Therefore, the only reasonable conclusion is that the parts exist actually before any act of division. This also confirms the mutakallimun's definition of division as separating contiguous parts. Thus, if a body were infinitely divisible, the infinite parts would actually exist in it.

Key Terms
إشارة (indication/pointing)
تقسيم (division)
متجاورين (contiguous)
متكلمون (mutakallimun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parts of a body only come into existence when we mentally or physically divide it.
Correction: Razi shows that if that were true, bodies would appear and disappear based on our thoughts, which is absurd. Therefore, parts must actually exist before any act of division or indication.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, what happens if the halves of a body do not exist before we point to them?
A) The body would be infinitely divisible
B) Bodies would appear and disappear based on arbitrary indications
C) The halves would merge back
D) The body would become simple

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma forces the conclusion that all possible divisions of a body must actually exist prior to any act of indication or division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's dilemma shows that if parts only existed when we pointed, the universe would be chaotic. ?
Razi argues that the second horn leads to an absurd universe where the heavens and earth would appear and disappear with every indication. In your daily life, do you act as if your opinions and perceptions determine truth, rather than submitting to what is actually the case? What would change if you truly believed that your mental acts do not create reality but only recognize what God has already made?
?
