Proof that Spatial Parts Are Finite (First Argument)
Al-Razi proves spatial parts are finite via traversal impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the traversal of distances in finite time to prove that the spatial parts of any distance are finite.

Core Idea
If we can cross a distance in finite time, its parts cannot be infinite.

This lesson presents al-Razi's first argument for the finitude of spatial parts. It is a classic kalām proof that uses a simple logical conditional: if the parts were infinite, then it would be impossible to traverse the distance in a finite time. Since we clearly do traverse distances, the parts must be finite. This argument is foundational for discussions about the composition of bodies and the impossibility of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the opening phrase about half and quarter, then the conditional structure.
The opening phrase may refer to the method of dividing the distance.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then' structure. The antecedent is 'if the parts were infinite', the consequent is 'reaching the end in finite time would be impossible'.
3
Observe the logical move
The author then states that this impossibility does not hold (we do reach the end), so the antecedent must be false. This is modus tollens.
Modus tollens: If A then B, not B, therefore not A.
4
Apply to the conclusion
The conclusion: 'we know that the sections occurring in the distance are finite'. The parts (maqati') are finite.
'Maqati'' refers to the subdivisions of the distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة والعشرون نصفها إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعها فلو كانت أجزاء غير متناهية، لامتنع الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها في مدة متناهية ولما لم يكن ذلك ممتنعا، علمنا : أن المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية
The twenty-seventh problem: its half except after reaching its quarter. For if the parts were infinite, reaching from its beginning to its end in a finite time would be impossible; and since that is not impossible, we know that the sections occurring in the distance are finite.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the parts of a continuous distance are finite. The author (al-Razi) begins with a partial statement about half and quarter, then states the conditional: if the parts were infinite, then it would be impossible to traverse the whole distance from start to finish in a finite time. The consequent is false (we do traverse distances in finite time), so the antecedent must be false. Therefore, the parts (al-maqati' al-hasila fi al-masafa) are finite. This is a standard kalām argument against infinite regress in spatial extension.

Key Terms
أجزاء غير متناهية
متناهية
المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes that time itself is finite, but the argument only uses the observation that we actually traverse distances in finite time, not that time must be finite.
Correction: The argument does not depend on any assumption about time; it simply uses the empirical fact that we complete journeys in finite durations.

Checkpoint
What logical form does al-Razi's argument take?
A) If A then B; A is true, so B is true.
B) If A then B; B is false, so A is false.
C) If A then B; B is true, so A is true.
D) A and B are both true, so C is true.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves that spatial parts are finite by showing that their infinitude would make finite traversal impossible, but we know traversal is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything in creation is finite—including your time, your energy, and your very life—how does that press you to live with urgency for the infinite that is your Lord? What will you do today that reflects the finitude of your hours?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that reason itself can prove the limits of the world. Do you trust that your intellect can lead you to certainty about the Creator? Or have you been relying on borrowed faith? Where do you need to personally engage with proofs like this to strengthen your conviction?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of the infinite in the created world. Yet you often act as if you have infinite time—postponing repentance, delaying good deeds. How will you remind yourself daily that your existence has finite parts that must be traversed before the end is reached?

Third Proof: Fast Overtaking Slow Proves Finite Parts
Al-Razi's third argument for finite parts using the overtaking paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the observable fact that a faster body catches a slower one to prove that spatial parts must be finite.

Core Idea
Infinite parts would make catching up impossible; since catching up happens, parts are finite.

Al-Razi continues his series of proofs for the finitude of spatial parts by invoking a logical argument reminiscent of Zeno's paradoxes. He asks: if a distance truly had infinite sections, how could a faster object ever overtake a slower one? The observed fact that it does overtake shows the sections must be finite. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then... But since... therefore...'.
2
Trace the logical conditional
Identify the assumption (infinite sections), the impossibility it entails (fast cannot catch slow), and the observed fact (fast does catch). See how the conclusion follows.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the role of observation
Consider why al-Razi relies on empirical observation of catching up. How does this strengthen his argument?
Observation provides the undeniable premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كانت المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة غير متناهية، لامتنع أن يصل السريع إلى البطيء ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا ابتدأ السريع بعده فإن وصل السريع إلى الموضع الذي وصل إليه البطيء، يكون البطيء قد قطع بعض الأجزاء وإلى أن يصل السريع أيضا إلى ذلك الموضع الثاني يكون قد وصل البطيء إلى موضع ثالث فلو حصل في الجسم مقاطع غير متناهية لكان كلما وصل السريع إلى موضع، يكون البطيء قد تعداه . وكان يجب أن لا يصل السريع إلى البطيء وحيث يصل إليه، علمنا المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية.
Third: If the sections occurring in the distance were infinite, then it would be impossible for the fast to reach the slow. That is because if the fast starts after it, then when the fast reaches the position the slow has reached, the slow will have crossed some parts; and before the fast reaches that second position, the slow will have reached a third position. So if there existed in the body infinite sections, then whenever the fast reaches a position, the slow would have passed it. And it would be necessary that the fast does not reach the slow. But since it does reach it, we know that the sections occurring in the distance are finite.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage contains another proof for the finitude of spatial parts, based on the famous 'Achilles and the tortoise' paradox. Al-Razi argues: if the distance from start to the point where the faster catches the slower contained infinite sections, then the slower would always remain ahead, because before the faster reaches any position the slower has already left, the slower moves further. Since we observe that the faster does catch up, the premise of infinite sections must be false. Hence, the sections (al-maqati') are finite.

Key Terms
مقاطع غير متناهية
السريع
البطيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi is arguing that motion is impossible, like Zeno's paradox.
Correction: Al-Razi uses the fact that motion occurs (fast overtakes slow) to prove that spatial parts are finite, not to deny motion.

Checkpoint
What phenomenon does al-Razi use to disprove infinite parts in this third argument?
A) A fast runner overtaking a slow one
B) A shadow shrinking as the sun rises
C) A line composed of indivisible points
D) The impossibility of motion without void

Key Takeaway
Because fast bodies do catch slow ones, distance cannot have infinite sections; hence spatial parts are finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a faster car overtake a slower one, do you ever pause to realize that this simple event would be impossible if the space between them had infinite parts? How does this make you appreciate the orderliness of Allah's creation in everyday moments?
?
This argument shows that accepting an unsupported premise (infinite parts) leads to a conclusion contradicting clear observation. In your own life, what comfortable but unexamined belief might be preventing you from seeing the truth? Are you willing to examine it today?
?
If the universe had no Creator and were eternal with infinite past events, then countless acts of injustice or suffering would have occurred without any ultimate justice. Based on the logical pattern in this lesson, does your experience of accountability and hope for justice point to a finite, created world? How does that change your sense of urgency in seeking the Creator?

Philosophers' First Proof: The Six Sides Argument
Philosophers' first version: six sides imply composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument for infinite imaginary division based on the distinct sides of an extended object.

Core Idea
Anything extended has distinct sides, so it is composite.

Al-Razi begins presenting the philosophers' case for infinite divisibility. Their first proof uses a simple observation: any object that occupies space has distinguishable directions. If it has distinct parts, it cannot be an indivisible atom. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
2
Identify the premise
Notice the key claim: any extended object (mutahayyiz) has six distinct directional sides. This is presented as self-evident.
The sides are right/left, front/back, above/below.
3
Derive the conclusion
If an object has distinct sides, it must have parts corresponding to those sides. Therefore it is divisible, not an indivisible atom.
This directly refutes the idea of an indivisible atom (jawhar fard).
4
Connect to imaginary division
The philosophers argue that this division can be continued infinitely in the mind, leading to infinite divisibility.
The term 'imaginary division' (al-qisma al-wahmiyya) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد ادعوا أولا أن القسمة الوهمية غير متناهية. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن كل ما كان متحيزا مختصا بجهة فإن يمينه غير يساره، وقدامه غير خلفه وفوقه غير تحته وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو مركب من هذه الجوانب الستة، فلا يكون فردا ، بل يكون منقسما وربما عبروا عن هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى . أنا لو فرضنا خطا مركبًا من ثلاثة جواهر متماسة، فالوسط يلاقي ما على يمينه يلاقي ما على يساره أو بغيره؟ والأول باطل، وإلا لكان كل واحد من الطرفين. ملاقيا كلية ذات الوسط. وهذا لا يكون ملاقاة بل يكون مداخلة، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد من الطرفين مداخلا بكليته في كلية ذات الوسط وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون مجموع الأجزاء الثلاثة أزيد في المقدار من الجزء الواحد. وحينئذ لا يكون تألف هذه الأجزاء سببا لازدياد القدر والحجم، ولا تكون الأعظام متألفة وكل ذلك باطل . ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن الوسط شيء يماس ما على يمينه بغير من تركبها . الجانب الذي به يماس ما على يساره. وإذا كان كذلك كان الجوهر الفرد منقسما.
As for the philosophers, they first claimed that imaginary division is infinite. They argued for it in several ways: The first argument: that everything which is extended and occupies direction, its right is not its left, its front is not its back, its above is not its below. Everything that is thus is composed of these six sides, so it is not an indivisible unit but is divisible. They sometimes express this statement in another expression: If we suppose a line composed of three contiguous atoms, does the middle one meet what is on its right by that by which it meets what is on its left, or by something else? The first is false, otherwise each of the two ends would be meeting the whole of the middle's essence. This would not be contact but interpenetration. Then each of the two ends would be entirely interpenetrating the whole of the middle's essence, and on this supposition the total of the three parts would not exceed a single part in magnitude. Then the composition of these parts would not cause an increase in size and volume, and bones would not be composed — all of which is false. And when this division fails, it is established that the middle is something that touches what is on its right by what it is composed of [؟] the side by which it touches what is on its left. And if so, then the indivisible atom is divisible.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the first of six proofs by the philosophers (falāsifa) for the claim that imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya) is infinite — i.e., that matter is infinitely divisible. The argument has two versions. The first version: any extended object has distinct right/left, front/back, top/bottom, so it is composed of these directions and hence divisible, not an indivisible unit (fard). The second version imagines a line of three contiguous atoms: the middle atom touches its neighbors. If it touches both with the same side, then both neighbors would be in contact with the whole middle, implying interpenetration (mudākhala) and thus no increase in size from composition, which is false. Therefore the middle must touch each neighbor with a different part of itself, proving that the supposedly indivisible atom is divisible. Hence infinite divisibility holds.

Key Terms
القسمة الوهمية
الجوهر الفرد
متحيز
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about physically cutting an object.
Correction: The argument is about imaginary division in the mind, not physical division.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the six sides argument?
A) Any object has six sides.
B) Any extended object has distinguishable directions.
C) Any object can be divided physically.
D) Imaginary division is finite.

Key Takeaway
The existence of distinct directions in any object shows it is composite, not an indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body occupies space and has distinct sides: right, left, front, back, above, below. Do you truly live as if you are a composite being—made of parts that can be separated? Or do you act as if you are an indivisible whole, ignoring your own fragility? Today, what one concrete step can you take to remember that you are not permanent?
?
The philosophers argued that anything extended is divisible. You are extended. Where in your character do you cling to indivisibility—refusing to admit you have conflicting desires or beliefs? Can you name one internal contradiction you have been avoiding? How might admitting it be the first step toward real change?
?
If your very existence is built on parts that can be taken apart, what does that mean for how you spend the next 24 hours? Are there attachments to status, possessions, or opinions that will collapse when you are 'divided'? What would it look like to hold them loosely, recognizing your own composition?

Philosophers' First Proof: The Three Atoms Argument
Philosophers' second version: three atoms and contact problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second version of the philosophers' first proof using three contiguous atoms to demonstrate divisibility.

Core Idea
If atoms touch, they must do so with distinct parts, proving divisibility.

The philosophers offer a more technical version of their first argument. They imagine three atoms in a line and examine how they touch. The conclusion forces us to accept that even the supposedly indivisible atoms have distinguishable parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the question posed about the middle atom.
2
Understand the scenario
Imagine three atoms (jawhar fard) in a line, each touching the next. Focus on the middle atom.
The atoms are assumed to be indivisible units.
3
Follow the reductio
Assume the middle atom touches both neighbors with the same side. Then both neighbors would interpenetrate it, meaning no increase in size from composition. This is absurd.
The absurdity is that bones (composed of atoms) would not be larger than a single atom.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore the middle atom must touch each neighbor with a different part, so it has parts, hence divisible.
Thus the atom is not truly indivisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد ادعوا أولا أن القسمة الوهمية غير متناهية. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن كل ما كان متحيزا مختصا بجهة فإن يمينه غير يساره، وقدامه غير خلفه وفوقه غير تحته وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو مركب من هذه الجوانب الستة، فلا يكون فردا ، بل يكون منقسما وربما عبروا عن هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى . أنا لو فرضنا خطا مركبًا من ثلاثة جواهر متماسة، فالوسط يلاقي ما على يمينه يلاقي ما على يساره أو بغيره؟ والأول باطل، وإلا لكان كل واحد من الطرفين. ملاقيا كلية ذات الوسط. وهذا لا يكون ملاقاة بل يكون مداخلة، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد من الطرفين مداخلا بكليته في كلية ذات الوسط وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون مجموع الأجزاء الثلاثة أزيد في المقدار من الجزء الواحد. وحينئذ لا يكون تألف هذه الأجزاء سببا لازدياد القدر والحجم، ولا تكون الأعظام متألفة وكل ذلك باطل . ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن الوسط شيء يماس ما على يمينه بغير من تركبها . الجانب الذي به يماس ما على يساره. وإذا كان كذلك كان الجوهر الفرد منقسما.
As for the philosophers, they first claimed that imaginary division is infinite. They argued for it in several ways: The first argument: that everything which is extended and occupies direction, its right is not its left, its front is not its back, its above is not its below. Everything that is thus is composed of these six sides, so it is not an indivisible unit but is divisible. They sometimes express this statement in another expression: If we suppose a line composed of three contiguous atoms, does the middle one meet what is on its right by that by which it meets what is on its left, or by something else? The first is false, otherwise each of the two ends would be meeting the whole of the middle's essence. This would not be contact but interpenetration. Then each of the two ends would be entirely interpenetrating the whole of the middle's essence, and on this supposition the total of the three parts would not exceed a single part in magnitude. Then the composition of these parts would not cause an increase in size and volume, and bones would not be composed — all of which is false. And when this division fails, it is established that the middle is something that touches what is on its right by what it is composed of [؟] the side by which it touches what is on its left. And if so, then the indivisible atom is divisible.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the first of six proofs by the philosophers (falāsifa) for the claim that imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya) is infinite — i.e., that matter is infinitely divisible. The argument has two versions. The first version: any extended object has distinct right/left, front/back, top/bottom, so it is composed of these directions and hence divisible, not an indivisible unit (fard). The second version imagines a line of three contiguous atoms: the middle atom touches its neighbors. If it touches both with the same side, then both neighbors would be in contact with the whole middle, implying interpenetration (mudākhala) and thus no increase in size from composition, which is false. Therefore the middle must touch each neighbor with a different part of itself, proving that the supposedly indivisible atom is divisible. Hence infinite divisibility holds.

Key Terms
القسمة الوهمية
الجوهر الفرد
ملاقاة
مداخلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves physical indivisibility is false.
Correction: The argument proves that even in thought, an atom must have parts, so it is divisible.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if the middle atom touches both neighbors with the same part?
A) The atoms would repel each other.
B) The three atoms would be larger than one atom.
C) There would be interpenetration and no increase in size.
D) The atoms would not be in contact.

Key Takeaway
If atoms touch, they must do so with distinct parts, proving they are divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the three atoms in your mind, do you find yourself forced to accept that the middle atom must have parts? What does that tell you about the limits of your imagination and the certainty of rational arguments?
?
If the philosophers' argument shows that even the smallest unit of matter must be divisible, what does that imply about your own reliance on physical reasoning when matters of faith arise? Are you willing to let a logical proof overturn a common-sense assumption you hold?
?
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to force a conclusion. Can you think of a belief you hold that you have never subjected to such rigorous logical testing? What would it take for you to abandon that belief if reason demanded it?

Philosophers' Light Argument for Divisibility
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' argument using light and shadow on a surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers use the analogy of light falling on a surface to argue that atoms must be divisible.

Core Idea
Light reveals the sides of an atom, proving it has parts and is divisible.

Imam al-Razi presents the second proof for infinite divisibility, this time using a thought experiment about light falling on a surface. The argument hinges on the fact that if one side of an atom is illuminated and the other is not, the atom must have distinct sides—meaning it has spatial extension and is therefore divisible. Read the passage carefully, noting the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with awareness of OCR issues
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the problematic phrase 'لا وجهي يصير مضيئا' which likely means 'the other face does not become illuminated'. The translation clarifies the intended meaning.
The source has high OCR risk; the argument is still clear.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Imagine a surface made of indivisible atoms (jawahir la tatajaza). Light falls on one side of this surface.
3
Identify the key distinction
Notice that one side (face) of the surface becomes illuminated while the other side does not. This implies that each atom must have two distinct sides: one illuminated, one not.
Illuminated and non-illuminated are different attributes.
4
Derive the conclusion
Since each atom has two distinct sides with different states, it must have spatial parts—i.e., it is divisible. This contradicts the assumption of indivisibility.
Therefore, infinite divisibility is supported.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider how the argument moves from a physical observation (light and shadow) to a metaphysical conclusion about the nature of atoms.
This is a classic example of philosophical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : أنا إذا فرضنا سطحا مركبا من جواهر لا تتجزأ فإذا وقع الضوء على أحد ذلك السطح، صار ذلك الوجه مضيئا والوجه الآخر منه لا وجهي يصير مضيئا والمضيء مغاير لما ليس بمضيء فكل واحد من تلك الجواهر التي منها تركب ذلك السطح يكون أحد وجهيه مضيئا والآخر غير مضيء، فيكون كل واحد منهما منقسما.
The second argument for them: If we suppose a surface composed of indivisible atoms, and if light falls on one side of that surface, that side becomes illuminated and the other side of it no [؟] face becomes illuminated, and the illuminated is other than what is not illuminated. So each one of those atoms of which that surface is composed has one of its sides illuminated and the other not illuminated; thus each of them is divisible.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This is the second proof for infinite divisibility. It assumes a surface made of indivisible atoms. When light falls on one side of the surface, one side of an atom (one face) becomes illuminated while the opposite face remains dark. Since illuminated and non-illuminated are distinct attributes, the atom must have two distinct sides or parts; therefore it is divisible, contradicting indivisibility.

Key Terms
جواهر لا تتجزأ
الضوء
مضيء
غير مضيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that illumination applies to the whole atom uniformly, so the atom cannot have parts.
Correction: The argument shows that illumination is not uniform: one side is lit, the other is not. This difference proves the atom has distinct spatial sides, hence parts.

Checkpoint
What does the light-on-surface argument demonstrate about the atom?
A) The atom is impenetrable.
B) The atom has distinct parts because one side is illuminated and the other is not.
C) Light cannot fall on an indivisible atom.
D) The atom must be spherical.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' second proof argues that atoms must be divisible because they have distinct sides with different states, as demonstrated by light and shadow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument shows that an atom can have two different states on its sides. Consider your own soul: are there sides of your character that are illuminated by faith while others remain in darkness? Which side will you work on turning towards Allah's light today?

Philosophers' Third Proof: Atoms and Empty Space
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' argument using movement of atoms towards an empty space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' third argument for infinite divisibility, which uses atoms moving into empty space to force the division of a middle atom.

Core Idea
Moving atoms in empty space forces indivisible atoms to have halves.

Al-Razi presents the philosophers' third proof, a thought experiment designed to show that even the smallest atom must be divisible. The scenario involves atoms moving into an empty space and making contact with a middle atom, which logically demands that the middle atom has distinct parts. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read and visualize the scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Then imagine a line of three touching atoms, with two additional atoms placed at the ends, leaving an empty space above the middle atom.
The empty space is crucial for the proof to work.
2
Understand the motion and contact
The proof supposes the two outer atoms move toward the middle. Since the space above the middle is empty, each outer atom can move and will touch half of the middle atom.
This contact implies the middle atom has two distinct halves.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Consider the potential objection that motion might not be possible in this scenario. Note how Al-Razi replies: each atom is capable of motion, and the target space is empty, so motion is certain.
The objection is preemptively answered, strengthening the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : أنا إذا ركبنا خطا من ثلاثة أجزاء متماسة، ووضعنا على طرفي هذا الخط جزأين، فعلى هذا التقدير بقي ما فوق الجزء الوسطاني خاليا ، فإذا فرضنا أن الجوهرين الموضوعين على طرفي الخط تحركا إلى الوسط لزم أن يصير كل واحد منهما مماسا لنصف الجوهر الوسطاني. وذلك القسمة. يوجب ولا يقال : ما الدليل على أن حركتهما ممكنة في هذه الصورة؟ لأنا نقول: الجزءان كل واحد منهما قابل للحركة وما فوق الجزء الوسطاني فارغ وإذا كان الشيء قابلا للحركة وكان المتحرك إليه فارغا كانت الحركة ممكنة قطعا.
The third argument for them: If we compose a line of three contiguous parts, and we place two atoms on the two ends of this line, then on this supposition what is above the middle atom remains empty. So if we suppose the two atoms placed on the ends of the line move toward the middle, it necessarily follows that each of them becomes tangent to half of the middle atom. And that necessitates division. And it cannot be said: what is the proof that their motion is possible in this scenario? For we say: each of the two atoms is capable of motion, and what is above the middle atom is empty; and if something is capable of motion and the place toward which it moves is empty, motion is possible without doubt.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This is the third proof. Consider a line of three contiguous atoms. Place two additional atoms on the two ends of this line (these two are presumably not contiguous with the middle atom but above it, leaving an empty space above the middle). If these two atoms on the ends move toward the middle, they will each touch half of the middle atom. Since they come into contact with different halves, the middle atom must have distinct halves, proving it is divisible. The proof anticipates an objection: how do we know the motion is possible? The reply: each atom is movable, and the space above the middle is empty (void), so motion is possible.

Key Terms
متماسة
الجزء الوسطاني
مماس
فارغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is impossible in this scenario because the atoms might be blocked or motion might not be possible in empty space.
Correction: The proof explicitly states that each atom is capable of motion, and since the space toward which it moves is empty, there is no impediment. Motion is therefore possible without doubt.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what do the two moving atoms each contact?
A) The entire middle atom
B) Half of the middle atom
C) The side of the middle atom only
D) Nothing because motion is impossible

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' third proof argues that if atoms can move into empty space and contact halves of a middle atom, then atoms must be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers assumed that motion into empty space is 'possible without doubt.' Is there any belief you hold with similar certainty? How would you test that certainty to see if it is as solid as you think?
?
The thought experiment forced a logical conclusion that contradicts the intuition that atoms are indivisible. Consider a habit or characteristic you believe is unchangeable (like 'I am not a morning person' or 'I always procrastinate'). What would it take for you to 'move' and see if that belief is actually divisible—i.e., that you could change it?

Philosophers' Fourth Proof for Infinite Divisibility
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' argument using aligned motion of two atoms on a four-atom line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' fourth proof for infinite divisibility and how the alignment of moving atoms at a specific junction implies division.

Core Idea
The meeting point reveals division.

In this proof, the philosophers propose a thought experiment: two atoms moving from opposite ends of a four-atom line meet at the boundary between the second and third parts. This alignment at a junction implies that the atoms have distinct positions and thus are divisible. Al-Razi presents this as an argument against atomism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the supposition: a line of four parts, an atom above the right end and another below the left end.
The line represents a continuum of atoms.
2
Follow the motion
Both atoms begin moving simultaneously and end together. Focus on what 'ending together' means for their positions.
They meet somewhere along the line.
3
Grasp the alignment condition
Alignment (muḥādhāt) only occurs when each atom falls on the connection (junction) between the second and third parts. This specific point forces division.
The junction is not an atom but a boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لهم : أنا إذا فرضنا خطا مركبًا من أربعة أجزاء، ووضعنا فوق طرفه الأيمن جزءًا، وتحت طرفه الأيسر جزءًا ،آخر وفرضنا أن هذين الجزأين، ابتدأ بالحركة معا، وانتهيا معا فمن الضرورة أن كل واحد منهما لما بالآخر، فقد تحاذيا . مر والمحاذاة لا تحصل إلا عند وقوع كل واحد منهما على متصل الثاني والثالث كان الأمر كذلك، كانت القسمة لازمة. من
The fourth argument for them: If we suppose a line composed of four parts, and we place above its right end an atom and below its left end another atom, and we suppose that these two atoms began motion together and ended together, then necessarily each of them when [؟] to the other, they have aligned. It passed, and alignment does not occur except when each of them falls on the connection of the second and third [parts]. If it is so, then division is necessary.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This is the fourth proof. Imagine a line of four atoms arranged in a row. Place an atom above the right end and another below the left end. Both move simultaneously toward the centre. They meet at the middle. The alignment (muḥādhāt) occurs only when each atom reaches the point between the second and third parts of the line. This point of contact implies that the atoms must have divisible parts, because the meeting point is at the junction of the second and third atoms, indicating that the atoms are not completely indivisible but have distinct positions that can align.

Key Terms
محاذاة
طرف
متصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two atoms meet exactly in the middle of the middle atom.
Correction: They meet at the junction between the second and third parts, not inside an atom. This shows the atoms have distinct boundaries.

Checkpoint
Where does alignment occur according to the fourth proof?
A) At the exact centre of the middle atom.
B) At the joint between the second and third parts of the line.
C) At the right end of the line.
D) At the left end of the line.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof uses the necessity of alignment at a specific junction to argue that atoms must have divisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your own understanding of faith or science are you merely repeating received ideas rather than reasoning through them? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?

When was the last time you genuinely entertained a view you disagreed with, seeking to understand its strongest form? Your spiritual growth may depend on that discipline.
?

Are you open to the possibility that your current mental model is incomplete? What would it take for you to revise it?

The Diagonal Argument Against Atomism
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' argument using a square and its diagonal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' geometrical argument that forces atoms to be divisible.

Core Idea
The diagonal of an atomist square forces atoms to be divisible.

This passage presents a clever geometrical proof against atomism. By constructing a square made of atoms and examining its diagonal, the philosophers show that the atomist assumption leads to geometric absurdities. The argument forces a choice: either atoms are divisible or geometry is false. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the atomist square model
Visualize a square made of four lines, each line made of four indivisible atoms, so the square has 16 atoms in a grid.
This is the assumed material composition.
2
Trace the diagonal construction
The diagonal takes one atom from each line: first from first line, second from second, etc. These diagonal atoms can either be contiguous (touching) or have gaps.
This sets up the dilemma.
3
Examine both possibilities
If contiguous, diagonal equals side (absurd). If not contiguous, gaps must be considered: either gap fits an atom (diagonal = 7 atoms, absurd) or gap is smaller than atom (implies divisible atoms).
Both lead to contradiction, so atoms must be divisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة لهم : نفرض مربعا مركبا من خطوط أربعة متماسة، وكل واحد تلك الخطوط يكون مركبا من أربعة أجزاء متماسة فيكون هذا المربع مركبا من ستة عشر جزءا، وقطره وهو الخط المركب من الجزء الأول من الخط الأول، والثاني من الثاني، والثالث من الثالث والرابع من الرابع فهذه الأجزاء إما أن تكون متلاقية أو غير متلاقية فإن كانت متلاقية لزم أن يكون القطر مساويا للضلع وهو محال . وإن كانت غير متلاقية فكل واحد من تلك الفرج، إما أن يكون بحيث يتسع لجزء لا يتجزأ وإما أن يكون أقل من ذلك والأول يقتضي أن يكون القطر في مقدار سبعة أجزاء والضلعان أيضا كذلك، فيكون القطر مساويًا لمجموع الضلعين وذلك محال وأما إن كانت كل واحدة تلك الفرج أقل من الجوهر الفرد، لزمته القسمة. من
The fifth argument for them: Let us suppose a square composed of four contiguous lines, and each of those lines is composed of four contiguous parts, so this square is composed of sixteen parts. Its diagonal is a line composed of the first part of the first line, the second of the second, the third of the third, and the fourth of the fourth. Now these parts are either contiguous or not contiguous. If they are contiguous, then the diagonal would be equal to the side, which is absurd. If they are not contiguous, then each of those gaps is either such that it can accommodate an indivisible atom or less than that. The first entails that the diagonal would measure seven parts, and the sides also likewise, so the diagonal would equal the sum of the two sides, which is absurd. And if each of those gaps is less than the indivisible atom, then division is necessary.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This is the fifth proof using a square grid of atoms. Suppose a square made of four lines each of four atoms, total 16 atoms. The diagonal line goes through one atom from each line (first of first line, second of second, etc.). These four diagonal atoms either touch each other or have gaps. If they touch, the diagonal length equals the side length (4 atoms), which is false because the diagonal of a square is longer. If they do not touch, there are gaps. If each gap can fit an atom, then the diagonal would be 7 atoms (4 diagonal atoms + 3 gaps filled), still absurd; if the gaps are smaller than an atom, the atoms must have parts to account for the gap sizes. Hence atoms are divisible.

Key Terms
مربع
قطر
ضلع
فرج
متلاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indivisible atoms can be arranged without any geometric problems.
Correction: The diagonal argument shows that any arrangement of indivisible atoms leads to geometric contradictions, proving atoms must have parts.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, what is the consequence if the diagonal atoms are contiguous?
A) The diagonal equals the side in length.
B) The diagonal is longer than the side.
C) The atoms must have gaps.
D) The diagonal becomes curved.

Key Takeaway
The diagonal argument shows that atomism leads to geometric contradictions, so atoms must be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The diagonal argument shows that a seemingly intuitive belief (atomism) leads to logical contradictions. What is one belief about Allah or your faith that you have never critically examined, but now feel compelled to test with the same rigorous reasoning?
?
After seeing how al-Razi uses geometry to demolish an assumption, do you trust your own ability to reason about theological matters? If not, what will you do to sharpen that tool?

Third Proof for the Indivisible Atom: The Three-Part Line Argument
Razi presents the third proof for the indivisible atom using a line of three parts and two moving bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the indivisible atom used by the mutakallimūn, which relies on a line of three parts and the movement of two substances to force division.

Core Idea
Even minimal movement forces division of the indivisible.

Imam Razi presents a clever thought experiment that forces the opponent to accept the division of the supposed indivisible atom. By constructing a line of three contiguous atoms and moving the end atoms toward the middle, the middle atom must be split. This proof strengthens the atomist stance against those who deny indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof setup
Read the first part of the passage where the line of three parts is composed and the atoms are placed at the ends.
Notice the assumption of contiguity.
2
Visualize the movement
Imagine the two end-atoms moving toward the middle. What must happen? The passage says each becomes tangent to half the middle atom.
This implies the middle atom is divided into two halves.
3
Consider the objection and response
Read the objection: why is movement possible here? The response explains that both atoms are movable and the destination is empty.
This addresses a potential gap in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this proof forces division of the middle atom, supporting the existence of indivisible parts.
This is the third proof, so it adds to the cumulative case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : أنا إذا ركبنا خطا من ثلاثة أجزاء متماسة، ووضعنا على طرفي هذا الخط جزأين، فعلى هذا التقدير بقي ما فوق الجزء الوسطاني خاليا ، فإذا فرضنا أن الجوهرين الموضوعين على طرفي الخط تحركا إلى الوسط لزم أن يصير كل واحد منهما مماسا لنصف الجوهر الوسطاني. وذلك يوجب القسمة. ولا يقال : ما الدليل على أن حركتهما ممكنة في هذه الصورة؟ لأنا نقول: الجزءان كل واحد منهما قابل للحركة وما فوق الجزء الوسطاني فارغ وإذا كان الشيء قابلا للحركة وكان المتحرك إليه فارغا كانت الحركة ممكنة قطعا.
The third proof for them: If we compose a line of three contiguous parts and place two atoms at the two ends of this line, then on this assumption what is above the middle part remains empty. If we suppose that the two substances placed at the ends of the line move toward the middle, it would necessitate that each of them becomes tangent to half of the middle substance. And that necessitates division. And it is not said: what is the evidence that their movement is possible in this situation? For we say: the two parts, each of them is capable of movement, and what is above the middle part is empty. And if something is capable of movement and that toward which it moves is empty, the movement is definitely possible.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-hujja al-thālitha) used by the mutakallimūn (theologians) to argue for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā). The argument proceeds as follows: (1) Imagine a line composed of three contiguous parts (e.g., three indivisible atoms). (2) Place one atom at each end of the line. (3) The space above the middle atom is empty. (4) Suppose both end-atoms move toward the middle. (5) Each end-atom will necessarily come into contact with half of the middle atom — that is, the middle atom is divided. (6) The opponent might ask: why is such movement possible? The answer: each end-atom is capable of movement (since it is a substance), and the destination (above the middle atom) is empty. When a movable object has an empty destination, movement is absolutely possible. Thus the argument forces the conclusion that the middle atom is divisible, supporting the atomist position that bodies are composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
جوهر فرد
خط
قسمة
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes movement is possible without justification.
Correction: The proof explicitly argues that each atom is capable of movement and the destination is empty, making movement possible.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does the movement of the two end-atoms toward the middle require?
A) The middle atom remains untouched
B) Each end-atom becomes tangent to half the middle atom
C) The line breaks apart
D) The end-atoms pass through each other

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that even in a minimal scenario, movement forces division, thus supporting the existence of indivisible atoms.

?

?
The proof forces the middle atom to divide. What in your own spiritual life is currently being 'divided' — your attention between dunya and akhira, your commitment between action and procrastination? Will you let that division clarify your priorities or just distract you?
?
Razi shows that even minimal movement forces a conclusion. What small, seemingly insignificant habit or thought pattern is moving you — towards or away from truth? Are you willing to let that movement reveal what you are made of?

Fourth Proof for the Indivisible Atom: The Four-Part Line Thought Experiment
Razi presents the fourth proof using a line of four parts and two bodies moving from opposite ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's fourth proof for the indivisible atom uses a thought experiment involving two bodies moving along a four-part line to demonstrate the necessity of discrete parts.

Core Idea
Alignment of moving atoms forces the line to be composed of indivisible parts.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's fourth proof, which employs a vivid mental model: a line of four parts and two atoms moving from opposite ends. This seemingly simple setup leads to a powerful conclusion that matter must be composed of indivisible atoms. By working through the steps, you will see why atomists argued that without discrete parts, alignment becomes impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: hypothesis, setup, motion, alignment, conclusion.
Pay attention to the term 'muḥādhāt' (alignment) as it is key.
2
Visualize the setup
Imagine a horizontal line made of four adjacent atoms. Place one atom above the right end and another below the left end. Both atoms start moving towards each other simultaneously and finish at the same time.
This mental picture is crucial for understanding the proof.
3
Understand the definition of alignment
Alignment (muḥādhāt) occurs when each moving atom falls exactly on the contiguous part of the second and third atoms of the original line. This requires precise correspondence.
The definition is explicitly given in the passage.
4
Connect alignment to divisibility
Razi argues that such alignment is only possible if the line is composed of discrete, indivisible parts (atoms). Otherwise, alignment could not be exactly defined.
This is the core inference of the proof.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
Consider how this thought experiment supports the broader atomist doctrine. It shows that everyday motion implies discrete building blocks of matter.
This proof is one of many used in kalam to defend atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لهم : أنا إذا فرضنا خطا مركبًا من أربعة أجزاء، ووضعنا فوق طرفه الأيمن جزءًا، وتحت طرفه الأيسر جزءًا آخر، وفرضنا أن هذين الجزأين ابتدآ بالحركة معا، وانتهيا معا، فمن الضرورة أن كل واحد منهما إذا مر بالآخر فقد تحاذيا، والمحاذاة لا تحصل إلا عند وقوع كل واحد منهما على متصل الثاني والثالث، فمتى كان الأمر كذلك، كانت القسمة لازمة.
The fourth proof for them: If we suppose a line composed of four parts, and we place a part above its right end and another part below its left end, and we suppose that these two parts begin moving together and end together, then necessarily each of them, when it passes the other, they become aligned (muḥādhāt). And alignment only occurs when each of them falls upon the contiguous [part] of the second and third. So whenever the situation is like that, division is necessary.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rābiʿa) for the atomist position. The set-up: (1) A line consisting of four contiguous parts (atoms). (2) Above the right end-place one atom; below the left end-place another atom. (3) Both atoms begin moving simultaneously and finish simultaneously toward each other. (4) Necessarily, each atom will pass the other, and at the moment of passing they become aligned (muḥādhāt). (5) Alignment (muḥādhāt) is defined as each atom falling exactly on the contiguous part of the second and third atoms of the original line. That is, they occupy positions that are side by side relative to the line's parts. (6) This state forces division of the original line into parts that are precisely aligned with the moving atoms. The argument is that such alignment is impossible without the line being composed of discrete, indivisible parts that allow exact alignment. Thus, the existence of indivisible atoms is necessary.

Key Terms
خط مركب
أجزاء
محاذاة
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'alignment' is a trivial everyday concept that does not imply division.
Correction: In atomist theory, alignment requires exact correspondence with discrete parts; without indivisible units, alignment cannot be precisely defined.

Checkpoint
What condition does the fourth proof say is necessary for alignment (al-muḥādhāt) to occur?
A) The two bodies touch each other
B) Each body falls on the contiguous part of the second and third parts
C) The line is composed of an odd number of parts
D) The bodies move at different speeds

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof demonstrates that alignment of moving bodies on a line necessitates the line's division into discrete, indivisible parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof uses a line of four parts to argue for the existence of indivisible atoms. Consider the 'lines' in your life—the structures of your days, your relationships, your duties. Are they composed of discrete, intentional parts that you manage, or do they blur into a continuous, undifferentiated mess? How does the concept of indivisible parts challenge you to bring more discipline and clarity to your life?
?
The moment of alignment (muḥādhāt) in the proof is precise. Think about the alignment of your will with Allah's commands. Is there a moment in your daily life where you consciously align your actions with His pleasure? Or do you drift without such precise alignment? What specific step can you take today to ensure that your actions fall upon the 'contiguous' parts of obedience?

Fifth Proof: Square, Diagonal, and Indivisible Parts
Razi presents the fifth proof using a square of sixteen parts and its diagonal to show divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth proof using a square of sixteen indivisible parts and its diagonal to argue that the assumption of indivisible atoms leads to a conclusion about divisibility.

Core Idea
Geometry forces a contradiction unless we accept divisibility.

Razi presents a geometric argument that seems to expose a contradiction if one assumes indivisible atoms. By constructing a square from four rows of four atoms each, he examines the diagonal line and the gaps that must appear. The proof shows that unless you accept divisibility, you face an absurdity. This lesson walks through the steps of this classic kalam proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the construction
Read how the square is built: four lines each with four contiguous parts, totaling sixteen indivisible atoms. Visualize a 4x4 grid.
Each 'line' is a row of four atoms.
2
Follow the diagonal definition
The diagonal is a line through the first atom of the first row, second atom of second row, etc. Note that it passes through exactly four atoms.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
The diagonal's atoms either coincide with the grid (sit exactly on the same spots as side atoms) or do not. Each leads to a problem.
The proof examines both cases to show a contradiction.
4
Reach the conclusion
Both possibilities lead to impossibility unless we admit that the gaps are smaller than an atom, which forces division. The proof aims to show that indivisible atoms cannot be consistently maintained.
The final phrase 'division follows' means the necessity of admitting parts (atomism) or divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة لهم : نفرض مربعا مركبا من خطوط أربعة متماسة، وكل واحد من تلك الخطوط يكون مركبا من أربعة أجزاء متماسة فيكون هذا المربع مركبا من ستة عشر جزءا، وقطره وهو الخط المركب من الجزء الأول من الخط الأول، والثاني من الثاني، والثالث من الثالث، والرابع من الرابع فهذه الأجزاء إما أن تكون متلاقية أو غير متلاقية فإن كانت متلاقية لزم أن يكون القطر مساويا للضلع وهو محال . وإن كانت غير متلاقية فكل واحد من تلك الفرج، إما أن يكون بحيث يتسع لجزء لا يتجزأ وإما أن يكون أقل من ذلك والأول يقتضي أن يكون القطر في مقدار سبعة أجزاء والضلعان أيضا كذلك، فيكون القطر مساويًا لمجموع الضلعين وذلك محال وأما إن كانت كل واحدة من تلك الفرج أقل من الجوهر الفرد، لزمته القسمة.
The fifth proof for them: We suppose a square composed of four contiguous lines, and each of those lines is composed of four contiguous parts, so this square is composed of sixteen parts. Its diagonal is the line composed of the first part of the first line, the second of the second, the third of the third, and the fourth of the fourth. These parts are either coincident (mutalāqiya) or not coincident. If they are coincident, then the diagonal must be equal to the side, which is impossible. If they are not coincident, then each of those gaps (furaj) is either such that it can accommodate an indivisible part or less than that. The first implies that the diagonal measures seven parts, and the two sides also similarly, so the diagonal equals the sum of the two sides, which is impossible. And if each of those gaps is less than the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard), then division follows necessarily.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khāmisa). It uses the geometry of a square constructed from indivisible parts. (1) Consider a square whose side consists of 4 contiguous atoms; thus the square contains 4×4 = 16 atoms. (2) Its diagonal is defined as the line joining opposite corners, passing through atoms: first atom of the first row, second atom of the second row, third atom of the third, fourth atom of the fourth. (3) The diagonal's atoms may either coincide with the square's grid (i.e., occupy the exact same spaces as some of the side atoms) or not. (4) If they coincide, the diagonal length equals the side length (4 atoms), which is false because the diagonal of a square is longer than the side. (5) If they do not coincide, then there will be gaps (furaj) between the diagonal atoms and the side atoms. Two possibilities for each gap: (a) it can exactly fit one indivisible atom, or (b) it is smaller than an atom. (6) If it fits one atom, then the diagonal would have 7 atoms (since there are 4 diagonal atoms and 3 gaps of one atom each between them), and the two sides together also total 8 atoms (4+4), so the diagonal equals the sum of the two sides (7 ≈ 8? Actually it says 'equal to the sum of two sides' but that's impossible geometrically). (7) If each gap is less than an atom, then that gap itself is divisible (since it is part of space that can be divided), leading to the conclusion that the atom is divisible. Hence the assumption of indivisible atoms leads to contradictions unless one accepts the divisibility of the atom — but the proof is meant to show that the opposite (i.e., infinite divisibility) leads to similar problems? Actually this is a classic argument for atomism: it tries to show that if there are no atoms, then the diagonal would be incommensurate; here it shows that assuming atoms leads to contradictions unless you allow partless atoms? Need to read carefully: It says 'if each gap is less than the individual substance (jawhar fard), then division is necessary' meaning that the gap itself is divisible, hence the atom (the smallest unit) is divisible. So the argument is meant to refute atomism? Actually this is from the mutakallimun's opponents? Wait: The book is Al-Arbaeen by Razi, who is a theologian but also a philosopher. He is presenting arguments and then refuting them? The context: These are 'hujaj lahum' meaning proofs for them (the opponents? Or for the mutakallimun?). In the previous lesson, it said 'الحجة الثالثة لهم' meaning the third proof for them (the mutakallimun). So these are arguments for atomism. This particular argument seems to lead to a contradiction, implying that the atomist position leads to absurdity. But Razi might be presenting it as a proof by contradiction? Actually, it ends with 'لزمته القسمة' meaning division follows, which is what the atomist wants to prove (that things are divisible). So it's a valid argument for atomism. Need to interpret: If the gap is less than an atom, then that gap itself is divisible (since it has magnitude), and therefore the atom is divisible. So the argument shows that the assumption of indivisible atoms leads to the conclusion that they are divisible, hence a contradiction, so atomism is false. But the text says 'لزمته القسمة' which could mean 'division follows necessarily' meaning the atomist's claim of indivisibility is refuted? Actually it's ambiguous. In classical kalam, these arguments are often used to prove the existence of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza). So it's likely that Razi is presenting these as proofs for atomism, and the 'division' that follows means the division of space into atoms, not the division of the atom. The final phrase 'لزمته القسمة' might mean that the existence of indivisible parts is proven. Without more context, we stick to the text: 'division follows necessarily' as a conclusion from the impossibility of the other options. The commentary should reflect what is in the Arabic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'division follows' means the atom itself gets divided, implying atomism is false.
Correction: In kalam, 'division follows' often means that the existence of parts (atoms) is proven, i.e., the atom is shown to be divisible, which supports atomism by refuting infinite divisibility.

Checkpoint

B) The diagonal equals the sum of two sides

Key Takeaway
The geometric argument shows that assuming indivisible atoms leads to a logical contradiction unless one admits divisibility, thus supporting the doctrine of atomism (or refuting it, depending on interpretation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When the proof forced you to hold two impossible outcomes side by side, did you feel the tension? That tension mirrors the intellectual struggle of the mutakallimun. Can you recall a time when your faith or worldview faced a similar logical challenge? Did you resolve it by abandoning the premise, or by rethinking it?
?

The Sixth Proof: Shadow and Sun Argument
Razi presents the sixth proof using the shadow of a stick as the sun rises, to argue for divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof for the necessity of division (inqisām) using the shadow of a stick.

Core Idea
A changing shadow proves that an indivisible rise must cause a divisible decrease.

This proof uses a simple observation—a stick's shadow shortening as the sun rises—to argue for the necessity of division. Razi presents this as the sixth proof, employing a logical reductio to show that assuming indivisible parts leads to contradictions unless those parts are divisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a setup, two possibilities, and their consequences.
2
Identify the two possibilities
When the sun rises by one indivisible part (jawhar fard), the shadow either does not decrease at all, or it does decrease. These are the only options.
The first possibility is ruled out by a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Follow the reductio
If the shadow never decreases, repeating the same rise multiple times would keep the shadow unchanged even at noon, which is impossible. Thus the shadow must decrease.
4
Understand the conclusion
The decrease must be by less than one part for each rise; otherwise the shadow would be too long. This 'less than a part' implies that the part itself is divisible, proving division (inqisām) is necessary.
The conclusion is 'وذلك يوجب الانقسام' — that necessitates division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة لهم : إذا غرزنا خشبة في الأرض فعند طلوع الشمس يقع لها ظل طويل، ثم كلما ازدادت الشمس ارتفاعا ازداد ذلك الظل انتقاصا فإذا فرضنا أن الشمس ارتفعت بمقدار جوهر فرد فإما أن لا ينتقص البتة من الظل شيء، أو ينتقص، والأول باطل؛ إذ لو عقل أن ترتفع الشمس جوهرا فردا ولا ينتقص من الظل شيء، جاز أن ترتفع ثانيا وثالثا ولا ينتقص من الظل شيء، حتى تصل الشمس إلى وسط السماء، ويبقى الظل كما كان وهو محال، وأما أن ينتقص من الظل شيء فإما أن يقال: كل ما ارتفعت الشمس جزءا واحدا ينتقص من الظل جزء واحد فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون طول الظل مثل مدار ربع الشمس وهو محال أو يقال : كل ما ارتفعت الشمس جزءًا، ينتقص من الظل أقل من جزء وذلك يوجب الانقسام.
The sixth proof for them: If we plant a stick in the ground, then at sunrise it has a long shadow. Then whenever the sun rises higher, the shadow decreases. If we suppose that the sun rises by the amount of one individual substance (jawhar fard), then either the shadow does not decrease at all, or it decreases. The first is false because if it could be conceived that the sun rises by one individual substance and the shadow does not decrease at all, then it could rise a second and third time with no decrease, until the sun reaches the zenith while the shadow remains as it was – which is impossible. As for the shadow decreasing: either it is said that whenever the sun rises by one part, the shadow decreases by one part – then the length of the shadow must equal a quarter of the sun's orbit – which is impossible. Or it is said that whenever the sun rises by one part, the shadow decreases by less than a part – and that necessitates division.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-hujja al-sādisa). It uses a practical observation: a planted stick has a long shadow at sunrise; as the sun rises, the shadow shortens. (1) Assume the sun rises by a single indivisible part (jawhar fard). (2) Two possibilities for the shadow: either it does not decrease at all, or it does decrease. (3) If it does not decrease at all, then by repeating the same rise of one part multiple times, the sun could reach the zenith (mid-sky) while the shadow remains its original length – which is absurd (since at noon the shadow is minimal). (4) Therefore the shadow must decrease. (5) Now consider the decrease: either it decreases by exactly one part for each one-part rise of the sun, or by less than one part. (6) If it decreases by exactly one part, then the total shadow length would be proportional to the sun's orbit – specifically, the shadow would be a quarter of the sun's orbit (based on geometry), which is impossible (because shadow length is not that large). (7) Therefore the decrease must be by less than one part for each one-part rise. (8) But 'less than one part' implies that the part is divisible, because the decrease is a fraction of a part. That is, the decrease itself is a magnitude smaller than an indivisible part, proving that the indivisible part can be divided. Thus division (inqisām) is necessary, supporting the atomist claim that matter is composed of indivisible parts, but paradoxically forcing division? Actually it supports the idea that the indivisible part is not truly indivisible, or that space is infinitely divisible. However, the text ends with 'وذلك يوجب الانقسام' meaning that necessitates division (of the part). So this is a proof for the existence of divisibility, hence against atomism. But since Razi is presenting these as proofs 'for them' (the mutakallimun), it may be that the mutakallimun use this to argue that the atom is indeed divisible? That would be contradictory. Actually in classical kalam, the mutakallimun hold that the atom is indivisible. So these arguments are likely presented as objections to atomism, or as proofs that the world must be composed of atoms? I think the intended conclusion is that the atom exists, and the shadow decreasing by less than a part shows that the part (the indivisible quantity) is not actually a unit? Hmm. Without full context, we stick to the literal: 'that necessitates division'. In proper kalam, division (inqisām) here means that the shadow's decrease shows that the indivisible part is not the smallest measure, i.e., that space is infinitely divisible. So it's an argument against atomism. Given the ambiguity, we keep the text and let commentary explain the logical steps.

Key Terms
ظل
ارتفاع الشمس
جوهر فرد
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof is about actual shadows and sun movement, not about logical divisibility.
Correction: The proof is a logical argument using a physical example to show that if space is composed of indivisible parts, contradictions arise; the conclusion is about the necessity of division (inqisām), not astronomy.

Checkpoint
In the sixth proof, what does the decrease of the shadow by less than one part for each one-part rise of the sun imply?
A) The sun moves faster
B) The shadow never disappears
C) Division (inqisām) is necessary
D) The stick is not straight

Key Takeaway
The decrease of the shadow by less than a part proves that the indivisible part is actually divisible, making division (inqisām) necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof concludes that nothing is truly indivisible; all parts are contingent and composed. Where in your life have you been acting as if something is self-sufficient or independent of Allah? How can you remind yourself today of your complete dependence on Him?
?
The argument forces you to admit that the smallest part of creation is maintained by Allah's precise decree. Do you live with this awareness, or do you function as if the world runs on autopilot? What practical step can you take to cultivate constant mindfulness of Allah's sustenance?
?
If every fraction of a shadow's decrease is a sign of Allah's perfect design, then your very existence at this moment is a continuous act of creation. Does this thought bring you closer to Allah in gratitude and submission, or does it unsettle you? Choose one specific moment today—eating, walking, or even sleeping—and intentionally reflect on Allah's ongoing creation of it.

The Seventh Proof: Sphere Rotation and the Impossibility of a Part Moving a Full Part
Razi presents the core sphere rotation argument leading to the conclusion that a part can move less than a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from celestial sphere rotation forces the conclusion that parts of space or body must be divisible, supporting atomist division.

Core Idea
The universe's motion reveals that indivisible parts cannot move in unison without contradiction.

Razi presents a clever argument based on the rotation of celestial spheres. If we consider the equator and a small circle near the pole, their different circumferences create a dilemma that can only be resolved by admitting that a part can move less than a full part—a conclusion that supports the possibility of division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the setup
Read the Arabic passage from 'الحجة السابعة لهم' to 'وهو المطلوب'. Notice the initial hypothesis: if the sphere rotates equatorially, all parallel circles rotate.
The key terms are منطقة (equator) and دائرة صغيرة (small circle).
2
Trace the two possible cases
The argument considers two possibilities when the equator moves by one part: either the small circle also moves by one part, or it does not move at all. Identify why each leads to a problem.
First case leads to contradiction because orbits would be equal; second leads to disintegration (تفكك).
3
Understand why both cases are rejected
Contradiction is impossible, and disintegration is rejected by philosophical proofs and the Quran describing spheres as 'seven strong ones'. Note the two reasons given.
Philosophical proofs against splitting (انخراق) and Quranic reference (سبعا شدادا).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining option is that the small circle moves by less than one part. This is 'المطلوب' – the desired conclusion that supports division of parts.
This conclusion is used by mutakallimun to argue for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة لهم : إذا استدار الفلك استدارة منطقية استدارت جميع الدوائر الموازية لتلك المنطقة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا تحركت المنطقة جزءا فالدائرة الصغيرة القريبة من القطب الموازية للمنطقة إن تحركت أيضا جزءًا لزم أن يكون مدار تلك الدائرة الصغيرة مساويا لمقدار المنطقة. هذا خلف. وإن لم تتحرك البتة، فحينئذ يلزم وقوع التفكك في أجزاء الفلك وذلك باطل أما الأول فلأن الدلائل الفلسفية قائمة على أن أجرام الفلك لا تقبل الانخراق والتفكيك وأما الثاني فلأن القرآن وصفها بكونها سبعا شدادا، وذلك ينافي وقوع التفكيك فيها فلم يبق إلا أن يقال: مهما تحركت المنطقة جزءا تحركت تلك الدائرة الصغيرة أقل من جزء وهو المطلوب. وهذا الكلام قد يفرضونه في حركة الرحى ويلزمون عليه : تفكك أجزاء الرحى والمتكلمون يلتزمونه ويقولون : إنه سبحانه وتعالى فاعل مختار فهو يفكك أجزاء الرحى حال استدارتها، ثم يعيد التأليف والتركيب إليها حال وقوفها. والفلاسفة يدفعون هذا من وجهين : الأول : الاستبعاد. والثاني : أن الإنسان لو ألصق عقبه بالأرض وأدار نفسه فحينئذ يلزم أن تتفكك أجزاء بدن الإنسان في تلك الحالة، ومن المعلوم أن الإنسان يعلم بالضرورة أنه في هذه الحالة بقيت أجزاء بدنه متلاصقة كما كانت قبل ذلك فبطل القول بالتفكيك.
The seventh proof for them: If the sphere rotates an equatorial rotation, all circles parallel to that region rotate. If you know this, we say: If the region (equator) moves by one part, then the small circle near the pole parallel to the region, if it also moves by one part, then the orbit of that small circle must equal the magnitude of the region. This is a contradiction. And if it does not move at all, then disintegration (tafakkuk) occurs in the parts of the sphere, and that is invalid. As for the first, because the philosophical proofs establish that celestial bodies do not accept splitting (inkhirāq) and disintegration. And as for the second, because the Quran described them as 'seven strong ones' (sab'an shidādā), and that contradicts the occurrence of disintegration in them. So nothing remains except to say: whenever the region moves by one part, that small circle moves by less than one part – and that is what is sought. And this argument they sometimes apply to the motion of a millstone (raḥā) and they conclude from it: the disintegration of the parts of the millstone. And the mutakallimūn commit to it and say: He, exalted and majestic, is a free agent, so He disintegrates the parts of the millstone while it rotates, then He restores composition and arrangement to them when it stops. And the philosophers refute this from two angles: first, implausibility (al-istib'ād). Second, that if a person sticks his heel to the ground and rotates himself, then disintegration of the parts of his body would be necessary in that state, yet it is known by necessity that in that state the parts of his body remain contiguous as they were before. Thus the claim of disintegration (tafakkuk) is invalidated.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-hujja al-sābi'a). It is a more extended argument involving celestial spheres and includes engagement with philosophical objections. The argument: (1) Consider a celestial sphere rotating equatorially (istiḍāra manṭiqiyya). (2) All circles parallel to the equator rotate. (3) If the equatorial region (al-minṭaqa) moves by one part (i.e., one indivisible unit), then a small circle near the pole that is parallel to the equator must also move. (4) Two possibilities: (a) The small circle also moves by one part, but then its orbit would equal that of the equator, which is false (since the small circle is much smaller). This is a contradiction (khalaf). (b) The small circle does not move at all, which would necessitate disintegration (tafakkuk) – parts of the sphere would separate or rip. (5) Both possibilities are invalid: (i) Philosophical arguments show celestial bodies cannot be split or disintegrated. (ii) The Quran describes them as 'seven strong ones' (sab'an shidādā), implying firmness, which prohibits disintegration. (6) Therefore, the only remaining option is that when the equator moves by one part, the small circle moves by less than one part. This proves that a part (the indivisible unit) can be subdivided – hence division is necessary, which is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) for the atomist position. (7) The argument is then applied to a millstone (raḥā): if the outer rim moves one part, the inner parts near the axle must move less, leading to disintegration. (8) The mutakallimūn accept this consequence: they argue that God is a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) who disintegrates the millstone's parts during rotation and then reassembles them when it stops. (9) The philosophers object in two ways: first, it is implausible (istib'ād). Second, a human rotating with heel on the ground would similarly require disintegration, but we know by necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) that the body parts remain contiguous. Hence the disintegration claim is refuted.

Key Terms
فلك
منطقة
دائرة صغيرة
تفكك
انخراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic description 'seven strong ones' (sab'an shidādā) is only about the strength of the spheres, not about their continuity.
Correction: In this argument, the phrase is used to argue that the spheres are firm and do not disintegrate, supporting the rejection of the tafakkuk option.

Checkpoint
In the seventh proof, what is the only remaining option to avoid contradiction and disintegration?
A) The small circle does not move
B) The small circle moves by one part
C) The small circle moves by less than one part
D) The sphere stops rotating

Key Takeaway
The sphere argument shows that the only way to avoid contradiction or disintegration is to accept that a part can move less than a full part, implying division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical dilemma that forces you to abandon a comfortable belief, do you stubbornly cling to the impossible or humbly accept the necessary truth? This proof demands you choose the evident conclusion even when it challenges your assumptions. What belief in your own life might you be avoiding because it requires such intellectual courage?
?
The proof unites philosophical reasoning and Quranic revelation as complementary evidences. Are you willing to let both guide your understanding, or do you often pit them against each other? How can this lesson inspire you to trust that truth is consistent across reason and scripture?
?
If you were in the place of the small circle—seemingly forced to either contradict reality or cause destruction—would you have the humility to accept the unexpected path of 'less than a part'? Reflect on a moment when you had to admit that a smaller step, a lesser compromise, was the only way to preserve integrity. What held you back from taking that step?

The Millstone Analogy and the Mutakallimūn's Defense of Tafakkuk
Razi extends the sphere argument to a millstone and records the mutakallimun's response and the philosophers' objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the millstone example illustrates the theory of tafakkuk and how the mutakallimūn justify it by appealing to God’s creative power.

Core Idea
God can disintegrate and reassemble matter at a moment’s notice, making tafakkuk possible.

The philosophers extend the sphere argument to a millstone, claiming that if the outer rim moves, the inner parts would have to disintegrate. The mutakallimūn, however, accept this consequence and argue that God, as a free agent, can dismantle and rebuild the millstone during each rotation, thus preserving the atomist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the millstone analogy
Read from 'وهذا الكلام قد يفرضونه' to the end. Notice that the philosophers apply the same logic to a millstone: the outer rim moving by one part would require the inner parts to disintegrate.
The millstone (رحى) is used as a familiar example.
2
Understand the mutakallimun's response
The mutakallimun accept the disintegration and argue that God, as a free agent (فاعل مختار), causes the parts to separate during rotation and then reassembles them when it stops.
This shows their willingness to accept seemingly absurd consequences for their theory.
3
Examine the philosophers' objections
The philosophers offer two objections: first, it is implausible (استبعاد). Second, a person spinning with heel on the ground would similarly require disintegration, but we know by necessary knowledge that the body remains intact.
The second objection uses a common experience to refute tafakkuk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
