The Eighth Proof: Geometry Refutes Indivisible Atoms
Razi presents a geometric proof against atomism using the incommensurability of the diagonal of a square.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a geometric argument (the Pythagorean theorem) to show that the diagonal of a square cannot be composed of a finite number of indivisible atoms, thereby refuting atomism.

Core Idea
Geometry proves that indivisible atoms cannot account for the diagonal length of a square.

Imam Razi presents the eighth proof against the doctrine of the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā). He uses a simple square with side ten to demonstrate a fatal contradiction: the diagonal's length, √200, is irrational and cannot be expressed as an integer multiple of the atom. This forces the conclusion that atoms must be divisible. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the key terms: 'murabba' (square), 'qutr' (diagonal), 'jadh' (root), and 'shakl al-ʿarūs' (Bride's Figure).
2
Understand the geometric premise
The proof assumes a square with side length ten equal indivisible parts. By the Pythagorean theorem (Bride's Figure), the diagonal is √200. Confirm that √200 is not a whole number.
The Pythagorean theorem states a² + b² = c² for a right triangle.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If the side consists of ten indivisible atoms, the diagonal would have to consist of some number of atoms. But √200 is irrational, so no integer number of atoms can measure the diagonal exactly. Hence the premise is false.
4
Recognise the conclusion
The failure shows that atoms cannot be indivisible; they must be further divisible. Thus the doctrine of atomism is refuted.
5
Reflect on the method
Note that Razi uses a purely mathematical argument to challenge a physical doctrine. This illustrates the integration of geometry into kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة لهم : إذا فرضنا مربعا متساوي الأضلاع بحيث يكون كل واحد من أجزائه عشرة لزم أن يكون قطره جذر مائتين ببرهان شكل العروس. ولكن ليس للمائتين جذر فعلمنا أن القول بالقسمة لازم لهم.
The eighth proof for them: If we suppose a square with equal sides such that each of its parts is ten, then its diagonal must be the root of two hundred by the proof of the Bride's Figure (shakl al-ʿarūs). But two hundred has no (rational) root, so we know that the claim of division (al-qawl bi-l-qisma) is necessary for them.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (al-hujja al-thāmina). It is a short geometric argument: (1) Consider a square whose side is composed of 10 parts (e.g., 10 indivisible atoms). (2) By the Pythagorean theorem (referred to as 'the Bride's Figure' – shakl al-ʿarūs), the diagonal equals √(10² + 10²) = √200. (3) But √200 has no rational root (i.e., it is irrational; it cannot be expressed as an integer or a ratio of integers). (4) If the side is composed of 10 indivisible atoms, the diagonal would have to measure some number of atoms, but it is impossible to measure it exactly with whole atoms because the square root is not an integer. (5) This forces the conclusion that the atoms must be divisible (i.e., the side must be divisible into smaller parts to account for the diagonal length). Therefore, the doctrine of the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā) cannot be maintained; rather, division (al-qisma) is necessary. The argument thus supports the view that matter is infinitely divisible.

Key Terms
مربع
ضلع
قطر
جذر
شكل العروس
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'no root' means the diagonal does not exist or is incalculable.
Correction: The phrase 'not for two hundred a root' means √200 is not a rational number (integer or fraction). The diagonal exists geometrically; its length is an irrational number, which cannot be expressed as a finite number of atoms.

Checkpoint
In the eighth proof, what geometric fact is used to show that the diagonal of a square with side ten is √200?
A) Euclid's parallel postulate
B) The Pythagorean theorem (Bride's Figure)
C) The Law of Sines
D) Archimedes' principle

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the irrationality of √200 derived from the Pythagorean theorem to refute the existence of indivisible atoms, arguing that matter must be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof forces us to accept an uncomfortable truth: the world doesn't always conform to our simple expectations. Where in your own life have you ignored evidence that contradicted a cherished belief? Are you willing to let that belief go today, even if it means starting over?
?
Razi used geometry to defend a theological point about the nature of creation. What tools has Allah given you—reason, scripture, experience—to discern truth? Are you using them all, or do you rely on only one? How might your life change if you took full advantage of all of them?
?
The diagonal of a square cannot be measured by the same unit as the side. In your spiritual life, what 'unit of measurement' do you use to assess your worth before Allah? Is it your deeds, your reputation, or something else? Could it be that you are using the wrong measure?

Ninth Proof: Slowness Not Due to Pauses
Razi begins the ninth proof with a claim and an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opening of Razi's ninth proof, which argues that slowness of motion is not caused by interposed moments of rest (sakanat), and how this relates to the divisibility of bodies.

Core Idea
Slowness is not due to pauses; it implies continuous motion and infinite divisibility.

Razi begins his ninth proof by tackling a key assumption about motion. He argues that slowness is not caused by tiny pauses between atomic moves, as some might think. Using a simple example of a horse and the sun, he sets up a proportional argument that, though incomplete here, points to the conclusion that bodies are always divisible. Let's examine this opening passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the Arabic breaks off mid-sentence at the end of the page.
The passage is incomplete, but the core claim is clear.
2
Identify the core claim and example
The passage begins with the claim that slowness is not due to interposed pauses. Find the example of the horse and the sun and understand why it is used to support the claim.
The horse's fast motion is still slower than the sun's daily motion.
3
Consider the incomplete proportion
The Arabic cuts off at 'لزم أن تكون نسبة' (then the proportion would have to be...). Think about what the proportion might be and how it could lead to the conclusion that bodies are always divisible.
This is a teaching moment to engage with the argument's logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة لهم بطء الحركة ليس لتخلل السكنات، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان الجسم قابلا للقسمة أبدا ، بيان الأول : أنا نفرض فرسا جوادا شديد العدو يعدو من أول النهار إلى وقت الظهر خمسين فرسخا فهذه الحركة مع أنها في غاية السرعة أبطأ من الحركة اليومية ؛ فإن الشمس تحركت من أول اليوم إلى وقت الظهر، ربع الفلك الأعظم. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان البطء لأجل تخلل السكنات، يلزم أن تكون نسبة
The ninth proof for them: The slowness (buṭ') of motion is not due to the interposition of pauses (sakanāt). And whenever this is the case, the body is always divisible. The explanation of the first: We suppose a swift racehorse that runs from the beginning of the day until noon fifty leagues (farsakh). This motion, though extremely fast, is slower than the daily motion; for the sun moves from the beginning of the day until noon one quarter of the largest sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam). If this is established, we say: If slowness were due to interposed pauses, then the proportion... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage begins the ninth proof (al-hujja al-tāsi'a). The argument aims to show that slowness (buṭ') of motion is not caused by intervening moments of rest (sakanāt) between movements. The claim is that if slowness were due to such pauses, then a faster motion would have fewer pauses and a slower motion more pauses, but the text argues otherwise using an example: (1) Consider a horse that runs 50 leagues from morning to noon. This is extremely fast, but it is still slower than the daily motion of the sun, which covers a quarter of the largest celestial sphere in the same time. (2) If slowness were caused by interposed pauses, then the horse's motion, being slower than the sun's, would have more pauses. (3) The text begins to show that this leads to a contradiction in proportions ('then the proportion...'), but the argument is cut off at the end of the page. The incomplete sentence likely intended to show that the ratio of speeds would force an irrational division, thus proving that bodies are always divisible (qābila li-l-qisma abadā). The fragment ends with 'لزم أن تكون نسبة' (then the proportion would have to be...). Despite the break, the core idea – that slowness is not due to pauses – implies continuous motion, which supports the notion of infinite divisibility of space and time.

Key Terms
بطء الحركة
سكنات
حركة يومية
فلك أعظم
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that slowness is caused by tiny moments of rest (sakanat) between atomic moves.
Correction: The passage explicitly states 'بطء الحركة ليس لتخلل السكنات' – slowness is not due to interposed pauses. Razi uses this to support continuous motion.

Checkpoint
In the ninth proof, what is the main claim about the nature of slowness?
A) Slowness is due to interposed pauses
B) Slowness is not due to interposed pauses
C) Slowness is illusory
D) Slowness depends on the observer

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof begins by asserting that slowness is not due to intervening pauses, using a comparison of motions to argue for continuous motion and infinite divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a goal you're pursuing slowly. Do you excuse your lack of progress by thinking you need more 'pauses'? Razi says slowness is not due to pauses. What would change in your daily effort if you stopped attributing slowness to rests and instead saw motion as continuous?
?
The horse runs fast but is slower than the sun. Which 'motions' in your life are you comparing to others, and are you using the right reference point? How might you realign your perspective so that you focus on your own steady movement rather than false comparisons?
?
If slowness is not from pauses, then every moment of your life is part of a continuous motion. What does that mean for how you spend each moment? Are you truly in motion, or are you using the illusion of pauses to avoid responsibility for your trajectory?

The Celestial Rotation Argument
Razi presents the seventh proof using celestial rotation to demonstrate the divisibility of the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the seventh proof against the indivisible atom and why it forces the conclusion that the small circle must move less than one part.

Core Idea
Celestial rotation forces the indivisible atom to be divisible.

This lesson explores a clever philosophical argument that uses the rotation of celestial spheres to challenge the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). By analyzing the motion of concentric circles, Razi presents a dilemma that can only be resolved by accepting that the supposedly indivisible part can be subdivided. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
The sphere rotates as a zone, and all parallel circles rotate.
Assume the sphere rotates uniformly.
2
Identify the dilemma
If the zone moves by one part, the small circle must either move one part (leading to contradiction) or not move at all (leading to disintegration).
3
See why both horns are impossible
The contradiction conflicts with reason; disintegration conflicts with philosophical proofs and the Qur'anic description of the heavens as 'seven strong ones'.
The Qur'an reference adds religious authority.
4
Conclude the only escape
The small circle must move less than one part, proving that the indivisible part can be divided.
This is the desired conclusion (al-matlab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة لهم : إذا استدار الفلك استدارة منطقة استدارت جميع الدوائر الموازية لتلك المنطقة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا تحركت المنطقة جزءا فالدائرة الصغيرة القريبة من القطب الموازية للمنطقة إن تحركت أيضا جزءًا لزم أن يكون مدار تلك الدائرة الصغيرة مساويا لمقدار المنطقة. هذا خلف. وإن لم تتحرك البتة، فحينئذ يلزم وقوع التفكك في أجزاء الفلك وذلك باطل أما الأول فلأن الدلائل الفلسفية قائمة على أن أجرام الفلك لا تقبل الانخراق والتفكيك وأما الثاني فلأن القرآن وصفها بكونها سبعا شدادا، وذلك ينافي وقوع التفكيك فيها فلم يبق إلا أن يقال: مهما تحركت المنطقة جزءا تحركت تلك الدائرة الصغيرة أقل من جزء وهو المطلوب. وهذا الكلام قد يفرضونه في حركة الرحى ويلزمون عليه : تفكك أجزاء الرحى والمتكلمون يلتزمونه ويقولون : إنه سبحانه وتعالى فاعل مختار فهو يفكك أجزاء الرحى حال استدارتها، ثم يعيد التأليف والتركيب إليها حال وقوفها. والفلاسفة يدفعون هذا من وجهين : الأول : الاستبعاد. والثاني : أن الإنسان لو ألصق عقبه بالأرض وأدار نفسه فحينئذ يلزم أن تتفكك أجزاء بدن الإنسان في تلك الحالة، ومن المعلوم أن الإنسان يعلم بالضرورة أنه في هذه الحالة بقيت أجزاء بدنه متلاصقة كما كانت قبل ذلك فبطل القول بالتفكيك.
The seventh proof for them: If the sphere rotates with a circular rotation, all circles parallel to that zone rotate. If you understand this, we say: If the zone moves by a part, then the small circle near the pole parallel to the zone—if it also moves by a part, then the orbit of that small circle would necessarily equal the extent of the zone. This is a contradiction (khalaf). And if it does not move at all, then disintegration (tafakkuk) necessarily occurs in the parts of the sphere, and that is invalid. As for the first (contradiction), because philosophical proofs are established that the celestial bodies cannot tolerate incision (inkhiraq) and disintegration. As for the second (invalidity), because the Qur'an describes them as seven strong ones, and that is contrary to the occurrence of disintegration in them. So there remains only to say: Whenever the zone moves by a part, that small circle moves less than a part—and that is what is sought (al-matlab). And this speech they may apply to the movement of a millstone, and they impute upon it the disintegration of the parts of the millstone. The theologians (mutakallimun) accept it and say: He, glorified and exalted, is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar); so He disintegrates the parts of the millstone during its rotation, then restores composition and structure to them when it stops. And the philosophers rebut this from two aspects: The first is implausibility (al-istib'ad). The second is that if a man sticks his heel to the ground and rotates himself, then the parts of his body would necessarily disintegrate in that state, and it is known that man knows by necessity that in this state the parts of his body remained contiguous as they were before, so the claim of disintegration is nullified.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained philosophical proof against the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). It begins by stating the seventh argument of the philosophers (al-falasifa). The argument uses celestial rotation: if the celestial sphere rotates as a whole zone, then all circles parallel to that zone must also rotate. Suppose the zone moves by one indivisible part (juz' la yatajazza'): then the small circle near the pole, if it also moves by one part, its orbit would equal the zone's orbit—a contradiction because the small circle's circumference is smaller. If the small circle does not move at all, then the parts of the sphere would disintegrate, which is impossible both philosophically (celestial bodies do not accept division) and religiously (the Qur'an describes them as 'seven strong ones'). Therefore, the small circle must move less than one part—proving the divisibility of the indivisible atom. Razi then presents the philosophers' extension of this argument to a millstone: they claim the same reasoning forces disintegration of the millstone's parts during rotation. The theologians (mutakallimun) accept this consequence, asserting that God, as a free agent, can disintegrate and recompose the millstone. The philosophers rebut this in two ways: first, it is implausible (istib'ad); second, a human rotating on his heel would experience no disintegration, known by necessity, so the disintegration claim fails. The passage follows the author's argumentative order: first the proof, then the objection and response.

Key Terms
الفلك
المنطقة
الجزء
الخلف
التفكك
الانخراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument shows that atoms actually break apart during rotation.
Correction: The argument shows that the indivisible atom cannot exist because motion requires fractional increments; it does not claim actual disintegration.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh argument conclude about the small circle's motion when the zone moves by one part?
A) It must also move by one part.
B) It must not move at all.
C) It must move less than one part.
D) It must move more than one part.

Key Takeaway
The celestial rotation argument forces the conclusion that the indivisible atom must be divisible into smaller parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces you to admit that even the smallest conceivable part of creation can be further divided. How does this challenge your sense of control or certainty about the fundamentals of reality? Will you accept this intellectual humility, or resist it?
?
Razi uses both philosophical proofs and the Qur'an to support his case. When have you faced a moment where reason and revelation seemed to pull in opposite directions? How did you resolve it—by dismissing one, or by seeking a deeper harmony?
?
The small circle must move less than a part—a fractional motion that our categories fail to capture. In your own life, where are you insisting on 'all or nothing' when the truth is a matter of degree? What specific step can you take today to embrace that grey area?

The Millstone Analogy and Philosophers' Rebuttal
Razi presents the theologians' acceptance and philosophers' rebuttal to the millstone analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologians respond to the millstone objection and how philosophers counter with the human rotation example.

Core Idea
The millstone analogy tests the limits of the theologians' commitment to divine intervention.

In this continuation of the seventh proof, Razi extends the celestial argument to a mundane millstone. The philosophers use this to show an absurd consequence, but the theologians accept it, appealing to God's power. The philosophers then offer two rebuttals, including a compelling empirical argument from human rotation. Let's explore this debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the extension
The same reasoning applied to a rotating millstone would imply its parts disintegrate.
2
The theologians' response
They accept that God disintegrates and recomposes the millstone during rotation.
This is consistent with their occasionalist worldview.
3
Philosophers' first rebuttal
Implausibility (al-istib'ad) — the idea is inherently unlikely.
4
Philosophers' second rebuttal
The human rotation example: if a man rotates with heel fixed, his body would need to disintegrate, but we know by necessity it does not.
This is an empirical refutation.

Eighth Proof: Square and Irrational Diagonal
Razi presents the eighth proof using a square's diagonal to show the atom must be divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the square's diagonal argument demonstrates the necessity of atomic divisibility.

Core Idea
The diagonal's irrational length forces atoms to be divisible.

Imam Razi now presents a clever mathematical proof that challenges the notion of indivisible atoms. By considering a square whose sides are made of ten atoms, he shows that its diagonal cannot be a whole number of atoms, exposing a contradiction. This argument relies on the well-known Pythagorean theorem, called 'the Bride's Figure' in Islamic mathematics.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: square with equal sides, diagonal, root two hundred.
The phrase 'شكل العروس' is the Bride's Figure, i.e., the Pythagorean theorem.
2
Understand the assumption
The proof assumes a square whose side consists of ten indivisible atoms. Ask yourself: what would the diagonal be if atoms are indivisible?
3
Follow the contradiction
The diagonal is √200 by geometry, but √200 is not an integer. If atoms are indivisible, the diagonal must be some integer number of atoms, but it cannot be — so the assumption leads to a contradiction.
This shows that the atom must be divisible to account for such lengths.
4
Draw the conclusion
Razi concludes that the claim of division (i.e., that the atom is divisible) is necessary. Understand why this refutes the indivisible atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة لهم : إذا فرضنا مربعا متساوي الأضلاع بحيث يكون كل واحد أجزائه عشرة لزم أن يكون قطره جذر مائتين ببرهان شكل العروس. ولكن ليس للمائتين جذر فعلمنا أن القول بالقسمة لازم لهم.
The eighth proof for them: If we suppose a square with equal sides such that each of its sides is ten, then its diagonal must be root two hundred (jider mi'atayn) by the proof of the Bride's Figure (shakl al-'arus). But two hundred has no [integer] root, so we know that the claim of division [into parts] is necessary for them.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a brief mathematical proof against the indivisible atom. The philosophers assume a square whose side is composed of ten indivisible parts. Its diagonal, by the Pythagorean theorem (called 'the Bride's Figure'), would be √200, but √200 is not an integer number of atoms. If the diagonal were composed of some number of atoms, that number would have to be either integer or fractional. Since it cannot be an integer, the atom must be divisible to account for fractional parts. Thus, the assumption of indivisible atoms leads to a contradiction, proving that the indivisible atom is divisible.

Key Terms
مربع متساوي الأضلاع
القطر
جذر مائتين
شكل العروس
القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on irrational numbers and is thus irrelevant to atomic theory.
Correction: The proof does not require irrational numbers; it only shows that √200 is not an integer, which is sufficient to disprove the assumption of indivisible atoms.

Checkpoint

B) The square's diagonal is √200, proving the atom is divisible.
C) The square's diagonal is irrational, so atoms do not exist.
D) The square's side can be any length.

Key Takeaway
The diagonal of a square with atomic sides is √200, which is non-integer, thus the atom must be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ninth Proof: Slowness and Divisibility
Razi disproves rests as cause of slowness, then proves divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's ninth proof uses the phenomenon of slowness to argue against the theory of interpenetration of rests and to establish the divisibility of all bodies.

Core Idea
Slowness proves divisibility: no rests observed, so bodies must be divisible.

Imam Razi presents the ninth argument in the debate on atomism. He begins by attacking the idea that slow motion is explained by moments of rest interspersed between movements. Using a vivid example of a fast horse and the sun's daily rotation, he shows that this theory leads to absurd consequences. He then demonstrates that the only consistent conclusion is that bodies are always divisible, undermining the theory of indivisible atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two-step argument: first disproving rests, then proving divisibility.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the horse-sun analogy
Razi compares a fast horse's motion to the sun's daily motion. If slowness were due to rests, the horse would have more rests than motions, but we observe no rests.
Ratio: sun's motions are a million times greater, so horse's rests would be a million times greater.
3
Follow the divisibility argument
Assume atoms are indivisible. Compare fast and slow motions in the same time. If they cover the same distance, speeds equal (contradiction). If slow covers less, atom divides.
This shows that atoms cannot be indivisible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that bodies are always divisible. Consider how this challenges atomist theories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة لهم بطء الحركة ليس لتخلل السكنات، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان الجسم قابلا للقسمة أبدا ، بيان الأول : أنا نفرض فرسا جوادا شديد العدو بحيث يعدو من أول النهار إلى وقت الظهر خمسين فرسخا فهذه الحركة مع أنها في غاية السرعة أبطأ من الحركة اليومية ; فإن الشمس تحركت من أول اليوم إلى وقت الظهر، ربع الفلك الأعظم. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان البطء لأجل تخلل السكنات، يلزم أن تكون نسبة سكنات هذا الفرس المذكور إلى حركاته كنسبة زيادة حركات الفلك الأعظم إلى حركات الفرس، لكن حركات الفلك أزيد من حركات هذا الفرس ألف ألف فيلزم أن يقال : سكنات هذا الفرس أزيد من حركاته ألف ألف مرة ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لما ظهرت تلك الحركات القليلة، فيما بين تلك السكنات الكثيرة، لكن الأمر بالضد; فإنا لا نشاهد في حركات الفرس المذكور شيئا من السكنات فعلمنا أنه ليس بطء الحركات لأجل تخلل السكنات. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : يلزم أن يكون الجسم قابلا أبدا للقسمة; لأنه لو كان مركبا من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، فلنفرض زمانا قطعت الحركة السريعة فيه جزءا لا يتجزأ ففي ذلك الزمان إن قطع البطيء أيضا جزءا كان البطيء مثل السريع في السرعة والبطء وهذا خلف، وإن قطع البطيء أقل من جزء لزم انقسام الجوهر الفرد وهو المطلوب.
The ninth proof for them: Slowness of motion is not due to interpenetration of rests (takhallul al-sakanat). And when this is the case, the body is always divisible. Explanation of the first: We suppose a fast racehorse that runs from the beginning of the day until noon fifty parasangs; this motion, although very fast, is slower than the daily motion; for the sun has moved from the beginning of the day until noon a quarter of the greatest sphere. If this is established, we say: If slowness were due to interpenetration of rests, then the ratio of the rests of this mentioned horse to its motions would be like the ratio of the increase of the motions of the greatest sphere to the motions of the horse. But the motions of the sphere are greater than the motions of this horse by a million (alf alf), so it would necessarily be said: The rests of this horse are greater than its motions by a million times. And if this were the case, those few motions would not appear among those many rests. But the opposite is the case; for we do not observe in the motions of the mentioned horse any rests. So we know that slowness of motions is not due to interpenetration of rests. If this is established, we say: The body is always divisible; because if it were composed of indivisible parts, then suppose a time in which the fast motion traverses one indivisible part. In that time, if the slow motion also traverses a part, then the slow would equal the fast in speed and slowness—a contradiction. And if the slow traverses less than a part, then the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is divisible—and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a proof that slowness of motion implies the divisibility of bodies. The philosophers first argue that slow motion cannot be caused by interpenetrating moments of rest (takhallul al-sakanat). They illustrate with a fast horse running 50 parasangs by noon: this motion is fast but still slower than the sun's daily motion, which covers a quarter of the celestial sphere by noon. If slowness were due to interspersed rests, the ratio of the horse's rests to its motions should equal the ratio of the sun's excess motions to the horse's motions. Since the sun's motions are a million times greater, the horse would have a million times more rests than motions. But we observe no rests in the horse's motion, so slowness is not due to rests. With this established, they prove the body is always divisible: if it were made of indivisible parts, consider a time in which the fast motion covers one indivisible part. In that same time, if the slow motion also covers one part, then both would have equal speed—a contradiction. If it covers less than one part, then the indivisible atom is divided—proving divisibility.

تخلل السكنات
السكنة

الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that rests do not exist at all.
Correction: The argument only proves that slowness cannot be explained by interspersed rests if atoms are indivisible; it does not deny the possibility of rests altogether.

Checkpoint

A) Because the sun's motion is faster, so the horse must have more motions.
B) Because the horse would have more rests than motions, which is not observed.
C) Because rests are impossible in animals.
D) Because the horse's motion is uniform.

Key Takeaway
Slowness of motion, if due to rests, would imply unobserved rests; thus motion shows that bodies are divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you convinced yourself that your lack of growth is due to external interruptions rather than a lack of real change within? What would it mean to recognize that you are fully capable of continuous movement, and that your slowness is not due to 'rests' but to a need for genuine transformation?
?
The proof concludes that bodies are divisible. Consider your own heart: Are there parts of your character that you treat as indivisible, saying 'this is just how I am'? Razi argues that nothing in creation is so atomic that it cannot be broken. What stubborn trait have you considered indivisible that Allah might be calling you to divide, to break apart through repentance and change?
?
Razi uses the sun's constant motion as a standard of speed. Your own lifespan is a finite motion from birth to death. If you were to measure your daily progress against the ultimate standard—your purpose in this life—would you find that your motion is actually slow because it is filled with 'rests' of heedlessness? Can you identify one specific 'rest' (distraction, sin, apathy) that you need to remove today so your motion toward Allah becomes continuous?

The Geometrical Refutation of the Indivisible Atom
Razi presents a geometrical argument against the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the geometric properties of spheres and polygons to prove that no body can be truly indivisible.

Core Idea
Geometric boundaries prove that no body is truly indivisible.

Razi now deploys geometry against the concept of the indivisible atom. He argues that any finite body must have boundaries, and those boundaries force the body to have parts of different sizes. This leads to the conclusion that even the smallest assumed particle is divisible. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the two distinct cases presented.
Razi uses a very compressed style; the translation will help clarify.
2
Understand the sphere case
The first case considers a body with a single boundary (a sphere). When spheres are adjacent, gaps appear that are smaller than the spheres themselves. This shows something smaller than the indivisible atom exists, so the atom is divisible.
The key point is that gaps are smaller than the spheres, not larger.
3
Understand the polygon case
The second case considers bodies with multiple boundaries (polygons). In polygons, the part near an angle (vertex) is smaller than the part along a side. This variation in size implies divisibility.
Think of a triangle: the tip is smaller than the base.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Razi concludes that in all cases—whether the atom is spherical or polygonal—it must be divisible. Therefore the indivisible atom posited by the theologians is impossible.
This refutes the atomic theory of the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة لهم كل متحيز فهو متناه، وكل متناه فإنه يحيط به حد وحدود. فإن أحاط به حد واحد فهو الكرة، وإذا تلاصقت الكرات حصلت فيما بينها فرج، بحيث يكون كل واحد من تلك الفرج أصغر من كل واحدة من تلك الكرات، فقد وجد شيء أصغر من الجوهر الفرد فينقسم الجوهر الفرد، وإن أحاط به حدود مختلفة فهو المضلعات كالمثلث والمربع، وكل ما كان كذلك فإن جانب الزاوية منه أصغر من جانب الضلع، وكل ما وقع التفاوت بالصغر والكبر في أجزائه وجوانبه، كان قابلا للانقسام، فالجوهر الفرد قابل للانقسام. فثبت أن الجوهر الفرد الذي يقول به المتكلمون لا بد أن يكون منقسما، على جميع التقديرات.
The tenth proof for them: Every extended thing (mutahayyiz) is finite, and every finite thing is surrounded by a boundary and boundaries. If it is surrounded by one boundary, it is the sphere. And when spheres are contiguous, gaps (furaj) occur between them, such that each of those gaps is smaller than each of those spheres. So something smaller than the indivisible atom has been found; thus the indivisible atom is divided. And if it is surrounded by different boundaries, it is polygons (al-mudalla'at) like the triangle and square. And everything like that has its angle-side smaller than its side-side. And whenever there is variation in smallness and bigness in its parts and sides, it is divisible. So the indivisible atom is divisible. Thus it is established that the indivisible atom which the theologians (mutakallimun) affirm must necessarily be divisible in all estimations.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof against the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) based on the geometry of limits. The argument runs: every body (mutahayyiz) is finite, and every finite body is enclosed by boundaries. If enclosed by a single boundary, it is a sphere. When spheres are placed together, gaps (furaj) arise between them; each gap is smaller than each sphere, therefore smaller than an indivisible atom. Since the atom is supposedly the smallest possible, but here a smaller thing exists, the atom must be divisible. If the body is enclosed by multiple boundaries, it is a polygon (e.g., triangle or square). In a polygon, the part near the angle (the vertex) is smaller than the part along the side. Hence there is variation in size among parts, implying divisibility. Thus, whether the atom is spherical or polygonal, it is divisible. Razi concludes: the indivisible atom affirmed by the theologians is necessarily divisible in all possible cases.

متناه
الحد
الكرة
الفرج
المضلعات
المثلث

الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spheres can be packed without leaving gaps.
Correction: When spheres touch, gaps appear between them, and these gaps are smaller than the spheres themselves, contradicting the indivisible atom.

Checkpoint

A) They fill space completely.
B) Gaps appear that are smaller than each sphere.
C) The spheres merge into one.
D) The gaps are larger than the spheres.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom is impossible because any finite body's shape implies smaller parts, leading to divisibility.

Razi used geometry to demolish a false certainty. What false certainties in your own thinking need a precise, logical examination? Are you willing to let evidence and reason break down what you hold dear, even if it is uncomfortable? What step can you take today to test a deeply held assumption about your faith or life?

Razi's Unified Answer to Ten Philosophical Proofs
Razi gives a single counterargument to ten proofs against the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's unified refutation of the ten philosophical arguments against the indivisible atom, focusing on the shared flaw that renders them invalid.

Core Idea
All ten proofs share a flaw: they prove actual infinite division, which even opponents reject.

Razi has presented ten arguments against the indivisible atom. Now he offers a single, elegant answer that applies to all of them. Instead of refuting each separately, he identifies the common mistake that undermines every proof. This passage shows his strategic efficiency and deep understanding of the philosophical principles at stake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let Razi's concise argument settle in.
Notice that Razi references a separate book for detailed answers.
2
Identify the shared flaw
Razi says all ten proofs share one thing: they assign a special property to one side of the atom that is not on the other side. This, he argues, would require actual infinite division.
3
Understand actual vs. potential division
The philosophers only accept potential division, not actual. Razi's point is that the proofs would force actual infinite division, which even their proponents do not believe.
This is the key distinction that nullifies the arguments.
4
See the conclusion
Since the philosophers deny actual infinite division, the proofs cannot prove what they intend. Therefore, all ten are invalid.
5
Note the deferral to the detailed work
Razi ends by directing readers to his separate book for detailed responses, indicating this is a summary.
This shows confidence in his comprehensive treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنقتصر من دلائل نفاة الجوهر الفرد على هذه الوجوه العشرة، فإن لنا كتابا منفردا في هذه المسألة. ومن أراد الاستقصاء فيها فليطالع ذلك الكتاب واعلم أنا نجيب عن جميع هذه الشبه بجواب واحد ونحيل بالأجوبة المفصلة على ذلك الكتاب، فنقول : إن جميع هذه الوجوه مشتركة في شيء واحد، وهو أنه قد اختص أحد جانبي الجزء بخاصية لا تحصل في الجانب الآخر منه، ولكن هذا المعنى يوجب حصول انقسامات غير متناهية بالفعل والفلاسفة لا يقولون بهذا المعنى. وما هو نتيجة هذه الشبه لا يقولون به، وما يقولون به فهو الانقسام بالقوة فقط. لا نتيجة هذه الوجوه. فثبت سقوط هذه الوجوه بأسرها. وأما الأجوبة المفصلة فهي مذكورة في الكتاب الذي صنفناه في هذه المسألة وبالله التوفيق.
And we restrict ourselves from the proofs of the negators of the indivisible atom to these ten aspects, for we have a separate book on this issue. Whoever desires exhaustive treatment of it should consult that book. And know that we answer all these doubts with a single answer, and we refer the detailed answers to that book. We say: All these aspects share one thing, namely that one side of the part (juz') is distinguished by a property that does not occur in the other side of it. But this meaning necessitates the occurrence of infinite actual divisions (inqisamat ghayr mutanahiya bil-fi'l), and the philosophers do not affirm this meaning. What is the result of these doubts they do not affirm, and what they affirm is only potential division (al-inqisam bil-quwwa), not the result of these aspects. So it is established that all these aspects are invalid entirely. As for the detailed answers, they are mentioned in the book we composed on this issue. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is Razi's summary response to the ten philosophical arguments against the indivisible atom. He first notes that he has limited his presentation to these ten proofs, deferring fuller treatment to a dedicated work. Then he offers a single overarching rebuttal: all the proofs share a common structure—they assign a special property to one side of the atom that is absent from the other side (e.g., in the rotation argument, the small circle is near the pole, so its motion differs from the zone; in the square, the diagonal is longer than the side; in the slow motion argument, the time for a slow atom is less than for a fast atom). This, he argues, would imply that the atom actually has infinite divisions (actual infinitude), which even the philosophers do not accept—they only accept potential divisibility. Since the philosophers do not affirm the very thing their proofs are supposed to establish (actual division), the proofs fail. Therefore, all ten arguments are invalid. Razi refers detailed responses to his separate book. He ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms

انقسامات غير متناهية
بالفعل
بالقوة
الانقسام بالقوة
الإحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi admits the proofs are strong and only gives a partial response.
Correction: Razi argues the proofs prove too much—they entail actual infinite division, which the philosophers themselves reject. Thus the arguments are not only weak but self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What is the single flaw Razi identifies in all ten proofs?
A) They rely on false premises about atomism.
B) They prove only potential division, not actual division.
C) They confuse physics with metaphysics.
D) They require the atom to have properties on one side only.

Key Takeaway
Razi's single answer refutes all ten proofs by showing they entail actual infinite division, which even the philosophers who deny the atom reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers rejected actual infinite division because it contradicted their own principles. Are there arguments you use to defend your beliefs that, if followed to their logical end, would actually undermine those very beliefs? Will you humbly audit your reasoning today and discard what is inconsistent?
?
Razi recognized that the proofs proved something the opponents themselves did not affirm. In your life, do you sometimes hold others to standards or expectations that you yourself do not meet? What is one concrete step you will take to align your treatment of others with the standard you set for them?
?
The passage contrasts actual (bil-fi'l) and potential (bil-quwwa) division. In your spiritual journey, are you content with potential obedience—good intentions and plans—without actual, concrete action? What specific act of worship or self-discipline will you move from intention to execution this week?

The Self is Not the Physical Body: Razi's Argument
Razi defines the self as the referent of 'I' and refutes the view that it is the physical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of the self and his two arguments proving that the self is not identical to the physical body.

Core Idea
You are not your body—you remain the same while your body changes completely.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins his discussion of the self (nafs) by clarifying what we mean by the term: it is the referent of 'I' in our everyday speech. He then refutes a popular view among theologians that this self is nothing but the physical body. In this passage, you will find two powerful arguments that show why the self cannot be the changing, material body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening lines where Razi defines the self as the thing each person points to when saying 'I'. This is the key term for the whole argument.
The word 'nafs' is used here in a psychological sense, not as 'soul' in a purely spiritual sense.
2
Observe the classification of views
Razi lists the possible positions: the self is a body, a bodily thing, neither, or a composition. Notice that the view he refutes is a subset: the self is the visible physical structure.
3
Follow the first proof
Razi argues from personal identity over time: you know you are the same person as twenty years ago, but your body has completely changed. Therefore, you cannot be your body.
Focus on the intuition of continuity despite physical change.
4
Follow the second proof
Razi argues that you know your own essence directly even while oblivious to all your limbs. What is known (the self) must differ from what is not known (the limbs).
This uses the distinction between direct awareness and external knowledge.
5
Connect the conclusion
Although the passage ends mid-sentence, the conclusion is clear: the self is not the physical body. The next lessons will explore what it actually is.
This refutation opens the door to positive definitions of the self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة والعشرون من في حقيقة النفس اعلم أن مرادنا لفظة النفس هو الشيء الذي يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله (أنا) حين يقول أنا فعلت، وأنا أدركت. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : اختلفوا في حقيقة النفس وضبط تلك المذاهب أن يقال : الذي يشير إليه كل واحد بقوله (أنا) إما أن يكون جسما أو جسمانيا، أو لا جسما ولا جسمانيا، أو يكون مركبا من هذه الأقسام تركيبا ثنائيا أو ثلاثيا، فإن كان جسما فذلك الجسم إما أن يكون هو هذا الهيكل المحسوس، أو جسم حاصل في داخله. والقول الأول: هو اختيار طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين إلا أنه ضعيف ويدل على ضعفه وجهان : الأول : أني أعلم ببداهة عقلي أني الآن هو الذي كنت موجودا قبل هذا اليوم بعشرين سنة وهذا الهيكل المحسوس الموجود ليس هو الذي كان موجودًا قبل ذلك بعشرين سنة ؛ لأن أجزاء هذا الهيكل متبدلة تارة بالسمن والهزال، وتارة بسائر أنواع التحللات من العرق وانفصال الفضلات من الدماميل وغيرها ولأنه محتاج إلى الغذاء والغذاء عبارة عما يصير بدلا لما تحلل من البدن وكيف لا يكون كذلك، والبدن حار رطب والحار إذا عمل في الرطب أصعد عنه الأجزاء البخارية وحللها ؟ وإذا ثبت أن هذا الإنسان هو الذي بعينه كان موجودا قبل هذا اليوم بعشرين سنة، وثبت أن هذا الهيكل ليس هو الذي كان موجودا قبل ذلك، ثبت قطعا أن الإنسان ليس عبارة عن هذا الهيكل. الوجه الثاني: أني أعلم بالضرورة ذاتي المخصوصة، حال ما أكون غافلا عن جميع ما لي من الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم، فوجب
The twenty-eighth question concerning the reality of the self. Know that our intended meaning of the word 'self' (nafs) is the thing to which every human points by his saying 'I' when he says 'I did' and 'I perceived'. If you know this, we say: they differed concerning the reality of the self, and the classification of those doctrines is that what every person points to by his saying 'I' is either a body or a bodily thing, or neither a body nor a bodily thing, or composed of these divisions in a dual or triple composition. If it is a body, that body is either this sensible structure or a body existing inside it. The first view is the choice of a great group of the mutakallimun, but it is weak, and two aspects indicate its weakness: The first: I know by my rational intuition that I am now the one who existed twenty years before today, and this sensible existing structure is not the one that existed twenty years before that, because the parts of this structure change, sometimes by fatness and emaciation, and sometimes by other types of dissolution from sweat and separation of wastes from abscesses and others, and because it needs food, and food is that which becomes a substitute for what has dissolved from the body. And how could it not be so? The body is hot and moist, and when heat acts upon moisture, it causes the vaporous parts to rise and dissolves them? And if it is established that this human is the very one who existed twenty years before today, and it is established that this structure is not the one that existed before that, it is definitively established that the human is not this structure. The second aspect: I know necessarily my specific essence while I am oblivious to all my external and internal limbs, and the known is different from what is not known, so it follows
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage begins the twenty-eighth question (mas'ala) on the reality of the self (nafs). First, Razi defines the term: it is what every person refers to when saying 'I' in statements like 'I did' or 'I perceived'. Then he classifies the possible doctrines: the referent of 'I' could be a body, a bodily thing (jismani), neither body nor bodily, or a composition of these. If it is a body, it is either the visible physical structure (haykal) or a body inside it. He then attacks the first view (that the self is the visible body), which he says is held by many mutakallimun but is weak. He gives two proofs. The first proof: I know intuitively that I am the same person as twenty years ago, but the body has changed completely (through growth, weight loss, dissolution, replacement via food), so I cannot be identical to the body. The second proof: I know my own essence directly when I am completely unaware of all my limbs (external and internal), so what is known (my self) must be different from what is not known (the limbs). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear: the self is not the physical body.

Key Terms
نفس (self)
أنا (I)
هيكل (body/structure)
بداهة (intuition)
ذات (essence)
أعضاء (limbs)
مغايرة (difference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is denying the physical body entirely or claiming dualism, but he is only refuting the equation of self with this specific physical structure.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that the self is not the changing visible body, but he does not yet assert what the self positively is (e.g., a non-physical substance).

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that Razi gives to show that the self is not the physical body?
A) The body is composed of atoms that are always dividing
B) A person remains the same over time while the body changes completely
C) The self is known only through divine revelation
D) The body is subject to decay after death

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes that the self cannot be the physical body because the self remains identical over time while the body constantly changes, and because we are directly aware of our essence apart from our limbs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look in the mirror, do you identify with that aging reflection? After reading this passage, can you honestly say you still think that person is 'you'? What would it mean for your daily life to live as if your real self is not that changing body?
?
Razi says you know your essence even when oblivious to your limbs. Have you ever felt a sense of self that survived forgetting your body? When? What does that tell you about who you really are?
?
If your body is not you, then what connection does your true self have to your physical actions on the Day of Judgment? How does this change your sense of accountability for what your body does?

Three Ancient Theories of the Soul and Their Refutation
Razi lists three ancient theories of the soul and refutes them logically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ancient materialist theories of the soul presented by Razi and his logical refutation of all of them.

Core Idea
Sharing a property does not prove identity—Razi's logical critique.

In this passage, Razi continues his examination of views on the soul by presenting three pre-Socratic theories: the soul as fire, air, or water. Each theory argues from a shared attribute. Razi then delivers a single logical refutation that dismantles all three using the rules of syllogistic reasoning. This lesson will help you see how kalam engages with ancient philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three theories
Read the Arabic and translation, then list the three theories and their proponents.
Pay attention to the logical structure of each argument.
2
Understand each theory's reasoning
For each theory, note the attribute shared between the soul and the element (fire: illumination/movement; air: colorless/penetrating; water: growth). How does each theorist infer identity?
3
Analyze Razi's refutation
Read the last sentence: Razi says the proofs are weak because they are syllogisms with two affirmative premises in the second figure. Understand why that is invalid: different essences can share attributes.
This is a key logical concept in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول الثاني . وهو أن الإنسان عبارة عن جسم موجود في داخل هذا الهيكل فهذا القول فيه وجوه. أحدها : قول (أقلو) طرجس). وهو أنه عبارة عن الأجزاء النارية السارية في هذا الهيكل قال : لأن خاصية النار الإشراق والحركة والنفس خاصيتها الإدراك والحركة الاختيارية والإدراك من جنس الإشراق فثبت أن النفس عبارة عن النار. قالوا : ويتأيد هذا بما يقول الأطباء : إن مدير البدن هو الحرارة الغريزية. وثانيها : قول (ديوجانس) وهو أن النفس هو الهواء قال : (وذلك لأنه متى كان النفس مترددا، كانت الحياة باقية. وإذا انقطع النفس زالت الحياة، فوجب أن تكون النفس عبارة عن التنفس، وهو الهواء المستنشق المتردد في مخالق البدن، وأيضا من خواص الهواء: أنه لا لون له ويدخل في المنافذ الضيقة، ويقبل الأشكال المختلفة، والجسم الذي يكون فيه مستقلا بذاته كالزق المنفوخ. والنفس بهذه الصفات فثبت أن النفس هو الهواء. وثالثها: قول ( ثاليس الملطي قال : ( إن النفس هو الماء، لأن الماء سبب لحصول النشوء والنمو، والنفس أيضا كذلك ( فكان النفس هو الماء. واعلم أن دلائل هذه الأقوال الثلاثة كلها ضعيفة لأنها أقيسة مركبة من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني، وذلك غير منتج ؛ لأنه لا يمنع في العقل استواء الماهيات المختلفة في بعض الصفات.
As for the second doctrine—that the human being is a body existing inside this structure—this doctrine has aspects. First of them: the saying of 'Aqlu Ṭurjus' (possibly Plato?): that it is the fiery parts permeating this structure. He said: because the property of fire is illumination and movement, and the property of the soul is perception and voluntary movement, and perception is of the genus of illumination, so it is established that the soul is fire. They said: This is supported by what the physicians say: that the director of the body is the innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya). Second: the saying of Diogenes: that the soul is air. He said: because when the breath (nafas) oscillates, life remains, and when breath ceases, life vanishes, so the soul must be the breath, i.e., the inhaled air oscillating in the cavities of the body. Also, among the properties of air: it has no color, enters narrow passages, accepts different shapes, and a body that is self-subsistent in it is like an inflated skin. The soul has these qualities, so it is established that the soul is air. Third: the saying of Thales of Miletus: that the soul is water, because water is the cause of growth and development, and the soul is likewise, so the soul is water. Know that the proofs of these three sayings are all weak because they are syllogisms composed of two affirmative premises in the second figure, which is non-productive; because reason does not prevent different essences (māhiyyāt) from being equal in some attributes.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the second major view: the soul is a body inside the observable body. Razi lists three ancient philosophical theories: (1) the soul as fiery particles (attributed to 'Aqlu Ṭurjus, likely Plato or another), because fire has illumination and movement, and the soul has perception and voluntary movement; also supported by medical theory of innate heat. (2) Diogenes says the soul is air, since breathing sustains life, and air has properties like being colorless and entering narrow passages, similar to the soul. (3) Thales says the soul is water, because water causes growth and the soul does too. Razi then refutes all three with a single logical criticism: their proofs are syllogisms with two affirmative premises in the second figure (i.e., both premises are affirmative and the middle term is predicate in both), which does not produce a necessary conclusion. It is possible for different essences to share some attributes without being identical. Hence the arguments are invalid.

Key Terms
أجزاء نارية (ajzāʾ nāriyya)
حرارة غريزية (ḥarāra gharīziyya)
هواء (hawāʾ)
شكل ثاني (shakl thānī)
موجبتان (mūjibatān)
ماهيات (māhiyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a property, they must be the same essence.
Correction: Razi shows that different essences can share attributes, so shared property does not prove identity.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the proofs of the fire, air, and water theories weak?
A) Because they rely on scripture
B) Because they use invalid syllogisms with two affirmatives in the second figure
C) Because they deny the Afterlife
D) Because they contradict Aristotle

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes ancient materialist theories of the soul by pointing out that syllogisms with two affirmative premises in the second figure do not yield necessary conclusions, as different essences can share attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed something about a person because they shared a superficial trait with someone else. How might that assumption have been as flawed as the ancient theories? Will you commit to examining deeper before judging?
?
Razi's logical caution reveals that we often confuse shared properties with identity. Where in your own spiritual life have you equated external actions with inner faith, or rituals with true devotion? What would it mean to seek the essence beyond the attributes?
?
The ancient philosophers were confident in their flawed reasoning. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself—your worth, your ability, your identity—that are based on shaky premises? Will you dare to question them and ground your self-understanding in sound evidence from revelation and reason?

Fifth View: The Soul is Blood
Razi states the fifth view: soul is blood, because blood is the noblest humor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth view that identifies the soul with blood, its rationale, and its place in the debate about the soul's essence.

Core Idea
Some ancients thought the soul is blood, the noblest humor.

Imam Razi continues his survey of historical views on the soul. The fifth view is a simple materialist theory: the soul is nothing but blood, because blood is the most noble of the body's four humors. This view reduces the soul to a bodily fluid, a claim Razi will likely refute later. Let's read the brief statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic sentence for the fifth view carefully: 'وخامسها أن النفس عبارة عن الدم؛ لأنه أشرف أخلاط البدن.' Notice the claim and its justification.
This is a very concise statement.
2
Understand the justification
The view is supported by the idea that blood is the noblest humor. Why would that make it the soul? Reflect on the ancient humoral theory.
3
Consider the implications
If the soul is blood, then death would be the loss of blood. This is a materialistic view. Think about how this contrasts with spiritual or dualist theories.
Razi will later critique this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage contains two final views. The fifth view identifies the soul with blood, because blood is the noblest humor. Razi does not provide an explicit refutation here. The sixth view is a more sophisticated theory: souls are subtle bodies (ajsām laṭīfa) by nature. The argument first states that bodies have different essences, using the example that earth is dense and cannot become subtle, while fire is subtle and cannot become dense. The soul, then, is a subtle body that permeates the physical structure (haykal) like rosewater permeates rose leaves or fire permeates coal (the text cuts off). This theory avoids the problem of the soul being a dense body, but the passage is incomplete, so the full argument is not given.

Sixth View: The Soul as Subtle Bodies (Incomplete)
Razi presents the sixth view: souls are subtle bodies that permeate the body, with an analogy.

Souls are subtle bodies that permeate physical structures.

Refuting the Divisibility of Accidents in Bodies
Imam Razi refutes four objections against the premise that every body is divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses four distinct examples (point, unity, relations, existence) to refute the claim that every accident inhering in a divisible body must itself be divisible.

Core Idea
Indivisible qualities like unity and existence prove that accidents need not be divided with the body.

Imam Razi has already argued for the existence of the indivisible atom. Now he confronts a natural objection: if the body is divisible, why aren't its accidents also divisible? He provides four compelling counterexamples — the point, unity, relations, and existence — each demonstrating that an indivisible attribute can inhere in a divisible subject. This lesson walks through each proof, clarifying how they together dismantle the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the four arguments. Notice how Razi structures his response: he first clarifies his position, then presents each proof with 'one of them', 'second', etc.
The passage begins with a concession about the atom, then moves to the core refutation.
2
Identify the four arguments
Locate the four distinct proofs: point, unity, relations, existence. Write down each one in your own words, noting the key point each makes.
Each argument is introduced by a number: أحدها, وثانيها, وثالثها, ورابعها.
3
Analyze each proof closely
For each argument, ask: What is the example? How does it show that an accident can be indivisible? Does it assume the indivisible atom or prove it? Discuss the logic behind each.
The point example is particularly interesting because it works whether the point is a substance or an accident.
4
Understand the overall refutation
Reflect on why Razi presents these four examples. They cover different categories: spatial (point), qualitative (unity), relational (fatherhood), and existential (existence). This shows the opponent that their assumption is not universally true.
The fourth argument also includes a reductio ad absurdum if one insists that existence is divisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا لا نسلم أن كل متحيز فهو يقبل القسمة أبدا وقد قدمنا الدلائل على إثبات الجوهر الفرد سلمنا ذلك ولكن لا نسلم أن الحال في المنقسم منقسم، ويدل عليه وجوه :

أحدها: أن النقطة موجودة مشار إليها غير منقسمة وهي إن كانت جوهرا فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد وإن عرضا وافتقرت إلى محل فذلك المحل إن كان منقسما لزم انقسام النقطة لانقسام محلها. وهو محال . وإن لم يكن منقسما، فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد.

وثانيها : أن الوحدة عرض . وهي من أشد الأشياء مباعدة عن الكثرة. ثم الجسم قد يوصف بالوحدة. فثبت أن ما لا يقبل القسمة يصح قيامه بالجسم .

وثالثها: أن الإضافات كالأبوة والبنوة والأخوة قائمة بالأجسام، ويمتنع أن يقال قام بنصف هيكل الأب نصف الأبوة، وقام بثلثه ثلثها.

ورابعها أن الوجود صفة قائمة بالجسم، ويمتنع أن يقال : قائم بنصفه نصف الوجود، وبثلثه ثلث الوجود أو يلتزم ذلك، ويقال : إن نصف الوجود أيضا وجود، وثلث الوجود وجود لكن إذا جاز هذا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم القائم بالجسم المنقسم يكون منقسما، ويكون أيضا نصف العلم علما، وثلث العلم علما.

فهذا ما في هذه المسألة . وبالله التوفيق.
Know that we do not concede that every spatially extended thing is divisible; indeed we have presented proofs for the indivisible atom. Granted that, but we do not concede that the accident in a divisible thing is divisible, and there are proofs for that:

One of them: The point exists, is pointed to, and is indivisible. If it is a substance, then the indivisible atom is established; if it is an accident and requires a subject, then if that subject is divisible, the division of the point would follow from the division of its subject, which is impossible. If it is not divisible, then the indivisible atom is established.

Second: Unity is an accident, and it is among the most distant things from multiplicity. Yet the body may be described by unity. Thus it is established that what does not accept division may validly inhere in a body.

Third: Relations such as fatherhood, sonship, and brotherhood inhere in bodies, and it is impossible to say that half of the father's structure carries half of the fatherhood, or one-third of it carries one-third.

Fourth: Existence is an attribute inhering in the body, and it is impossible to say that half of it carries half of existence, or one-third of it carries one-third of existence. Or if one concedes that, and says that half of existence is also existence and one-third of existence is existence, then if this is allowed, why is it not allowed to say that knowledge inhering in a divisible body is divisible, and that half of knowledge is knowledge and one-third of knowledge is knowledge? 

This is what pertains to this issue. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its four rebuttals. The objector denies that every body is divisible (citing the indivisible atom). Imam Razi concedes the possibility of atoms but rejects that an accident inhering in a divisible thing must itself be divisible. He provides four examples: (1) The point: whether substance or accident, it shows an indivisible entity can inhere in a subject. (2) Unity: an accident that strongly repels multiplicity can inhere in a divisible body. (3) Relations: fatherhood cannot be divided according to the father's body parts. (4) Existence: existence cannot be divided; if one claimed half of existence is existence, then by analogy half of knowledge would be knowledge, which is absurd. Thus the original claim that knowledge in a divisible body must be divisible is refuted.

Key Terms
نقطة
وحدة
إضافات
وجود
جوهر فرد
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body is divisible, then every accident inhering in it must also be divisible.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that many accidents — like unity, relations, and existence — are indivisible despite inhering in divisible bodies, refuting the notion that divisibility of the subject entails divisibility of the accident.

Checkpoint
Which example does Imam Razi use to show that an indivisible accident can inhere in a divisible body?
A) Knowledge
B) The point
C) Color
D) The soul

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the argument that every accident in a divisible body must be divisible by providing four counterexamples: point, unity, relations, and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own existence as something that can be divided among your different roles—parent, employee, worshipper? How does the indivisibility of existence in the body challenge you to see yourself as a unified whole before Allah?
?
If unity is an accident that is indivisible yet describes a body, how does that reflect on the oneness of Allah? Does your daily life reflect unity of purpose or division of loyalty between worldly desires and faith?
?
Imam Razi uses clear logical arguments to defend a point of aqeedah. Are you using reasoning in your faith, or do you accept assumptions without proof? Will you commit to studying proofs like these to strengthen your certainty and avoid blind following?

Four Proofs for the Indivisible Atom
Al-Razi gives four arguments that qualities in a divisible body are not divisible, proving the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's four arguments that the indivisibility of accidents like point, unity, relations, and existence requires an indivisible substratum, proving the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Indivisible qualities demand an indivisible bearer.

Al-Razi here tackles a key objection to the atomic theory: if bodies are divisible, why can't the qualities inhering in them be divisible too? He offers four independent arguments to show that certain accidents—like a point, unity, relations, and existence—cannot be divided, and therefore their locus must also be indivisible. Each argument drives home the same conclusion: the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) must exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that Razi first states his position, then gives four numbered arguments.
The arguments are independent; each alone is meant to be sufficient.
2
Examine the first argument (the point)
Focus on the argument using the geometrical point (nuqta). It is either a substance (proving the atom) or an accident. If an accident, its locus must be indivisible, or the point would divide. This forces the existence of an indivisible locus.
This argument assumes points exist, a common medieval view.
3
Study the second argument (unity)
Unity (wahda) is an accident farthest from multiplicity. Since it inheres in bodies, the body must support something indivisible. This shows indivisibility can inhere in bodies.
Unity here is the attribute of being one, not the number one.
4
Analyze the third argument (relations)
Relations like fatherhood cannot be divided: half of fatherhood cannot inhere in half the father. So the relation inheres in the whole body, implying the body as a whole is the locus, not its parts.
This argument relies on the intuition that relational properties are not quantitative.
5
Consider the fourth argument (existence)
Existence (wujud) is a single attribute of a body. If it were divisible, half-existence would be existence, leading to absurdities like knowledge being divisible. Thus existence is indivisible, requiring an indivisible subject.
This argument parallels the third but uses a different attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا لا نسلم أن كل متحيز فهو يقبل القسمة أبدا وقد قدمنا الدلائل على إثبات الجوهر الفرد سلمنا ذلك ولكن لا نسلم أن الحال في المنقسم منقسم، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن النقطة موجودة مشار إليها غير منقسمة وهي إن كانت جوهرا فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد وإن عرضا وافتقرت إلى محل فذلك المحل إن كان منقسما لزم انقسام النقطة لانقسام محلها. وهو محال . وإن لم يكن منقسما، فقد ثبت الجوهر الفرد. وثانيها : أن الوحدة عرض . وهي من أشد الأشياء مباعدة عن الكثرة. ثم الجسم قد يوصف بالوحدة. فثبت أن ما لا يقبل القسمة يصح قيامه بالجسم . وثالثها: أن الإضافات كالأبوة والبنوة والأخوة قائمة بالأجسام، ويمتنع أن يقال قام بنصف هيكل الأب نصف الأبوة، وقام بثلثه ثلثها. ورابعها أن الوجود صفة قائمة بالجسم، ويمتنع أن يقال : قائم بنصفه نصف الوجود، وبثلثه ثلث الوجود أو يلتزم ذلك، ويقال : إن نصف الوجود أيضا وجود، وثلث الوجود وجود لكن إذا جاز هذا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم القائم بالجسم المنقسم يكون منقسما، ويكون أيضا نصف العلم علما، وثلث العلم علما. فهذا ما في هذه المسألة . وبالله التوفيق.
Know that we do not accept that every extended thing is always divisible; we have already presented proofs for the existence of the indivisible atom. We accept that, but we do not accept that what inheres in something divisible is itself divisible. This is indicated by several points: First: that the point exists, is pointed to, and is indivisible. If it is a substance, then the indivisible atom is established; if it is an accident and requires a locus, then that locus, if divisible, would necessitate the divisibility of the point due to the divisibility of its locus, which is impossible. If it is not divisible, then the indivisible atom is established. Second: that unity is an accident, and it is among the things most far from multiplicity. Yet the body is described as having unity. So it is established that what does not admit division can validly inhere in a body. Third: that relations such as fatherhood, sonship, and brotherhood inhere in bodies, and it is impossible to say that half of fatherhood inheres in half of the father's body and a third of it in a third. Fourth: that existence is an attribute inhering in the body, and it is impossible to say that half of existence inheres in half of it and a third of existence in a third of it, or to commit to that and say that half of existence is also existence and a third of existence is existence. But if this is allowed, then why is it not allowed to say that knowledge inhering in a divisible body is divisible, and that half of knowledge is knowledge and a third of knowledge is knowledge? This is what is in this problem. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a series of four arguments (وجوه) responding to the claim that whatever is extended is divisible. The author first concedes that proofs for the indivisible atom have been given, but then argues that properties inhering in divisible bodies are not themselves divisible. The first argument uses the point (nuqta): the point is indivisible and either a substance (proving the atom) or an accident requiring a locus; if the locus were divisible, the point would be divisible, which is impossible, so the locus must be indivisible, again proving the atom. The second argument uses unity (wahda): unity is an accident that maximally repels multiplicity, yet bodies are described as one, so something indivisible can inhere in a body, showing that indivisibility does not prevent inherence. The third argument uses relations (idafat) like fatherhood: these relations inhere in bodies as wholes, not in parts, so half-of-fatherhood cannot inhere in half the father's body. The fourth argument uses existence (wujud): existence is a single attribute inhering in a body; if it were divisible, half-existence would be existence, leading to the absurd consequence that knowledge, another attribute, would also be divisible into half-knowledge as knowledge. Thus the indivisible atom is established.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
النقطة
الوحدة
الإضافات
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi is directly proving the existence of atoms without considering objections.
Correction: He is responding to the objection that accidents in a divisible body must themselves be divisible, and he uses the indivisibility of certain accidents to argue for an indivisible substratum.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, why does al-Razi claim that relations like fatherhood disprove the divisibility of qualities?
A) Because fatherhood is not a real attribute.
B) Because half of fatherhood cannot inhere in half the father's body.
C) Because fatherhood is divisible but we cannot perceive it.
D) Because fatherhood is an accident that does not need a locus.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's four arguments show that certain accidents are indivisible, thereby requiring an indivisible substance—the indivisible atom—as their bearer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your faith is one indivisible whole, but do you live it as such, or do you try to portion out your obedience to Allah? The unity that inheres in your body demands a response: will you allow that unity to transform your entire life, or will you keep parts of yourself separate?
?
Relations like fatherhood or brotherhood are not divisible in the body—they reflect deeper spiritual bonds. Are your relationships with others grounded in the same indivisible commitment as the attribute of existence itself, or are they conditional and partial?
?
Al-Razi argues that existence itself is indivisible. If your very existence is a single, undivided gift from Allah, how can you treat some moments as less important or some sins as insignificant? What would it mean to live with the awareness that every atom of your being is held together by His constant will?
