Answer to the First Objection: Possibility Does Not Require Existence
Refutation of the first objection: circularity and the principle of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection (which argues that re-creation requires identity) is circular, and how the author refutes it by showing that possibility judgments do not require actual existence.

Core Idea
Possibility does not depend on the subject's actual existence.

The first objection claims that a re-created thing must be identical to the original, otherwise it is a different thing. The author points out that this reasoning is circular (it explains a thing by itself). Then he provides a key principle: judgments about possibility do not require the subject to be actual and determinate. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The objection is stated first, then the answer.
Notice how the author identifies the circularity.
2
Identify the circularity
The objection says 'this is the same as that because this is the same as that' – which is an explanation of a thing by itself. Understand why this is invalid.
3
Grasp the author's refutation
The author says we have proven that judging something as possible does not require the subject to be actual or determinate. This undercuts the need for identity.
This is a fundamental principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما هو عين ذلك الذي عدم والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فقولكم : إنما كان ذلك الذي عدم؛ لأن هذا هو عين ذلك هذا عين الشيء، تعليل للشيء بنفسه، ويكون المعنى أنه إنما كان هذا عين ما انقضى ؛ لأن هذا عين ما انقضى، ومعلوم أن هذا باطل. والجواب عن الأول : أنا قد دللنا على أن الحكم على الشيء بالجواز لا يستدعي كون المحكوم عليه متحققا متعينا في نفسه بالدلائل القاطعة. والجواب عن الثاني : أن حدوث الشيء غير مشروط بالوقت، وإلا لكان ذلك الوقت حادثاً، فيلزم افتقاره إلى وقت آخر، ويلزم التسلسل، بل حدوث المبدأ هو الذي لا يكون مسبوقا بالحدوث البتة، وحدوث المعاد هو أن يكون مسبوقا بحدوث آخر، وعلى التقديرين يزول السؤال. والجواب عن الثالث : أن أفراد النوع الواحد وإن كانت متماثلة في الماهية إلا أنه لا شك أنها غير متماثلة في الشخصية، بل كل واحد من تلك الأفراد مختص بتعينه وتشخصه، وإذا كان كذلك فلا نسلم أن نسبة هذين الجوهرين الحادثين إلى ذلك الذي عدم نسبة واحدة بل القول بأن هذه النسبة واحدة لا بعد القول يصح إلا بأن إعادة المعدوم باطلة؛ لأن بتقدير أن يصح هذا القول كان أحد هذين الحادثين هو ذلك الذي عدم والحادث الآخر ليس عينه، بل مثله، فإذن القول بأن نسبة هذين الحادثين إلى ذلك الذي عدم نسبة واحدة مبني على أن إعادة المعدوم ممتنعة، ولو بينا هذا الامتناع بهذه المقدمة لزم الدور، وأنه باطل.
One of them is the very same as that which ceased to exist, and the other is like it, the first being more appropriate than the opposite. So your statement: 'It was that which ceased to exist only because this is the very same as that' is an explanation of a thing by itself, and the meaning is that this is the very same as what passed away because this is the very same as what passed away, and it is known that this is false. The answer to the first: we have already demonstrated by conclusive proofs that judgment about a thing as possible does not require that the subject be actual and determinate in itself. The answer to the second: the origination of a thing is not conditional upon time, for otherwise that time would also be originated, necessitating its need for another time, leading to infinite regress. Rather, the origination of the beginning is that which is not preceded by any origination at all, and the origination of the return is that which is preceded by another origination, and on both assumptions the objection is removed. The answer to the third: the individuals of a single species, though similar in essence, are undoubtedly dissimilar in individuality; indeed each individual is specified by its determination and individuation. If so, we do not concede that the relation of these two originated substances to that which ceased to exist is a single relation. Rather, the statement that this relation is one is not valid except if the re-creation of the non-existent is false, because assuming this statement is valid, one of these two originated substances would be the very same as that which ceased to exist, and the other originated substance would not be its very self but its like. Therefore, the statement that the relation of these two originated substances to that which ceased to exist is one is based on the impossibility of re-creation of the non-existent. If we demonstrate this impossibility by this premise, a circle would result, and it is false.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three objections against the possibility of re-creation of the non-existent (إعادة المعدوم). The author first quotes an objection that claims one of two originated things must be identical to the original and the other similar, and that the objector's reasoning is circular. Then he gives three answers: (1) The judgment of possibility does not require the subject to actually exist; we have proven this conclusively. (2) Origination is not conditional on time, otherwise time itself would be originated, leading to infinite regress. Instead, the first origination is not preceded by any origination, and the origination of a return is preceded by another origination. (3) Individuals of one species, though similar in essence, differ in individuality. Thus the two originated substances cannot have the same relation to the original; claiming a single relation would imply the impossibility of re-creation, creating a circular argument.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
الجواز
تعليل الشيء بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that for something to be possible, it must actually exist or be determinate beforehand.
Correction: The author asserts that possibility (جواز) does not require actualization or determinacy; it is a rational judgment independent of existence.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the flaw in the first objection?
A) It assumes time is required for origination.
B) It confuses essence with individuality.
C) It explains a thing by itself (circularity).
D) It leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Possibility does not depend on the subject's actual existence; thus the first objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on the assumption that something must be 'real' or 'actual' before you can consider it possible? How does this lesson challenge that tendency in your own thinking about God's power?
?
When you reflect on the circular reasoning exposed here, can you identify any area in your own beliefs where you might be using a similar 'explanation by itself' fallacy—believing something just because it 'feels' true? What would it take to break that cycle?
?
This lesson shows that possibility does not depend on actual existence. In your life, are you limiting what you believe God can do based on what you see or experience as 'actual'? What step can you take today to trust in His absolute power beyond your current reality?

Answer to the Second Objection: Origination and Time
Refutation of the second objection: origination and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection (which ties origination to time) leads to infinite regress, and how the author distinguishes between the origination of the beginning and the origination of the return.

Core Idea
Origination is not conditional on time; otherwise time itself would require origination.

The second objection argues that re-creation would require a specific time, which itself would be originated, leading to an infinite chain. The author refutes this by explaining that origination is not inherently tied to time, and distinguishes between the first origination (unpreceded) and the origination of a return (preceded by another). This lesson clarifies how this removes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second answer. It is concise.
Notice the key terms: حدوث المبدأ and حدوث المعاد.
2
Understand the regress argument
If origination required a time, that time would also be originated, requiring another time, ad infinitum. This is the problem of infinite regress (التسلسل).
3
Grasp the distinction
The author distinguishes between the first origination (not preceded by any origination) and the return origination (preceded by a previous origination). Both are valid without invoking time.
This distinction resolves the objection.

Answer to the Third Objection: Individuality and Circularity
Refutation of the third objection: individuality and circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection about essence and individuality, and how the author refutes it by showing it is circular (دور).

Core Idea
Individuals differ in individuality even if they share essence; equality of relation is circular.

The third objection claims that two originated substances have the same relation to the original, making re-creation impossible. The author counters that individuals of a species, while similar in essence, differ in individuality. Thus the two substances cannot have the same relation; to claim otherwise would imply the impossibility of re-creation, and using that to prove impossibility is circular. This lesson explains this nuanced refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third answer carefully. It is a bit complex.
Pay attention to the terms: ماهية, شخصية, تعين, تشخص.
2
Understand the distinction between essence and individuality
The author says individuals of the same species are alike in essence but distinct in individuality (تشخص). Each has its own determination (تعين).
3
Analyze the circularity
The objection assumes a single relation between two substances and the original. The author says this assumption is only valid if re-creation is impossible, but using that to prove impossibility is circular (دور).
This logical flaw destroys the objection.

Key Takeaway
Individuals differ in individuality despite shared essence; the third objection is circular and thus invalid.

?

?
The author warns against circular reasoning. In your own life, do you ever use assumptions to prove the very thing you should be questioning? What is one belief you hold that might rest on a circular justification? Ask Allah to guide you to truth, and be willing to re-examine your foundations.
?
Consider the third objection: it assumes that similarity in essence means identity. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes assume that because you share certain rituals with other believers, your spiritual state is identical? How can you strive for true individuality in your worship and sincerity? Commit to one heartfelt prayer today that is uniquely yours.

The First Doubt: Unknown Intermediate Layers
Al-Razi presents the first doubt: unknown intermediate layers undermine tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against tawatur—that certainty requires knowledge of intermediate transmitters' essences, which is unattainable.

Core Idea
You cannot trust a report if you don't even know who transmitted it.

Before al-Razi gives his own defense of tawatur, he presents the strongest objections raised by skeptics. The first doubt questions whether we can ever be certain of a mutawatir report when we know nothing about the intermediate generations who transmitted it. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the first doubt carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the doubt builds a logical chain: to know the attributes of the intermediaries, we must first know their essences (number and existence).
Focus on the conditional structure: 'if not A, then not B'.
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is: 'knowledge of the attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence.' Ask yourself: is this always true?
This is a philosophical claim common in kalam.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the argument: we don't know the number of intermediate layers, so their essences are unknown, hence their attributes are unknown, so tawatur does not yield knowledge.
The doubt concludes that such reports give no knowledge at all.
4
Reflect on the force of the doubt
Consider how powerful this objection is. If accepted, it would undermine all historical knowledge, not just religious reports.
Al-Razi will later refute this by appealing to necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : ما لم نعلم أن جميع الطبقات التي بيننا وبين الطبقة الذين شاهدوا محمدا كانوا موصوفين بالصفات المعتبرة في كون التواتر مفيدًا للعلم، لم يمكنا القطع بصحة هذا التواتر، لكن العلم بكون هذه الطبقات المتوسطة موصوفين بصفات التواتر علم بصفة من صفاتهم، وقد ثبت ان العلم بصفة الشيء مشروط بالعلم بذاته، فإذن لا يمكننا أن نعلم أن هذه الطبقات موصوفة بهذه الصفات، إلا إذا علمنا عدد تلك الطبقات ووجودهم، لكننا لا نعلم البتة من الطبقات المتوسطة أنها كم هي ؟ وإذا كان كذلك كانت ذواتها مجهولة لنا، وإذا كانت الذوات مجهولة كانت الصفات مجهولة، فلم يكن العلم بكونهم موصوفين بالصفات المعتبرة في التواتر حاصلًا، فوجب أن لا يحصل بهذا الخبر علم، والله أعلم. الشبهة الثانية: أنا نرى المجوس على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في الشرق والغرب يخبرون عن المعجزات العظيمة لزرادشت مع أنه كان كذابًا ساحرًا عندكم. وأيضا : اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في الشرق والغرب يخبرون أن موسى قال : إن شريعتي لا تصير منسوخة البتة. وأيضا : النصارى على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في الشرق والغرب يخبرون أن عيسى كان يقول : بثلاثة ثلاثة - سبحانه وتعالى عما يشركون - وبأنه ابن الله ويخبرون ان اليهود صلبوا عيسى وقتلوه، فإن كان التواتر مفيدًا للعلم فقد صحت هذه الأخبار، ويلزم من صحتها الطعن في نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإن كانت غير مفيدة للعلم فحينئذ خرج التواتر عن كونه مفيدا للعلم. لا يقال : إنما دفعنا هذه الأقوال لقيام الدلائل العقلية على أنها باطلة، فأما التواتر عن وجود محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وعن إدعائه الرسالة فإنه لم تقم الدلالة على بطلانه، فظهر الفرق، لأنا نقول : هذا القدر يقوي كلامنا ؛ لأن خبر التواتر لما حصل في تلك الصور ثم قامت الدلائل القاطعة على بطلانها، فحينئذٍ ثبت أن الدلائل القاطعة قامت على بطلان خبر التواتر في هذه الصورة، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أنه لا يمكن الاستدلال بخبر التواتر على المخبر عنه حق وصدق. الشبهة الثالثة : الخبر المتواتر إذا كان خبرًا عن الأمور الماضية حصل احتمال مانع من القطع واليقين، وذلك الاحتمال هو أن يقال لعل واحدًا ألقى ذلك الكذب على سبيل الإرجاف، ثم إنه انتشر ذلك الإرجاف واشتهر قليلا قليلا حتى امتلأ العالم منه، ونحن نشاهد أن مثل هذا الإرجاف كثيرًا ما يحصل في زماننا، فإذا جاز ذلك في هذا الزمان ونحن نشاهد فكذا في سائر الأزمنة. لا يقال : كل ما كان كذلك فإنه لا بد أن ينكشف بالآخرة أنه كان إرجافا لأنا نقول : لم قلتم إنه لو كان الأمر واقعا على هذا الوجه لوجب أن يصل خبره إلينا؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه واقعة عظيمة والوقائع العظيمة يجب تصل إلينا أخبارها. إلا أنا نقول : هذه القاعدة منقوضة بصور منها : أن رسول الله داوم على أداء الصلوات نيفا وعشرين سنة، ثم إنه لم ينقل إلينا شيء من أحوال الصلاة، إلا برواية الآحاد ومع التعارض، ولهذا السبب اختلف المجتهدون في كيفية أداء الصلاة اختلافا شديدا لا يرجى زواله. ومنها : أن أحوال عاد وثمود وسائر الأمم وسائر أرباب الدول والملل كانت أمورًا عظيمة، ثم إنها خفيت وما وصلت إلينا أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : طالت المدة بيننا وبينهم فخفيت إلا أن هذا إنما ينفع إذا ذكرتم في ضبط المدة الطويلة، والمدة القريبة ضابطًا معلوما، حتى إن ما كان قريبا نحكم عليه بأنه لا بد أن يكون معلوما لنا ، وما كان بعيدا لا يجب فيه ذلك، إلا أن ذكر هذا الضابط كالمتعذر والممتنع. فهذه جملة الوجوه التي تمسك بها الطاعنون في التواتر. والجواب : اعلم أن العلماء اختلفوا في أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري أم نظري؟ والاختيار أنه ضروري، وذلك لأن الضروري هو الذي إذا شكك فيه صاحبه لا يتشكك، ونحن نجرب أنفسنا عند سماع هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها فنجدها جازمة بأن محمدا كان موجودا، وكان يدعي أنه رسول الله إلى الخلق، ولا نجد هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها طاعنة في ذلك الجزم وقادحة فيه، فثبت ان العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري، فكانت هذه الشبهات طاعنة في الضروريات، فلم تكن مستحقة للجواب ؛ لأن ذلك الجواب لا محالة يكون نظريا، فيتوقف الضروري عليه فيقع الدور، وهو محال، فعلمنا أن أمثال هذه الشبهات لا تستحق الجواب. وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي أن المعجزات ظهرت على وفق دعواه - فاعلم أن
If you know this, we say: As long as we do not know that all the layers between us and the layer that witnessed Muhammad were described with the attributes considered in making tawatur beneficial for knowledge, we cannot decisively affirm the correctness of this tawatur. But the knowledge that these intermediate layers are described with the attributes of tawatur is knowledge of an attribute of theirs, and it has been established that knowledge of the attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence. So then, we cannot know that these layers are described with these attributes unless we know the number of those layers and their existence, but we do not know at all of the intermediate layers how many they are. And if that is the case, their essences are unknown to us, and if essences are unknown, attributes are unknown. So the knowledge that they are described with the attributes considered in tawatur is not obtained, and therefore no knowledge is obtained through this report. And God knows best. The second doubt: We see the Magians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, reporting great miracles for Zoroaster, even though he was, according to you, a lying sorcerer. Also the Jews, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Moses said: 'My law will never be abrogated.' Also the Christians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Jesus used to say: 'By three, three' – exalted is He above what they associate – and that he is the son of God, and they report that the Jews crucified and killed Jesus. If tawatur is beneficial for knowledge, then these reports are confirmed, and from their confirmation follows impugning the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And if they are not beneficial for knowledge, then tawatur ceases to be beneficial for knowledge. It cannot be said: We repudiate these statements because rational proofs indicate they are false, whereas the tawatur about the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and his claim to messengership has no proof indicating its falsehood, so a distinction appears. For we say: This actually strengthens our argument, because the tawatur report occurred in those instances, then decisive proofs indicated their falsehood. So then it is established that decisive proofs indicated the falsehood of the tawatur report in this instance. And if the matter is so, it is established that one cannot infer from a tawatur report that its reported content is true and correct. The third doubt: The mutawatir report, if it reports about past matters, a possibility arises that prevents certainty and conviction. That possibility is that one might say: Perhaps one person cast that lie as a rumor, then that rumor spread and became famous little by little until the world was filled with it. And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time. If that is possible in this time, which we witness, then likewise in all other times. It cannot be said: Everything that is like that must eventually be uncovered as a rumor. For we say: Why do you say that if the matter were actually so, its report must reach us? The most that can be said is: It is a great event, and great events must have their reports reach us. But we say: This principle is refuted by instances, including: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) persisted in performing prayers for about twenty years, yet nothing of the circumstances of prayer reached us except through solitary reports and with contradiction. For this reason, the mujtahids differed severely in how to perform prayer, a difference whose removal is not hoped for. And among them: The circumstances of 'Ad, Thamud, and other nations, and the various rulers and kingdoms were great matters, yet they became hidden and did not reach us. The most that can be said is: The period between us and them was long, so they became hidden. But this is only useful if you mention, in delimiting the long period and the short period, a known criterion, such that what is near we judge that it must be known to us, and what is far knowledge of it is not necessary. But mentioning this criterion is nearly impossible and unattainable. This is the totality of the arguments that the detractors of tawatur have clung to. The answer: Know that the scholars differed concerning the knowledge that occurs consequent to a mutawatir report: is it necessary or acquired knowledge? The chosen opinion is that it is necessary. That is because the necessary is that which, if its possessor doubts it, he is not skeptical. We test ourselves upon hearing these doubts you have mentioned and find ourselves certain that Muhammad existed and that he claimed to be the Messenger of God to creation, and we do not find these doubts you have mentioned affecting that certainty or impugning it. So it is established that the knowledge occurring consequent to a mutawatir report is necessary knowledge. Thus these doubts attack necessary matters, so they do not deserve an answer, because that answer would inevitably be theoretical, so the necessary would become dependent on it, leading to circularity, which is impossible. Therefore we know that such doubts do not deserve an answer. As for the second premise – which is that miracles appeared in accordance with his claim – then know that
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the validity of tawatur (mutawatir reports) as a source of certain knowledge, followed by a response from al-Razi. The passage is a combination of objections and a unified refutation. The first objection (shubha) argues that to be certain of a tawatur report, one must know that all intermediate generations possess the conditions of tawatur (e.g., large numbers, impossibility of collusion). But knowledge of their attributes requires knowledge of their essences (numbers and existence), which is unknown. Therefore, certainty is impossible. The second objection points out that Magians, Jews, and Christians also have widespread reports (tawatur) about their founders' miracles and doctrines. If tawatur yields certainty, then those reports would be true, contradicting Islamic claims. If not, then tawatur is unreliable. Al-Razi anticipates a counterargument that rational proofs refute those reports, but he replies that decisive proofs have shown those reports false, thus proving that tawatur can be overridden, so it cannot guarantee truth. The third objection suggests that a lie could originate as a rumor and gradually spread until it becomes universally accepted, like some contemporary rumors. It rejects the response that such a rumor would eventually be exposed, citing examples: the detailed practices of prayer were not transmitted via tawatur but through solitary reports with disagreement, and ancient nations like 'Ad and Thamud are known only through limited reports, despite their significance. The answer then states that scholars debated whether knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari). Al-Razi chooses necessary, defining it as that which cannot be doubted. He argues that despite hearing these doubts, we are still certain of Muhammad's existence and prophethood. Therefore, these doubts attack necessary knowledge and do not deserve a theoretical response, as that would cause circularity (the necessary depending on the theoretical). The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the second premise about miracles.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
طبقات متوسطة (intermediate layers)
علم ضروري (daruri/necessary knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think tawatur simply means a report transmitted by many people, but the doubt shows it requires verifying each transmitter's reliability individually.
Correction: The doubt is not about individual verification but about the impossibility of knowing the collective state of all transmitters across generations.

Checkpoint
According to the first doubt, why can we not obtain knowledge from a mutawatir report?
A) Because the report might be fabricated.
B) Because we do not know the attributes of the intermediate layers, and that requires knowing their essences.
C) Because the number of layers is infinite.
D) Because the witnesses were not reliable.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt argues that knowledge from tawatur is impossible because we lack knowledge of the intermediate transmitters' essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran or accept a hadith, do you ever pause to consider the chain of narrators that brought it to you? How do you respond to the unsettling thought that you cannot personally verify every link?
?
If a philosopher presented you with this doubt, would your certainty in the transmitted reports of Islam be shaken? What in your heart would you rely on to overcome this intellectual challenge?
?
Are you willing to examine your own sources of religious knowledge and ask: 'Do I truly know that each generation transmitted faithfully, or am I assuming without proof?' How would you answer al-Razi's objection without merely dismissing it?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The Second Doubt: Conflicting Widespread Reports
Al-Razi presents the second doubt: other religions also have tawatur, creating a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection: that other religious groups also have widespread reports that contradict Islam, so tawatur cannot be a criterion for truth.

Core Idea
If tawatur guarantees truth, then other religions' widespread reports would also be true.

The second doubt shifts from epistemology to comparative religion. If mutawatir reports yield certain knowledge, then the universally transmitted claims of Magians, Jews, and Christians should also be true—but they contradict Islamic teachings. This seems to trap the Muslim theologian in a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the second doubt
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how al-Razi lists examples from three religious communities: Magians, Jews, and Christians.
Each report is claimed to be widely transmitted among its followers.
2
Understand the dilemma
The doubt presents a dilemma: either tawatur yields knowledge (so these reports are true, undermining Islam) or it does not (so tawatur is invalid).
The doubler thinks the Muslim cannot escape.
3
Examine the counter-argument
Al-Razi then anticipates a reply: rational proof shows those reports false, but tawatur about Muhammad has no such proof. He turns this against the opponent.
He says this actually strengthens the doubt: if decisive proofs can override tawatur, then tawatur is not a reliable source.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The doubt concludes that one cannot infer truth from tawatur alone.
This sets the stage for al-Razi's own answer.

If you know this, we say: As long as we do not know that all the layers between us and the layer that witnessed Muhammad were described with the attributes considered in making tawatur beneficial for knowledge, we cannot decisively affirm the correctness of this tawatur. But the knowledge that these intermediate layers are described with the attributes of tawatur is knowledge of an attribute of theirs, and it has been established that knowledge of the attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence. So then, we cannot know that these layers are described with these attributes unless we know the number of those layers and their existence, but we do not know at all of the intermediate layers how many they are. And if that is the case, their essences are unknown to us, and if essences are unknown, attributes are unknown. So the knowledge that they are described with the attributes considered in tawatur is not obtained, and therefore no knowledge is obtained through this report. And God knows best. The second doubt: We see the Magians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, reporting great miracles for Zoroaster, even though he was, according to you, a lying sorcerer. Also the Jews, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Moses said: 'My law will never be abrogated.' Also the Christians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Jesus used to say: 'By three, three' – exalted is He above what they associate – and that he is the son of God, and they report that the Jews crucified and killed Jesus. If tawatur is beneficial for knowledge, then these reports are confirmed, and from their confirmation follows impugning the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And if they are not beneficial for knowledge, then tawatur ceases to be beneficial for knowledge. It cannot be said: We repudiate these statements because rational proofs indicate they are false, whereas the tawatur about the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and his claim to messengership has no proof indicating its falsehood, so a distinction appears. For we say: This actually strengthens our argument, because the tawatur report occurred in those instances, then decisive proofs indicated their falsehood. So then it is established that decisive proofs indicated the falsehood of the tawatur report in this instance. And if the matter is so, it is established that one cannot infer from a tawatur report that its reported content is true and correct. The third doubt: The mutawatir report, if it reports about past matters, a possibility arises that prevents certainty and conviction. That possibility is that one might say: Perhaps one person cast that lie as a rumor, then that rumor spread and became famous little by little until the world was filled with it. And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time. If that is possible in this time, which we witness, then likewise in all other times. It cannot be said: Everything that is like that must eventually be uncovered as a rumor. For we say: Why do you say that if the matter were actually so, its report must reach us? The most that can be said is: It is a great event, and great events must have their reports reach us. But we say: This principle is refuted by instances, including: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) persisted in performing prayers for about twenty years, yet nothing of the circumstances of prayer reached us except through solitary reports and with contradiction. For this reason, the mujtahids differed severely in how to perform prayer, a difference whose removal is not hoped for. And among them: The circumstances of 'Ad, Thamud, and other nations, and the various rulers and kingdoms were great matters, yet they became hidden and did not reach us. The most that can be said is: The period between us and them was long, so they became hidden. But this is only useful if you mention, in delimiting the long period and the short period, a known criterion, such that what is near we judge that it must be known to us, and what is far knowledge of it is not necessary. But mentioning this criterion is nearly impossible and unattainable. This is the totality of the arguments that the detractors of tawatur have clung to. The answer: Know that the scholars differed concerning the knowledge that occurs consequent to a mutawatir report: is it necessary or acquired knowledge? The chosen opinion is that it is necessary. That is because the necessary is that which, if its possessor doubts it, he is not skeptical. We test ourselves upon hearing these doubts you have mentioned and find ourselves certain that Muhammad existed and that he claimed to be the Messenger of God to creation, and we do not find these doubts you have mentioned affecting that certainty or impugning it. So it is established that the knowledge occurring consequent to a mutawatir report is necessary knowledge. Thus these doubts attack necessary matters, so they do not deserve an answer, because that answer would inevitably be theoretical, so the necessary would become dependent on it, leading to circularity, which is impossible. Therefore we know that such doubts do not deserve an answer. As for the second premise – which is that miracles appeared in accordance with his claim – then know that
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the validity of tawatur (mutawatir reports) as a source of certain knowledge, followed by a response from al-Razi. The passage is a combination of objections and a unified refutation. The first objection (shubha) argues that to be certain of a tawatur report, one must know that all intermediate generations possess the conditions of tawatur (e.g., large numbers, impossibility of collusion). But knowledge of their attributes requires knowledge of their essences (numbers and existence), which is unknown. Therefore, certainty is impossible. The second objection points out that Magians, Jews, and Christians also have widespread reports (tawatur) about their founders' miracles and doctrines. If tawatur yields certainty, then those reports would be true, contradicting Islamic claims. If not, then tawatur is unreliable. Al-Razi anticipates a counterargument that rational proofs refute those reports, but he replies that decisive proofs have shown those reports false, thus proving that tawatur can be overridden, so it cannot guarantee truth. The third objection suggests that a lie could originate as a rumor and gradually spread until it becomes universally accepted, like some contemporary rumors. It rejects the response that such a rumor would eventually be exposed, citing examples: the detailed practices of prayer were not transmitted via tawatur but through solitary reports with disagreement, and ancient nations like 'Ad and Thamud are known only through limited reports, despite their significance. The answer then states that scholars debated whether knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari). Al-Razi chooses necessary, defining it as that which cannot be doubted. He argues that despite hearing these doubts, we are still certain of Muhammad's existence and prophethood. Therefore, these doubts attack necessary knowledge and do not deserve a theoretical response, as that would cause circularity (the necessary depending on the theoretical). The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the second premise about miracles.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
مجوس (Magians)
يهود (Jews)
نصارى (Christians)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the widespread nature of a report by itself guarantees its truth.
Correction: This doubt shows that widespread reports from other religions exist but are false, so tawatur alone cannot guarantee truth.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second doubt?
A) Tawatur is the only way to know the truth about Muhammad.
B) Rational proofs are always superior to tawatur.
C) Tawatur cannot be used to prove the truth of its content because it can be overridden by proofs.
D) The Magians, Jews, and Christians are all correct in their reports.

Key Takeaway
The second doubt uses conflicting widespread reports to argue that tawatur cannot be an independent criterion for truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your faith that you have never critically examined. Are you holding it because it is widely accepted among Muslims, or because you have rational evidence for it? What would you do if you found a rational argument that seemed to contradict it?
?
The doubler uses the existence of widespread false reports to argue that tawatur is not reliable. In your own life, have you ever been convinced of something simply because 'everyone says so'? What belief are you most vulnerable to accepting without investigation?
?
If you were challenged to defend the truth of Islam using only reason and not appeals to authority or widespread acceptance, how confident would you be? What steps can you take this week to strengthen your rational understanding of a key belief?

The Third Doubt: Rumors Spreading Widely
Al-Razi presents the third doubt: rumors can become widespread, and not all great events are known via tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection: that a lie can spread as a rumor and become universally accepted, so tawatur does not guarantee truth.

Core Idea
A single lie can snowball into a widespread belief, so popularity isn't proof.

The third doubt is a practical one: we see rumors (إرجاف) start from a single source and gradually spread until everyone accepts them. Could the reports about Muhammad's prophethood be such a rumor? The doubter also argues that even great events can be forgotten or not transmitted widely, using examples from Islamic prayer practices and ancient nations.

Guided Path
1
Read the third doubt
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the rumor possibility and the examples showing that not all significant events are widely transmitted.
The doubt is cumulative: first the rumor possibility, then the examples.
2
Understand the rumor argument
A lie (إرجاف) can start from one person and spread gradually until it fills the world. This is observable in our own time.
If this can happen now, it could have happened in the past.
3
Examine the counter-argument anticipated
The doubter expects the response: such a rumor would eventually be exposed. He rejects this by asking: why must news of exposure reach us?
He then gives counterexamples.
4
Review the counterexamples
Detailed prayer practices were not transmitted via tawatur but through solitary reports with disagreement. Also, ancient nations like 'Ad and Thamud are not fully known.
These show that lack of tawatur does not mean the event didn't happen, but also that tawatur is not necessary for great events.
5
Reflect on the difficulty of a criterion
The doubter says it's impossible to define a clear time limit for when events should be known.
This undermines any attempt to say 'this event is too great to be forgotten.'

If you know this, we say: As long as we do not know that all the layers between us and the layer that witnessed Muhammad were described with the attributes considered in making tawatur beneficial for knowledge, we cannot decisively affirm the correctness of this tawatur. But the knowledge that these intermediate layers are described with the attributes of tawatur is knowledge of an attribute of theirs, and it has been established that knowledge of the attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence. So then, we cannot know that these layers are described with these attributes unless we know the number of those layers and their existence, but we do not know at all of the intermediate layers how many they are. And if that is the case, their essences are unknown to us, and if essences are unknown, attributes are unknown. So the knowledge that they are described with the attributes considered in tawatur is not obtained, and therefore no knowledge is obtained through this report. And God knows best. The second doubt: We see the Magians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, reporting great miracles for Zoroaster, even though he was, according to you, a lying sorcerer. Also the Jews, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Moses said: 'My law will never be abrogated.' Also the Christians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Jesus used to say: 'By three, three' – exalted is He above what they associate – and that he is the son of God, and they report that the Jews crucified and killed Jesus. If tawatur is beneficial for knowledge, then these reports are confirmed, and from their confirmation follows impugning the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And if they are not beneficial for knowledge, then tawatur ceases to be beneficial for knowledge. It cannot be said: We repudiate these statements because rational proofs indicate they are false, whereas the tawatur about the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and his claim to messengership has no proof indicating its falsehood, so a distinction appears. For we say: This actually strengthens our argument, because the tawatur report occurred in those instances, then decisive proofs indicated their falsehood. So then it is established that decisive proofs indicated the falsehood of the tawatur report in this instance. And if the matter is so, it is established that one cannot infer from a tawatur report that its reported content is true and correct. The third doubt: The mutawatir report, if it reports about past matters, a possibility arises that prevents certainty and conviction. That possibility is that one might say: Perhaps one person cast that lie as a rumor, then that rumor spread and became famous little by little until the world was filled with it. And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time. If that is possible in this time, which we witness, then likewise in all other times. It cannot be said: Everything that is like that must eventually be uncovered as a rumor. For we say: Why do you say that if the matter were actually so, its report must reach us? Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the validity of tawatur (mutawatir reports) as a source of certain knowledge, followed by a response from al-Razi. The passage is a combination of objections and a unified refutation. The first objection (shubha) argues that to be certain of a tawatur report, one must know that all intermediate generations possess the conditions of tawatur (e.g., large numbers, impossibility of collusion). But knowledge of their attributes requires knowledge of their essences (numbers and existence), which is unknown. Therefore, certainty is impossible. The second objection points out that Magians, Jews, and Christians also have widespread reports (tawatur) about their founders' miracles and doctrines. If tawatur yields certainty, then those reports would be true, contradicting Islamic claims. If not, then tawatur is unreliable. Al-Razi anticipates a counterargument that rational proofs refute those reports, but he replies that decisive proofs have shown those reports false, thus proving that tawatur can be overridden, so it cannot guarantee truth. The third objection suggests that a lie could originate as a rumor and gradually spread until it becomes universally accepted, like some contemporary rumors. It rejects the response that such a rumor would eventually be exposed, citing examples: the detailed practices of prayer were not transmitted via tawatur but through solitary reports with disagreement, and ancient nations like 'Ad and Thamud are known only through limited reports, despite their significance. The answer then states that scholars debated whether knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari). Al-Razi chooses necessary, defining it as that which cannot be doubted. He argues that despite hearing these doubts, we are still certain of Muhammad's existence and prophethood. Therefore, these doubts attack necessary knowledge and do not deserve a theoretical response, as that would cause circularity (the necessary depending on the theoretical). The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the second premise about miracles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that if a report is widely accepted, it must be true, and if an event is great, it must be widely known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How do you handle uncertainty in your own religious practice? Do you demand absolute certainty before acting, or can you live with ambiguity? Which approach is more faithful?

Al-Razi's Answer: Necessary Knowledge from Tawatur
Al-Razi refutes the three doubts by asserting that knowledge from tawatur is necessary and beyond theoretical challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's response to the three doubts: that knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri) and therefore immune to theoretical refutation.

Core Idea
You can't argue against what you already know for certain.

After presenting the skeptics' arguments, al-Razi gives his answer. He does not engage the doubts point by point. Instead, he makes a bold claim: knowledge from tawatur is necessary (ضروري), meaning it is self-evident and cannot be shaken by theoretical arguments. Any attempt to refute it with reasoning would lead to circularity. Let's see how he defends this.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key definitions: necessary knowledge is that which cannot be doubted.
Al-Razi uses self-reflection as evidence.
2
Understand the self-test
He says: when we hear these doubts, we find ourselves still certain that Muhammad existed and claimed prophethood. The doubts do not affect that certainty.
This is an appeal to introspection.
3
Grasp the circularity argument
If we tried to answer the doubts with theoretical reasoning, the necessary (self-evident) knowledge would depend on that reasoning, causing circularity (دور).
Circularity is logically impossible, so the doubts do not deserve a response.
4
Note the transition
The passage ends by transitioning to the second premise about miracles, which is not covered in this lesson.
The answer here focuses solely on the necessary nature of tawatur knowledge.

يشركون - وبأنه ابن الله ويخبرون ان اليهود صلبوا عيسى وقتلوه، فإن كان التواتر مفيدًا للعلم فقد صحت هذه الأخبار، ويلزم من صحتها الطعن في نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإن كانت غير مفيدة للعلم فحينئذ خرج التواتر عن كونه مفيدا للعلم. لا يقال : إنما دفعنا هذه الأقوال لقيام الدلائل العقلية على أنها باطلة، فأما التواتر عن وجود محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وعن إدعائه الرسالة فإنه لم تقم الدلالة على بطلانه، فظهر الفرق، لأنا نقول : هذا القدر يقوي كلامنا ؛ لأن خبر التواتر لما حصل في تلك الصور ثم قامت الدلائل القاطعة على بطلانها، فحينئذٍ ثبت أن الدلائل القاطعة قامت على بطلان خبر التواتر في هذه الصورة، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أنه لا يمكن الاستدلال بخبر التواتر على المخبر عنه حق وصدق. الشبهة الثالثة : الخبر المتواتر إذا كان خبرًا عن الأمور الماضية حصل احتمال مانع من القطع واليقين، وذلك الاحتمال هو أن يقال لعل واحدًا ألقى ذلك الكذب على سبيل الإرجاف، ثم إنه انتشر ذلك الإرجاف واشتهر قليلا قليلا حتى امتلأ العالم منه، ونحن نشاهد أن مثل هذا الإرجاف كثيرًا ما يحصل في زماننا، فإذا جاز ذلك في هذا الزمان ونحن نشاهد فكذا في سائر الأزمنة. لا يقال : كل ما كان كذلك فإنه لا بد أن ينكشف بالآخرة أنه كان إرجافا لأنا نقول : لم قلتم إنه لو كان الأمر واقعا على هذا الوجه لوجب أن يصل خبره إلينا؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه واقعة عظيمة والوقائع العظيمة يجب تصل إلينا أخبارها. إلا أنا نقول : هذه القاعدة منقوضة بصور منها : أن رسول الله داوم على أداء الصلوات نيفا وعشرين سنة، ثم إنه لم ينقل إلينا شيء من أحوال الصلاة، إلا برواية الآحاد ومع التعارض، ولهذا السبب اختلف المجتهدون في كيفية أداء الصلاة اختلافا شديدا لا يرجى زواله. ومنها : أن أحوال عاد وثمود وسائر الأمم وسائر أرباب الدول والملل كانت أمورًا عظيمة، ثم إنها خفيت وما وصلت إلينا أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : طالت المدة بيننا وبينهم فخفيت إلا أن هذا إنما ينفع إذا ذكرتم في ضبط المدة الطويلة، والمدة القريبة ضابطًا معلوما، حتى إن ما كان قريبا نحكم عليه بأنه لا بد أن يكون معلوما لنا ، وما كان بعيدا لا يجب فيه ذلك، إلا أن ذكر هذا الضابط كالمتعذر والممتنع. فهذه جملة الوجوه التي تمسك بها الطاعنون في التواتر. والجواب : اعلم أن العلماء اختلفوا في أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري أم نظري؟ والاختيار أنه ضروري، وذلك لأن الضروري هو الذي إذا شكك فيه صاحبه لا يتشكك، ونحن نجرب أنفسنا عند سماع هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها فنجدها جازمة بأن محمدا كان موجودا، وكان يدعي أنه رسول الله إلى الخلق، ولا نجد هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها طاعنة في ذلك الجزم وقادحة فيه، فثبت ان العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري، فكانت هذه الشبهات طاعنة في الضروريات، فلم تكن مستحقة للجواب ؛ لأن ذلك الجواب لا محالة يكون نظريا، فيتوقف الضروري عليه فيقع الدور، وهو محال، فعلمنا أن أمثال هذه الشبهات لا تستحق الجواب. وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي أن المعجزات ظهرت على وفق دعواه - فاعلم أن
If you know this, we say: As long as we do not know that all the layers between us and the layer that witnessed Muhammad were described with the attributes considered in making tawatur beneficial for knowledge, we cannot decisively affirm the correctness of this tawatur. But the knowledge that these intermediate layers are described with the attributes of tawatur is knowledge of an attribute of theirs, and it has been established that knowledge of the attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence. So then, we cannot know that these layers are described with these attributes unless we know the number of those layers and their existence, but we do not know at all of the intermediate layers how many they are. And if that is the case, their essences are unknown to us, and if essences are unknown, attributes are unknown. So the knowledge that they are described with the attributes considered in tawatur is not obtained, and therefore no knowledge is obtained through this report. And God knows best. The second doubt: We see the Magians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, reporting great miracles for Zoroaster, even though he was, according to you, a lying sorcerer. Also the Jews, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Moses said: 'My law will never be abrogated.' Also the Christians, despite their multitude and dispersion in east and west, report that Jesus used to say: 'By three, three' – exalted is He above what they associate – and that he is the son of God, and they report that the Jews crucified and killed Jesus. If tawatur is beneficial for knowledge, then these reports are confirmed, and from their confirmation follows impugning the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And if they are not beneficial for knowledge, then tawatur ceases to be beneficial for knowledge. It cannot be said: We repudiate these statements because rational proofs indicate they are false, whereas the tawatur about the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and his claim to messengership has no proof indicating its falsehood, so a distinction appears. For we say: This actually strengthens our argument, because the tawatur report occurred in those instances, then decisive proofs indicated their falsehood. So then it is established that decisive proofs indicated the falsehood of the tawatur report in this instance. And if the matter is so, it is established that one cannot infer from a tawatur report that its reported content is true and correct. The third doubt: The mutawatir report, if it reports about past matters, a possibility arises that prevents certainty and conviction. That possibility is that one might say: Perhaps one person cast that lie as a rumor, then that rumor spread and became famous little by little until the world was filled with it. And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time. If that is possible in this time, which we witness, then likewise in all other times. It cannot be said: Everything that is like that must eventually be uncovered as a rumor. For we say: Why do you say that if the matter were actually so, its report must reach us? The most that can be said is: It is a great event, and great events must have their reports reach us. But we say: This principle is refuted by instances, including: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) persisted in performing prayers for about twenty years, yet nothing of the circumstances of prayer reached us except through solitary reports and with contradiction. For this reason, the mujtahids differed severely in how to perform prayer, a difference whose removal is not hoped for. And among them: The circumstances of 'Ad, Thamud, and other nations, and the various rulers and kingdoms were great matters, yet they became hidden and did not reach us. The most that can be said is: The period between us and them was long, so they became hidden. But this is only useful if you mention, in delimiting the long period and the short period, a known criterion, such that what is near we judge that it must be known to us, and what is far knowledge of it is not necessary. But mentioning this criterion is nearly impossible and unattainable. This is the totality of the arguments that the detractors of tawatur have clung to. The answer: Know that the scholars differed concerning the knowledge that occurs consequent to a mutawatir report: is it necessary or acquired knowledge? The chosen opinion is that it is necessary. That is because the necessary is that which, if its possessor doubts it, he is not skeptical. We test ourselves upon hearing these doubts you have mentioned and find ourselves certain that Muhammad existed and that he claimed to be the Messenger of God to creation, and we do not find these doubts you have mentioned affecting that certainty or impugning it. So it is established that the knowledge occurring consequent to a mutawatir report is necessary knowledge. Thus these doubts attack necessary matters, so they do not deserve an answer, because that answer would inevitably be theoretical, so the necessary would become dependent on it, leading to circularity, which is impossible. Therefore we know that such doubts do not deserve an answer. As for the second premise – which is that miracles appeared in accordance with his claim – then know that
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the validity of tawatur (mutawatir reports) as a source of certain knowledge, followed by a response from al-Razi. The passage is a combination of objections and a unified refutation. The first objection (shubha) argues that to be certain of a tawatur report, one must know that all intermediate generations possess the conditions of tawatur (e.g., large numbers, impossibility of collusion). But knowledge of their attributes requires knowledge of their essences (numbers and existence), which is unknown. Therefore, certainty is impossible. The second objection points out that Magians, Jews, and Christians also have widespread reports (tawatur) about their founders' miracles and doctrines. If tawatur yields certainty, then those reports would be true, contradicting Islamic claims. If not, then tawatur is unreliable. Al-Razi anticipates a counterargument that rational proofs refute those reports, but he replies that decisive proofs have shown those reports false, thus proving that tawatur can be overridden, so it cannot guarantee truth. The third objection suggests that a lie could originate as a rumor and gradually spread until it becomes universally accepted, like some contemporary rumors. It rejects the response that such a rumor would eventually be exposed, citing examples: the detailed practices of prayer were not transmitted via tawatur but through solitary reports with disagreement, and ancient nations like 'Ad and Thamud are known only through limited reports, despite their significance. The answer then states that scholars debated whether knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari). Al-Razi chooses necessary, defining it as that which cannot be doubted. He argues that despite hearing these doubts, we are still certain of Muhammad's existence and prophethood. Therefore, these doubts attack necessary knowledge and do not deserve a theoretical response, as that would cause circularity (the necessary depending on the theoretical). The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the second premise about miracles.

Key Terms
علم ضروري (daruri/necessary knowledge)
علم نظري (nazari/theoretical knowledge)
دور (circularity)
جازم (certain)
شبهة (doubt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-Razi ignores the doubts or dismisses them without reason.
Correction: He gives a principled reason: the knowledge is necessary, so any theoretical answer would create circularity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why do the three doubts against tawatur not deserve a theoretical answer?
A) Because they are too weak to consider.
B) Because knowledge from tawatur is necessary, and a theoretical answer would lead to circularity.
C) Because the doubts have already been answered by earlier scholars.
D) Because tawatur is a matter of faith, not reason.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's answer: Knowledge from tawatur is necessary (self-evident), so doubts that attack it are invalid and do not require a theoretical response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear doubts about the prophethood of Muhammad (ﷺ) — whether from external critics or internal whisperings — do you find that your heart remains as certain as al-Razi describes, or do you feel shaken? What does your reaction reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
Al-Razi says that necessary knowledge cannot be doubted by its possessor. Have you ever experienced a moment of genuine certainty in your faith that felt so solid that no argument could move you? If not, what might be missing in your relationship with that truth?
?
If you were to honestly examine your own belief in the prophethood of Muhammad (ﷺ) right now, would you say it is necessary (self-evident) or theoretical (based on proofs)? What would it take for you to move from theoretical to necessary certainty — and what is stopping you?

Razi's Two Interpretations of Yusuf's 'Intention'
Razi analyzes the meaning of 'hamm' and offers two interpretations that exonerate Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi linguistically and theologically interprets the word 'hamm' to exonerate Prophet Yusuf.

Core Idea
The word 'hamm' does not necessarily imply sin; it can mean any intention.

Razi carefully analyzes the word 'hamm' in the story of Yusuf. He argues that the verse does not specify the action, and offers two plausible interpretations that clear Yusuf of any wrongdoing. The first involves a protective action, the second is a grammatical reordering.

Guided Path
1
Define 'hamm'
Read Razi's definition: 'hamm' is determination (azm). Note that determination cannot be attached to a person's essence but only to an action.
This sets up the logical argument.
2
Follow the logical argument
Razi reasons that since the action is not mentioned, it is not necessarily fornication. The verse leaves the action unspecified.
3
Examine the first interpretation
Yusuf intended to push her away. Understand the potential objection and Razi's response about the divine proof showing harmful consequences.
This interpretation is about self-protection.
4
Examine the second interpretation
The sentence involves 'taqdim wa ta'khir' (advancement and delay), meaning the actual order is 'she intended him, and if not for the proof, he would have intended her'.
This is a common Arabic literary device.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الهم هو العزم، ولا يمكن تعلق العزم بذاتها ؛ لأن الذوات في حال بقائها لا تتعلق الإرادات بها، بل لا بد من تعلق العزم بإحداث فعل في الذات، وذلك الفعل غير مذكور، فليس القول بأن المراد هو الهم بالزنى أولى من القول بأن المراد هو الهم بفعل آخر. ولنا فيه وجهان : الأول: أن يحمل على أنه هم بأن يدفعها عن نفسه. ولا يقال: فأي فائدة على هذا التأويل في قوله تعالى : ﴿لَوْلَا أَن رَّأَى بُرْهَانَ رَبِّهِ﴾ [يوسف: ٢٤] فإن دفعها عن النفس واجب والبرهان لا يصرف عنه؛ لأنا نقول : يجوز أن يكون لما هم به أراه الله برهانا على أنه لو أقدم على ما هم به لأهلكه أهلها وقتلوه أو أنها تدعي عليه المراودة على القبيح وتنسبه إلى أنه دعاها إلى نفسه وضربها لأجل امتناعها منه، فأخبر الله تعالى أنه صرف بالبرهان السوء عنه والفحشاء، وهو القتل والمكروه أو ظن القبيح واعتقاده فيه. والثاني: أن يحمل هذا الكلام على التقدم والتأخر، والمعنى: ولقد همت به، ولولا أن رأى برهان ربه لهم بها، وهذا يجري مجرى قولهم : قد كنت هلكت لولا أن تداركتك، واستبعد الزجاج هذا الجواب من وجهين :
If this is established, we say: 'hamm' (intention) is determination, and it is impossible for determination to be attached to the essence itself, because essences in their state of remaining do not receive wills. Rather, determination must be attached to producing an action in the essence. That action is not mentioned, so it is not more likely that the intended meaning is the intention of fornication than that the intended meaning is the intention of some other action. We have two aspects: First: that it is interpreted as meaning he intended to push her away from himself. And one may not say: What benefit is there in this interpretation for the Almighty's saying: 'If not that he saw the proof of his Lord'? Because pushing her away from oneself is obligatory, and the proof does not turn one away from it. For we say: It is possible that when he intended what he intended, God showed him a proof that if he proceeded with what he intended, her family would destroy him and kill him, or that she would claim that he sought to seduce her to evil and accuse him of inviting her to himself and hitting her because she refused him. So God informed that He turned away evil and immorality from him through the proof, and that is being killed and harmed, or the assumption of evil and belief in it. Second: that this statement is interpreted as advancement and delay (taqdim wa ta'khir), the meaning being: 'And she certainly intended him, and if not that he saw the proof of his Lord, he would have intended her.' This is like their saying: 'You would have perished had I not saved you.' And al-Zajjaj rejected this answer on two grounds:
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the word 'hamm' in the verse. Razi first defines 'hamm' as 'determination' (azm). He argues that determination cannot be directed to a person's essence (dhat) but only to an action. Since the verse does not specify the action, it is not necessarily the intention to commit fornication; it could be any action. Then he gives two possible interpretations. The first: Yusuf intended to push her away. The objection is that pushing her away is obligatory, so why would a divine proof prevent him? Razi answers that the proof showed him the harmful consequences of such an action (e.g., being killed, accused). The second interpretation is a grammatical one: the sentence involves 'taqdim wa ta'khir' (advanced and delayed order), meaning the protasis and apodosis are rearranged, so the intended meaning is 'she intended him, and if not for the proof, he would have intended her.' This is a common literary device. Razi notes that al-Zajjaj (a grammarian) rejected this interpretation, but the reasons are not given here.

Key Terms
الهم
العزم
الذات
التقديم والتأخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'hamm' in the verse necessarily implies that Yusuf intended to commit fornication with Potiphar's wife.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that 'hamm' is a general term for intention or determination, and the verse does not specify the action. He offers two alternative interpretations that do not involve sin.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first interpretation, what action did Yusuf intend?
A) Fornication
B) To push her away
C) To run away
D) To call for help

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that careful linguistic and contextual analysis can exonerate prophets from apparent accusations, showing that 'hamm' is not inherently sinful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed the worst intention from someone's words or actions, without considering that the 'hamm' could be something else? How can Razi's linguistic caution change your approach to judging others?
?
In your own life, are you quick to label intentions as sinful based on limited evidence? What would it cost you to pause and consider the possibility of a different, even righteous, intention?
?
The verse shows that even a prophet's intended action could be misinterpreted. How does this challenge your tendency to assume you fully understand another person's motives? Will you commit to seeking out alternative explanations before forming judgments?

Resolving Pronoun Ambiguity in the Story of Sulayman
Razi examines four possible pronoun referents and rejects three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi systematically evaluates possible referents of ambiguous pronouns in the Quranic account of Prophet Sulayman and the horses.

Core Idea
Careful pronoun analysis protects prophetic honor.

The Quranic story of Sulayman and the horses contains ambiguous pronouns. If misinterpreted, they could suggest that the prophet sought miracles while neglecting prayer, which is unworthy of a prophet. Imam Razi examines four possible interpretations of the pronouns, rejecting three, to preserve the dignity of the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on pronoun ambiguity
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi begins by dismissing a false interpretation that would imply Sulayman sought great miracles while committing great sins.
This sets the stage for the pronoun analysis.
2
Identify the four pronoun probabilities
Razi lists four possibilities for what the two pronouns refer to. List them in order: (1) both to the sun (false), (2) first to sun and second to horses (majority view, far-fetched), (3) first to horses and second to sun (unattested). Note that the first probability he already dismissed, and the third and fourth are discussed.
The second probability is not explicitly listed? Actually he starts with saying 'هذا الاحتمال' referring to the one he just dismissed, then third and fourth. So the first probability is the one he dismissed at the start. So we have three total? That's fine.
3
Understand Razi's evaluations
For each probability, Razi gives a reason for rejection: the first leads to an absurd statement, the third is far-fetched because it distinguishes between two pronouns in the same context, and the fourth has no proponents. Conclude that the correct interpretation must be something else, which he will provide later.
This shows the rigorous method of Quranic exegesis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية والثلاثون

يلتمس من ربه أعظم المعجزات عند إقدامه على أعظم الذنوب، وهو ترك الصلاة - بمثل هذه العبارة التي لا تستعمل ولا تذكر إلا مع العبيد وأراذل الخدم؟ فعلمنا أن هذا الاحتمال - وهو القول بعود هذين الضميرين إلى الشمس - باطل.

والاحتمال الثالث : أن يعود الضمير الأول إلى الشمس، والثاني إلى الصافنات، وهو قول الأكثرين كأنه قيل : " حتى توارت" "الشمس" بالحجاب ، ردوا " الصافات، وهذا بعيد ؛ لأنهما ضميران وردا في وضع واحد، فتفريقهما خلاف الأصل. الاحتمال الرابع: أن يعود الضمير الأول إلى الصافنات، والثاني إلى الشمس، وهذا لم يقل به أحد ثم قال: فَطَفِقَ مَسْطا بِالسُّوقِ وَالْأَعْنَاقِ [ ص: ۳۳] أي فجعل يمسح سوقها وأعناقها مسحا.

وقال الأكثرون : المعنى أنه كان يمسح السيف بسوقها وأعناقها ـ أي يقطعها ـ وهذا أيضًا بعيد لأنه لو كان المسح بالسوق والأعناق هو القطع لكان القائل إذا قال: مسحت رأس فلان فُهِم منه أنه قطعه ولكان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة : ٦] يفيد القطع، بل قيل : مسح الرأس بالسيف فربما منه ضرب العنق، فأما إذا لم يذكر السيف لم يفهم منه القطع البتة.

فأما إذا لم يذكر السيف لم يفهم منه القطع البتة. كما إذا عرفت تفسير الآية فنقول : إن رباط الخيل كان مندوبا إليه في دينهم، في ديننا، فجلس سليمان لتعرض عليه الخيل، ثم بين ان ذلك ليس بسبب حب الدنيا، بل لأن الله تعالى أمره بذلك ثم أمر بإعدادها حتى توارت بالحجاب ـ أي حتى غابت عن بصره - ثم أمر بردها، فلما وصلت إليه أخذ يمسح سوقها وأعناقها إما لأجل تشريفها وإظهار شدة رحمته عليها ، أو إظهارًا لكونها من أعظم الأعوان في دفع العدو ؛ أو لأنه أراد أن يظهر من نفسه أنه في ضبط مصالح دينه ودنياه، بحيث يتكفلها بنفس، ولا يفوضها إلى أحد أو لأنه كان أعلم بأحوال الخيل من غيره، فكان يمتحنها ويمسحها ليعلم حالها في الصحة والسقم وهذا الذي ذكرناه كلام ينطبق عليه لفظ القرآن، وفي تعظيم أحوال الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - فيكون أولى مما يكون بالضد من ذلك.
The thirty-second issue: He seeks from his Lord the greatest miracles when he commits the greatest sins, which is abandoning prayer - with such an expression that is not used nor mentioned except with slaves and the lowest servants? So we know that this probability - which is saying that these two pronouns refer to the sun - is false. And the third probability: that the first pronoun refers to the sun, and the second to the horses, which is the saying of the majority, as if it was said: 'until the sun disappeared behind the veil,' they returned the horses, and this is far-fetched because they are two pronouns occurring in one context, so differentiating between them is contrary to the principle. The fourth probability: that the first pronoun refers to the horses and the second to the sun, and no one has said this. Then He said: 'Then he began to pass (his hand) over the legs and necks' [Sad:33], i.e. he began to wipe their legs and necks with a wipe. And the majority said: The meaning is that he was wiping the sword over their legs and necks - i.e. cutting them - and this is also far-fetched, because if wiping over the legs and necks meant cutting, then if someone said: 'I wiped the head of so-and-so' it would be understood that he cut it, and the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And wipe your heads and your feet' [Al-Ma'idah:6] would imply cutting. Rather, it is said: wiping the head with a sword possibly results in striking the neck. But if the sword is not mentioned, cutting is absolutely not understood. So if you have understood the interpretation of the verse, we say: The keeping of horses was recommended in their religion and in our religion, so Sulayman sat for the horses to be displayed to him, then he explained that this was not due to love of the world, but because Allah commanded him to do so. Then he ordered their preparation until they disappeared behind the veil - i.e. until they were hidden from his sight - then he ordered them to be returned. When they reached him, he began to wipe their legs and necks, either to honor them and show intense mercy upon them, or to demonstrate that they are among the greatest aids in repelling the enemy, or because he wanted to show that he is in control of the interests of his religion and his world, so that he undertakes them himself and does not delegate them to anyone, or because he was more knowledgeable about horses than others, so he examined them and wiped them to know their condition in health and sickness. And this which we have mentioned is speech that matches the wording of the Quran, and in glorifying the states of the prophets - prayers and peace of Allah be upon them - it is more appropriate than the opposite.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a detailed interpretation and defense of the account of Prophet Sulayman and the horses from Surah Sad. The author begins by rejecting a false interpretation that would imply the prophet sought great miracles while committing great sins (abandoning prayer). He then systematically examines the possible referents of two pronouns in the verse, concluding that the second and third probabilities are far-fetched and the fourth is unattested. He then clarifies the meaning of 'مسح' (wiping), arguing that without mentioning a sword, it cannot mean cutting, as shown by the Quranic verse about ablution. Finally, he presents the correct interpretation: Sulayman ordered the horses to be displayed because Allah commanded him, not out of love of the world. When they were presented, he wiped their legs and necks out of honor, mercy, or to examine their health, all of which glorifies the prophet and is consistent with the Quranic wording.

Key Terms
الضمير (pronoun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Sulayman sought great miracles while committing great sins.
Correction: Razi shows that such an interpretation is impossible because the language used is only fitting for slaves, not prophets.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the interpretation that both pronouns refer to the sun false?
A) It implies Sulayman was a sinner.
B) It uses language only appropriate for servants.
C) It contradicts the majority.
D) It is linguistically impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi systematically rejects three interpretations of ambiguous pronouns to protect the prophet's honor, demonstrating the importance of careful linguistic analysis in Quranic exegesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you consider the precise wording and grammatical structure, or do you sometimes settle for a surface-level understanding? This lesson shows how a single pronoun can change the entire meaning. Choose one verse you often recite and commit to studying its tafsir with careful attention to language. Write down that verse and one step you will take this week.
?
Razi was willing to reject a majority interpretation (the third probability) because it violated a linguistic principle. Are you willing to question commonly held views if the evidence demands it, even if it means standing alone? Identify a belief you accepted without scrutiny because 'everyone says so.' What would it cost you to re-examine it?
?
Imagine you are explaining this verse to someone and they suggest the first interpretation. How would you feel? Would you be quick to defend the prophet's honor, or would you be indifferent? This lesson calls us to be vigilant in protecting the dignity of Allah's chosen messengers. How will you actively guard against careless interpretations in your own speech and teaching?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The True Meaning of 'Wiping' in Sulayman's Action
Razi refutes the cutting interpretation and offers a noble interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that the word 'مسح' (wiping) in the story of Sulayman cannot mean cutting, and what the correct interpretation is.

Core Idea
Linguistic context determines meaning, not arbitrary assumptions.

The verse says Sulayman 'wiped' the legs and necks of the horses. Some scholars interpreted this as cutting them with a sword, but Razi challenges this by examining the Quranic usage of 'مسح'. He shows that without mentioning a sword, 'مسح' never means cutting, as demonstrated by the verse on ablution. He then provides several noble reasons for Sulayman's action, all of which glorify the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the meaning of 'مسح'
Read the Arabic passage from 'فَطَفِقَ مَسْطا' to the end. Note how Razi first refutes the interpretation that it means cutting.
Pay attention to his linguistic argument.
2
Follow Razi's linguistic argument
Razi argues that if 'مسح' alone meant cutting, then the Quranic command 'وامسحوا برؤوسكم' would imply cutting the head, which is false. He shows that 'مسح' requires an instrument to imply cutting. Since no instrument is mentioned here, it cannot mean cutting.
This is a logical argument from Quranic usage.
3
Understand Razi's positive interpretation
Razi then explains that Sulayman's wiping was an act of honoring, showing mercy, demonstrating the horses' importance in defending against enemies, or examining their health. All these interpretations glorify the prophet and are consistent with the Quran.
This shows Razi's commitment to upholding prophetic dignity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية والثلاثون

يلتمس من ربه أعظم المعجزات عند إقدامه على أعظم الذنوب، وهو ترك الصلاة - بمثل هذه العبارة التي لا تستعمل ولا تذكر إلا مع العبيد وأراذل الخدم؟ فعلمنا أن هذا الاحتمال - وهو القول بعود هذين الضميرين إلى الشمس - باطل.

والاحتمال الثالث : أن يعود الضمير الأول إلى الشمس، والثاني إلى الصافنات، وهو قول الأكثرين كأنه قيل : " حتى توارت" "الشمس" بالحجاب ، ردوا " الصافات، وهذا بعيد ؛ لأنهما ضميران وردا في وضع واحد، فتفريقهما خلاف الأصل. الاحتمال الرابع: أن يعود الضمير الأول إلى الصافنات، والثاني إلى الشمس، وهذا لم يقل به أحد ثم قال: فَطَفِقَ مَسْطا بِالسُّوقِ وَالْأَعْنَاقِ [ ص: ۳۳] أي فجعل يمسح سوقها وأعناقها مسحا.

وقال الأكثرون : المعنى أنه كان يمسح السيف بسوقها وأعناقها ـ أي يقطعها ـ وهذا أيضًا بعيد لأنه لو كان المسح بالسوق والأعناق هو القطع لكان القائل إذا قال: مسحت رأس فلان فُهِم منه أنه قطعه ولكان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة : ٦] يفيد القطع، بل قيل : مسح الرأس بالسيف فربما منه ضرب العنق، فأما إذا لم يذكر السيف لم يفهم منه القطع البتة.

فأما إذا لم يذكر السيف لم يفهم منه القطع البتة. كما إذا عرفت تفسير الآية فنقول : إن رباط الخيل كان مندوبا إليه في دينهم، في ديننا، فجلس سليمان لتعرض عليه الخيل، ثم بين ان ذلك ليس بسبب حب الدنيا، بل لأن الله تعالى أمره بذلك ثم أمر بإعدادها حتى توارت بالحجاب ـ أي حتى غابت عن بصره - ثم أمر بردها، فلما وصلت إليه أخذ يمسح سوقها وأعناقها إما لأجل تشريفها وإظهار شدة رحمته عليها ، أو إظهارًا لكونها من أعظم الأعوان في دفع العدو ؛ أو لأنه أراد أن يظهر من نفسه أنه في ضبط مصالح دينه ودنياه، بحيث يتكفلها بنفس، ولا يفوضها إلى أحد أو لأنه كان أعلم بأحوال الخيل من غيره، فكان يمتحنها ويمسحها ليعلم حالها في الصحة والسقم وهذا الذي ذكرناه كلام ينطبق عليه لفظ القرآن، وفي تعظيم أحوال الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - فيكون أولى مما يكون بالضد من ذلك.
The thirty-second issue: He seeks from his Lord the greatest miracles when he commits the greatest sins, which is abandoning prayer - with such an expression that is not used nor mentioned except with slaves and the lowest servants? So we know that this probability - which is saying that these two pronouns refer to the sun - is false. And the third probability: that the first pronoun refers to the sun, and the second to the horses, which is the saying of the majority, as if it was said: 'until the sun disappeared behind the veil,' they returned the horses, and this is far-fetched because they are two pronouns occurring in one context, so differentiating between them is contrary to the principle. The fourth probability: that the first pronoun refers to the horses and the second to the sun, and no one has said this. Then He said: 'Then he began to pass (his hand) over the legs and necks' [Sad:33], i.e. he began to wipe their legs and necks with a wipe. And the majority said: The meaning is that he was wiping the sword over their legs and necks - i.e. cutting them - and this is also far-fetched, because if wiping over the legs and necks meant cutting, then if someone said: 'I wiped the head of so-and-so' it would be understood that he cut it, and the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And wipe your heads and your feet' [Al-Ma'idah:6] would imply cutting. Rather, it is said: wiping the head with a sword possibly results in striking the neck. But if the sword is not mentioned, cutting is absolutely not understood. So if you have understood the interpretation of the verse, we say: The keeping of horses was recommended in their religion and in our religion, so Sulayman sat for the horses to be displayed to him, then he explained that this was not due to love of the world, but because Allah commanded him to do so. And this which we have mentioned is speech that matches the wording of the Quran, and in glorifying the states of the prophets - prayers and peace of Allah be upon them - it is more appropriate than the opposite.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed interpretation and defense of the account of Prophet Sulayman and the horses from Surah Sad. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sulayman was cruel to the horses by cutting them.
Correction: Razi shows that the word 'مسح' does not mean cutting without an instrument, and the context indicates honor and mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what would be the problem if 'مسح' meant cutting?
A) It would contradict the story.
B) It would make Sulayman a sinner.
C) It would imply cutting the head in the ablution verse.
D) It is linguistically impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's linguistic analysis shows that Sulayman's wiping the horses was an act of mercy and honor, not punishment, thereby preserving the prophet's impeccable character.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about a Prophet's action that seems questionable, do you immediately assume the worst or do you pause to examine the linguistic and contextual evidence? Which Prophet have you judged hastily, and what will you do to correct that judgment today?
?
Razi passionately defends Sulayman's honor by seeking the best interpretation. Is there a believer you have maligned without evidence? Will you commit to giving others the benefit of the doubt (husn al-zann) even when the evidence is ambiguous?
?
The lesson shows that words have precise meanings rooted in language, not arbitrary assumptions. In your own understanding of Allah's Book or the Prophet's Sunnah, where have you imposed your own meaning instead of submitting to the intended one? What would it cost you to surrender that control?
